The Red Page
The Job
In Russia.
Stanley Washburn, the London Times
correspondent, With the''Russian armies, was
by his frie|nds not to publish liis
second 'volume of ; Field Fates from the
Russian Front,, on the ground that during
the period April-August, 1915, Russia,
almost destitute of munitions, was being
practically pushed off the map by the im-
perturbable advance of the Hun behind his
phalanx of big guns. But precisely because
Washburn still believed in the ultimate vic-
tory of the Russians, he published The Rus-
sian Campaign (Melrose, _ per Dymock's
Library). The volume begins with the fall
of ITzemysl and ends with the fall of War-
saw.
Washburn has unfortunately become accus-
tomed to the war. He has been so long in
the thick of it that, automatically, his mind
has ceased to register those vivid impressions
that gave, for instance, to Pares’ remark-
able book its marvellous realism. Wash-
burn is now merely the official war-corres-
pondent, systematically touring in his motor
the whole vast length of the Russian front.
The story of the heroic defence of the
Russians against the first of the Huns’ gas
attack is admirably told. The soldiers re-
ceived orders not to fire, but to remain in
their trenches —remain and die, if necessary
—in order to make the Huns believe that
they had been overwhelmed by the gas.
Many of them were; but the survivors took
a terrible revenge; for as the triumphant
Huns advanced to the uncannily-silent
trenches they were met with a counter-
charge that routed them. “In this trying
ordeal not a man, soldier or officer budged
from his position. To a man they remained
firm, some overcome, some dying and others
already dead.... The net results were an
absolute defeat of the Germans, with a loss
of several of their own positions, and a loss
in dead and wounded probably three times
greater than was suffered by the Russians.”
So powerful were the chlorine fumes that it
was found that many of the hospital helpers
were overcome by the fumes in the men’s
clothing. So the first thing done when the
patients reached the hospitals was to strip
off their clothes and give them a hot bath.
Even after these cases were brought to War-
saw the gas still given out. from their lungs
as they exhaled so poisoned the air that sonie
of the women nurses were affected with
severe headaches and with nausea. “The
incredible part is that out of the thousands
affected hardly 1000 died in the trenches, and
of the 1500 brought to Warsaw only 2 per-
cent. have died to date. It is probably true
that the Russian moujik soldier is the
hardiest individual in Europe; add to this
the consideration that for ten months none
of them have been touching a1c0h01.... The
victims that die quickly are spared the worst
effects, but those that linger on and finally
succumb suffer a torture which the days of
the Inquisition can hardly parallel. . . .These
men die not only of strangulation, which in
itself is a slow torture, but in their last
moments their internal organs are slowly
being eaten away by the acids which they
have taken into their stomachs. ... In this
single night’s work the Germans have built
up for themselves in the heart of every Rus-
sian moujik a personal hatred and detesta-
tion that has spread like wildfire in all parts
of the army, and has made the Russian
troops infinitely fiercer both in attack and
defence than at any other period of the
war.”
&
In Russia the effects of the gas were
noticeable 25 miles from the line, and indi-
viduals were overcome as far away as 10
miles from the positions. If the Huns utilise
poison gas on a wholesale scale, the effects
upon the peasant and his stock will be
severe. Every small body of water will
gradually be transformed into a diluted solu-
tion of hydrochloric acid. Stock and the in-
habitants of large areas of Poland will be
slowly poisoned by chloral.
Washburn is not depressed by the long
retirement of the Russian line, for he is with
the Russians. “Given four months of rest
and recuperation we shall have an entirely
new situation in the beginning of next year
(that is, now), which will present an
entirely neev problem. It will really mean
the starting of a new war with new objec-
tives and practically with a new and re-
equipped army. . . .There are many who
maintain that Russia will find it difficult to
reconquer Galicia and Poland. Probably she
will never have to do so. It is perfectly
possible that when the end comes, Germany
will still be on the territory of France, Bel-
gium and Russia. Peace will bring back in-
stantly all of these provinces without any
fighting at all. It matters not, then, whether
Germany is broken while still in the heart, of
Russia or under the walls of Berlin itself.
The task is to break the enemy, and that this
will be done eventually I think cannot be
doubted. It is the stamina, the character
and the resources of the Allies that in the
end will decide this war, and nothing is more
unwise than to judge the situation from the
study of pins moved back and forward on
the map of Europe.”
The numerous photographs add much to
the value of the book.
In South-West Africa.
With Botha in the Field, by Moore Ritchie
(Longmans, Green, per George Robertson,
Sydney), makes no pretence to being an ex-
haustive study of the campaigns against the
rebels in South Africa and against German
South-West Africa; but it is the first
account of the doings in the Union that
has reached book-form, and though, to Aus-
tralian readers, the narrative is not always
cleai', Moore Ritchie does his best by dia-
grams and maps to help us. The many
photographs, mostly taken by the author, are
interesting. Swakopmund, he says, is the
most extraordinary place he has seen.
“There is not a spot of vegetation. There is
not a genuine tree to be seen. The water has a
brackish taste.... If ever there was a work
of bluff, Swakopmund. the German town at
the mouth of the Swakop River, is that
thing. One fancies the German commer-
cial expert, looking along this ferocious and
awful spot to found a town that should
appear on the maps and be esteemed a sea-
port. The Swakop River? Very well.
Was there water there? But certainly so;
water obviously of the worst quality —yet
water. Besides, were there not always
refrigerators and condensing machinery?
Upon which Swakopmund was forced into
existence —planked down there bit by bit in
the - face of circumstance. Walk a trifle
over 1000 yards from the edge of the Atlan-
tic through Swakopmund’s deep sandy
streets and you get the key to the town. For
it ceases utterly, abruptly; from the door
of its last villa, fitted with perfect furnish-
ings from Hamburg, the bitter desolation
that is the Namib Desert stretches away
from your very feet. . . .It is a monument to
Teutonic determination. The Germans
willed this town there; fitted it out, from
bioscope theatre to church with organ and
electric organola; and they lived in it, with
the climate of perdition and all the acces-
sories of a suburb of Berlin, and called it a
seaport. It is not a seaport; in a fair gale
you can’t land a barrel of corks at the pier.
But given time and they would have built
a £2.000,000 breakwater... .Nevertheless, it
is not colonising.”
It was from this little hell that Botha
made his wonderful trek across the desert to
Windhuk. “When Botha treks he treks at
express speed.’* He had to capture and hold
every waterhole on the way. The author
speaks of this trek as a feat “that broke all
known marching records and caused two
hemispheres to talk. Striking right across
the desert, Botha’s army marched night and
day, and in five of those days covered over
40 miles per day.”
And the news of the sinking of the Lusi-
tania reached Windhuk just after its occu-
pation. “Botha’s forces had crossed a
desert through which it was the open boast
of the enemy that it was strewn with mines
and every well poisoned. Was a single de-
fenceless citizen of Windhuk the worse for
it after the occupation? Not one. . . .It was
magnificent. But I wonder if the chival-
rous Teuton would call it war!”
One of the great objectives of the cam-
paign was to secure the Windhuk wireless
station. “When you catch a glimpse of it
suddenly where it stands on the veld outside
the town, you get a thrill of £heer astonish-
ment. The thing seems monstrous here. It
is foreign to our ideas, a wireless colossus
in such a place. .. .Fancy a pretty bijou
town, red roofs, neat church, pepper trees,
sleepy people—and across the veldt, a mile
and a half away, darkening the sky with
great Vertical lines, five terrific steel lattice
pillars, nearly 400 feet high, tied by cables
with stay bolts as big as a man.... There
is no electric light in this village of Wind-
huk, or Windy Corner, yet. And there are
not enough Germans in the whole territory
to populate a good-sized town. There is also
the usual telegraphic communication to the
coast. Yet —the wireless.”
The narrative lays stress upon Botha’s
brilliant strategy. He surprised Windhuk
by his suddenness of approach and the care-
ful co-ordination of his enveloping move-
ments ; and in the last phase, where he
rounded up the remnants of the enemy,
Franke, their commander, was actually sur-
rounded before he had an inkling of that
unpleasant fact. He had no alternative but
to surrender. “Even guerrilla warfare is an
unattainable luxury when you are sur-
rounded.”
In Fkance.
Boyd Cable, whose name is familiar to
Bulletin readers, enlisted in England, and
contributed from the Front in France a
series of brilliant articles to the Corniiill
Magazine. These sketches, composed for
the most part within shell and rifle range,
are now published in book form. Between
the Lines (Smith, Elder, per Dymock’s
Library) ranks as one of the few worth-
while books that the war has produced. Boyd
Cable, like lan Hay, tells us exactly what
modern war means, though his method is
more scientific. He takes certain war-worn
phrases from official despatches, such as:
A section of advanced trench changed hands
several times, finally remaining in our hands
....to the right a violent artillery bombard-
ment has been in progress... .a mine was
successfully exploded under a section of the
enemy's trench, etc.; and he fills in the
graphic meaning of these bald and uncon-
vincing narratives.
Here, for instance, is a picture of artillery
support: “The advancing Germans reached
at last the strip of ground where the shrap-
nel hailed and lashed, reached the strip and
pushed into it—but not past it. Up to the
shrapnel zone the advance could press—
through, it could not. Under the shrapnel
nothing could live. It swept the ground in
driving gust on gust, swept and besomed it
bare of life. Here and there, in ones and
twos and little knots and groups, the Ger-
mans strove desperately to push on. They
came as far as that deadly fire-belt; and in
ones and twos and little knots and groups
they stayed there and died. Supports
hurried up and hurled themselves in. and a
spasm of fresh strength and fury lifted the
line and heaved it forward. So far the hre
of its fury brought it ; and there the hosing
shrapnel met it, swept down and washed it
away, and beat it out to the last spark and
the last man.” *
J*
And here is a snapshot of a British ad-
vance: “Scores, hundreds of men fell before
they could clear the entanglements to form
up in the open. . . .But the mass poured out,
shook itself roughly into line, and began
to run across the open. They ran toi the
most part with shoulders hunched and heads
stooped, as men would run through a hoa\
rainstorm to a near shelter. . . .As the line
swept on doggedly it thinned and shredded
into broken groups,... here and there, where
it ran into the effective sweep of a maxim,
the line simply withered and stayed still in
a string of huddled heaps amongst and on
which the bullets continued to drum and
thud Fifty yards across the battalion
assaulting was no longer a line, but a scat-
tered series of groups like beads on a broken
string; 60 yards across, and the groups had
dwindled to single men and couples, with
desperately long intervals between; ioyards,
and there were no more than odd occasional
men, with one little bunch near the centre
THE CHILDREN OF THE LUSITANIA.
“We do not want any love among the Americans,
but we do want respect. and the case of the Lusi-
tania will win it for us better than a hundred
victories on land.”
Berlin LOHALANZEIGER. May 9, 1915.
Cartoon by Louis Raemaekers.
that had by some extraordinary chance
escaped the sleet of bullets; at 80 yards a
sudden swirl of lead caught this last group
—and the line at lasf was gone, wiped out,
the open was swept clear of those dogged
runners.... Another battalion had com-
menced to climb out of the trench before
the advancing line w y as more than half-way
across. This time the casualties, although
appallingly heavy, were not so hopelessly
severe, probably because a salient of the
enemy trench to a flank had been reached by
a battalion further along, and the devastat-
ing fire of rifles and machine-guns cut off. . .
The following battalion came surging
across, losing heavily, but still bearing
weight enough to teli when at last they
poured in over the parapet. . . .The neutral
ground was w0n.... The fighting fell to a
new phase—the work of the short-arm bayo-
net and the bomb-throwers.”. . . .
And then “the weak point that so often
hampers attack” began to make itself felt.
“The bombers and ‘grenadiers’ had ex-
hausted the stock they carried: fresh sup-
plies were scanty; were brought up with
difficulty, and distributed to the most
urgently required places with still greater
difficulty. The ammunition carriers had to
cross the open of the old neutral ground, the
battered first trench, pass along communica-
tion trenches choked with dead and wounded,
or again cross the open to the second and
third line.... They had to pass through a
zone or ‘barrage’ of shrapnel built across
their path for just this special purpose of
destroying supports and supplies.”
With the same graphic touch Boyd Cable
pictures a score of other interesting opera-
tions —the making and exploding of a mine ;
the hell of being shelled all day in the
trenches; the exact method by which “artil-
lery support” is managed; the first experi-
ence of a new regiment in the front trenches ;
and the amazement of those new soldiers
when after a day that seemed to them to be
crammed with exciting fighting they learned
in the official report that there was “nothing
to report” ; the “cold-bloodedly unpleasant
work” of sappers digging a communication
trench under fire —just digging and dying
without any of the satisfaction or the glory
of a charge; the job of the ammunition
column ; and a signaller’s crowded day. It is
the Tommy’s sense of humor that saves him
from the horror of this confused and station-
ary warfare; and Boyd Cable continually
stresses this point. There is his story of the
delight of a Cockney regiment when it heard
for the first time from the German trench
the celebrated Hymn of Hate.
“The word flew down the British trench :
‘lt’s the ’ymn! They’re singin’ the ‘Ymn of
’Ate,’ and every man sat drinking the air in
eagerly. This was luck, pure gorgeous luck.
... .They had read it in the papers, and had
had it declaimed with a fine frenzy by Pri-
vate ’Enry Irving. Hadn’t they, like plenty
other regiments, longed to hear the tune, but
longed in vain? And here it was being sung
to them in full chorus by the Germans them-
selves. Oh, that was luck.
“The mouth-organist was sitting with his
mouth open and liis head turned to listen, as
if afraid to miss a single note.
“ ‘ ’Ave you got it, Snapper?’ whispered
Private Robinson anxiously at the end.
‘Will you be able to remember it?’ ”
He hadn’t got it—quite. “We might kid
em to sing it again, said Robinson hope-

The bitterest and most scathing indictment
of the Hun comes, significantly, from a
neutral artist. Louis Raemaekers, the Dutch
artist, has crucified the Hun on a lastine
cross of ignominy; and his war cartoons
are an answer to the
“Hymn of Hate” that
will long survive that
childish scream of
rage. I bidder and
Stoughton publish, at
the extremely modest
price of 2d.. 40 of
Raemaekers’ Cartoons,
by permission of Land
and Water, in which
these cartoons first
appeared. That maga-
zine also offers artist’s
proofs, in the original
size, at a guinea each,
and unsigned proofs'at
half a guinea. The
irony of these pictures
is appalling, and
their pathos is, at
times, almost too
awful. One of them
lias already gained world-wide fame—that
showing the evilly smiling Kaiser, on receiv-
ing the news of the execution of Nurse
Cavell, remarking : “Now you can bring me
the American protest.”
Anzac—and a Guinea
Well, it is all over—that heroic attempt to
storm the impossible. But there remains the
need of recording in poetry that glorious for-
lorn hope. And this page invites its corres-
pondents to write a sonnet that will sum up
all that Anzac means, and will mean, to Aus-
tralia and to the world. In 14 lines you
must put the soul of Anzac; and those 14
lines must be in the form of the sonnet that
for the octette utilises only two rimes, in this
order, a,b,b,a, a,b,b,a; and for the sextette
may use two or .three rimes, but the last two
lines must not rime. There are other rules
for the sonnet, but the sonneteers of Aus-
tralasia have already proved that they know
them, and those who do not can learn only
by their mistakes. Sonnets, addressed to
this page, must reach it by April 15. The
writer of the best sonnet will receive a
guinea.
o
David Low, whose volume of Caricatures
has had an excellent sale, has one interesting
hobby ; and in order to extend his valuable
collection he is this month holding an exhibi-
tion of his caricatures and cartoons at Hor-
derns’ Gallery, Sydney. His hobby —nearly
forgetting this!—is the collection of auto-
graphs on the lower right-hand corner of
cheques.
Potted Australian Sketches, the first
number of an illustrated threepenny maga-
zine, chiefly cartoons and illustrated jokes, is
a young and tender thing. Three artists,
William Farrow, Rick Elmes and Stanley
Osborn, furnish the drawings, which show no
trace of even budding genius in the posses-
sion of any of the three. Still the magazine
is khaki in outlook and topical, and it pro-
bably deserves the casual 3d. Anyhow, the
three artists must have got a lot of fun out
of the production.

Lift the outer walla of the nostrils as shown above. Then Inhale vigorously through the

HAVE YOU GOT
CATARRH ?
If you have a dropping of phlegm into the throat.
If your nose is stopped up,
If crusts form in your nose,
If you are easily susceptible to colds,
If you suffer from frontal* headaches,
If you have noises in the head,
If your hearing is affected,
If you have to clear your throat and expectorate often,
if a change in the weather affects you,
If you are a victim to shortness of breath,
If you sneeze frequently,
If your sense of smell is affected,
If you have a heavy, tired feeling on awaking,
If you suffer from a husky throat,
If you are affected with any of the above symptoms,
the chances are that you are suffering from
Catarrh. It is an exceedingly dangerous com-
plaint, and is so insidious that the sufferer hardly
realises how serious is his or her condition until
matters have reached an almost chronic stage.
Catarrh often begins with a cold in the head, but it never stops there.
The simple cold becomes a protracted one, while the discharge from the
nose grows more profuse and uncomfortable.
The inflammation extends to the throat and bronchial tubes, causing
hoarseness, a tickling sensation, and an aggravating cough. The matter
that is continually dropping back into the throat finds its way into the
stomach, resulting in a distressing form of dyspepsia, nausea, and loss of
appetite and strength.
The catarrhal poisons are absorbed into the blood, and all the membranes
of the body become infected. What was supposed to be a purely local
disease has become constitutional, deep-seated, and chronic, and may
eventually result in Deafness, Bronchitis, Asthma, Neuritis, Catarrh of
the Stomach, or that dread scourge of the human race, Consumption.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sentinel of God.
When we had but a mile to go
The lights of home shone clear ;
Below the sunset’s afterglow
They flashed a signal dear,
And save for one lone cricket’s song
The dreaming world was still.
A grey cloud rampart stretched along
The skies above the hill.
And as we watched and said no word
We saw a star appear,
And a low roll of thunder heard
Beyond the shining spear;
Along the rampart in the skies
He walked with thunder shod —•
Thus we beheld with our own eyes
The Sentinel of God.
N.S.W.
KURANUI.
Friendship.
Friendship watched for you, setting wide her
door:
Gladly your feet had sped that way before.
And yet to-night no shadow blurred the
floor —-
Only the pattern of a leaf, wind-blown.
Friendship watched for you in the dusk
alone.
The hunting moon came riding o’er the hill ;
The lesser stars sought coyer from her will
In forest pools, moss mystified, and still.
Friendship sat rigid in her grey alcove....
And never saw your pleading fingers move
About the window-latch of tardy love!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The Singing Roads.
No man can point the singing roads in any
land or town,
Yet, winding to the Vale of Song, the sing-
ing roads go down ; '
And men there are who take the road and
hearken to the strain
Which calls to every singing heart and every
gipsy brain. . , , , ,
Yet never hand can link with hand, and
never heart can teach,
The secrets of the singing roads which come
to each and each;
For every man who heeds the call must ven-
ture forth and find
The open road, the lonely road, which loiters
through the mind.
Dear comrades of the singing roads a-
dreaming down the days,
I stretch a hand which holds my heart across
the lonely ways;
Until I seem to know the songs which move
upon your lips
And feel the pulsing of your wrists upon
my finger tips!
O patient eyes that gaze and gaze, and
eager ears that strain
To catch the magic of the wind, the music
of the rain,
To find a tender symphony a trembling hand
may pen
Within a score of burning lines to melt the
minds of men !
To melt the minds of eager men who hurry
to and fro
On other roads than singing roads, and who
can never know
The bitter aching of the soul from whence
the song is wrung,
Or catch the echoing of Life in ev’ry song
that’s sung.
For unto every little song is set the living
beat, , ,
The beat of heart, the beat of pulse, the
beat of marching feet, •
And many songs ring out and out which
hold a throbbing note —
The anguish of an empty life, the sob within
the throat.
He best can sing the song of Love for whom
Love holds no part;
It is an honest song which swells from out
a yearning heart;
And he can hymn the breadth of Life to
whom Life is a name,
And panting fans a waning spark within a
wasting frame.
O baby tones and rosy heels which rest
within the palm !
He, childless, sires his babe of dreams and
shrines it in a psalm, %
And from the depth of Heart’s desire the
cry rings true and strong.
For each man takes his darling Hope and
weaves it into song.
The dreams we love, the hopes we hold,
the tone of wind and wave
Are culled upon the lonely ways and pris-
oned in a stave.
From odors of the wooded paths, the colors
of the street,
We weave the garlands that we toss about
the City’s feet.
And ever ’mid a world of words the perfect
word is sought,
That thoughtless men may learn to love the
thinker for the thought,
That men might pause awhile to hear the
music ringing strong,
And feel a tenderness towards the singer
for the song.
Adown the roads, the lonely roads, the sing-
ing men have sped—
The singers of the sweeter songs are always
right ahead. .
We catch the music of the voice, the echo
of the rhyme,
And mark the pressure of their feet upon the
dust of Time.
Yet still we quest the singing ways and
still the heart will yearn—-
Perchance the perfect song of songs is just
around the turn.
Dear comrades of the singing roads, stretch
hands to me and take
This song, an earnest of the songs the singer
longs to make.
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWSEN.

fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3OUO voids, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
ivill be regarded as strictly confidential.

To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
Suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 9, 1916.
SELLING AUSTRALIA.
Philip Snowdon, M.P., talking in Lan-
cashire (Eng.), was asked by a woman
in his audience why England couldn’t get
rid once and for all of her burden
of debt by selling a dependency or two.
Snowdon said he had suggested to an Aus-
tralian that the war trouble might be fixed
up by tossing the Commonwealth to Ger-
many ; but the man from the South countered
the proposal by recommending that Austra-
lia should be left alone and England handed
over. Then the Lancashire audience gave
a loud smile and went home side-stepping
the Zeppelins.
The notion of presenting Australia, like
John the Baptist’s head, to somebody who
asked very loudly for it. is not exactly new ;
but the nation that made the purchase or
got the gift would be likely to find that it
had a vaudeville show of some vigor on its
hands. In fact, Siiakspeare’s person, who
sold the lion’s skin while the animal was
still in full possession of its whiskers and
fingernails, might be regarded as having a
first-class ticket as compared with the man
who attempted to put Australia in his over-
coat pocket. It is not that the defeat of
our troops and invasion of this continent
might be such an overwhelming task to a
nation with plenty of navy and a lot of
spare armies, but that the conquest of the
Australian and the real subjugation of Bill-
jim would be such a tangled, elusive and
altogether unsatisfactory affair that the gen-
erals would die of broken hearts chasing
armed nomads beyond Alice Springs, while
the civic governors would perish of internal
cramp trying to contort themselves into such
shapes as might enable them to see the
length and breadth of the problems of State-
craft. When the present complications in
the general management of Australia are
coiuLlercd, and the general ineffectiveness of
things is reviewed, and when we remember
that everything is now supposed to be done
with a high and worthy aim by persons who
have the sympathy and help of the public,
we may get some sort of an idea of what
government would be like when that same
public sowed the official motor-track with
tin tacks and stuck darning-needles in the
seats of the mighty. To sell Australia
would be to sell Australia’s people, and the
Australian objects to being sold by anybody
except his own selected and elected and prop-
erly-pledged auctioneer. Of course, a
nation to whom we had been handed over as
a guarantee that the olive branch would wag
its tail like a tame poodle for some genera-
tions to come might endeavor to swamp us
with immigrants of its own type and ideals;
but just there we should get in some of our
finest work. The Hans or Carl who took
off his spectacles and attempted to dig or
plough among us would find the intricacies
of the game exciting. It would soon be
discovered that agriculture could only pro-
ceed behind barbed-wire entanglements and
on the basis of a machine-gun to each cab-
bage, that a dead dingo was always in the
waterhole, and that it required an armored
train to round up the cows at milking time.
Australia may be weak in the matter of con-
structive progress; but Australia would
take to the job of destructive irritation as
chorus girls take to iced asparagus. The
sale of Australia would be the signal for a
great universal indulgence of all the sporting
instincts of the race. Everybody would go
bushranging with zest, and the Ned Kelly
of the new order would be the cheeriest
thing in brigands the world has known. One
good outlaw in the past could generally keep
30 smart policemen from putting on w T eight;
and Australia with a million bushrangers
who felt a patriotic enthusiasm over their job
might reasonably expect to keep 30 million
not-particularly-bright troopers going at full
sw T ear for an unlimited time.
What is true of Australia is true in some
degree of any nation. You can’t sell peoples
or give them away without their consent;
and, in fact, you can’t conquer them except
by extermination. The racial boundaries of
a State are fixed; and it is a question
whether a so-called conquest is ever a profit-
able investment for the conqueror. Wil-
liam I. seemed to conquer England with
his Normans; but in two centuries it was
quite clear that the Englishman had come
out winner in the long struggle. Ireland
conquered all her invaders by turning them
into Irishmen; and to-day that place
of verdant distress has for a political
leader a direct descendant .of Raymond
the Fat —the most barbarous Norman horror
that ever led a bunch of steel-clad cut-
throats. France conquered everything that
ever walked over her, including her own revo-
lutionaries. Possibly these things are
so because the people are what they
eat, and the same country will always
produce the same sort of blood and
bone and brain; but, in any case,
the buying and selling or giving and stealing
of territories is entirely played out. Eng-
land, even at the last gasp, and waiting for
the knock-out punch, would never dream
that she could sell Australia. Indeed, the
present war, whatever its immediate causes
and justification, is largely a struggle to
establish once and for all the big principle
that European soil with the people on it
can’t be handed round at the will of kings
and statesmen. But, while this is plain
enough, the soil that has no people on it
might be sold or taken; and Australia has
a lot of the raw material of agriculture lying
about without a boot-print to 50 square
miles. Big guns might destroy Sydney or
Melbourne without the slightest hope of ever
taming Billjim to the will of a conqueror;
but if the big guns were applied to the wide,
unsettled North a very small expenditure of
high explosives would tear away from the
Commonwealth the best heritage of the Aus-
tralian kid. Sooner or later we shall have
to sell the Northern Territory to pay off
our big debt to the world. It is up to us to
see to it that the sale is made to the settler
wh > is, or who is ready to become, an Aus-
tralian. If we don’t sell it to him
quickly we may yet find that the
highest bidder at the next auction is some
obscene person who makes a ribald jest of
our best ideals. The Australian can’t be
chaffered for by huxters in the European
markets; but he may be sold by his own
alleged statesmen who won’t complete a
promised line of railway even when the
hopes of a continent hang upon its unfinished
steel link.
THE STINKING FISH THAT IS
HAWKED TO THE DETRIMENT
OF THE DESERT AIR.
An agitated disciple of wood-and-water
joeyism rushed a letter through the post
office the other day to inform this paper that
Lanolin is not taken out of wool in Australia
because labor does not give full value for wages
received. That as soon as labor gives value indus-
tries will be started, and meanwhile capital can, as
a rule, be taken to where it is welcomed. To pro-
duce lanolin brains and machinery are required, and
we would almost certainly have to go out of Aus-
tralia to get them.
All of which is a very good sample of the
ignoble doctrine that the Australian worker
is a constitutional loafer, unskilled and
brainless, who reaches the highest pinnacle
of the industrial efficiency he is capable of
when shaving and butchering jumbucks,
heaving great masses of dirt and grease and
wool and meat into ships and driving capital
oif the landscape. Of course it is all the
wildest trash, and if made the basis of public
policy could lead Australia nowhere but the
scrapheap. It represents the Gospel of
Despair, which of all gospels is the most
fatal of acceptance. Yet it is sedulously
preached in Australia, and because of
demented vaporings from the top of soap
boxes, of the spineless wrigglings and the
perjuries of party politicians, the ineptitude
of Parliaments and the underground
engineering of the foreign-trade brigade it
gets a certain measure of credence from
people of the kind that are always ready to
believe anything so long as it sounds horrible
enough. Wherefore The Bulletin calls
Knibbs, the Federal Statistician, as first
witness for the defence against that part
of the indictment charging the Australian
workman with not giving a fair return for
his weekly wad.
* * * *
In the Commonwealth Year Book
Knibbs has tabulated, over a series of years,
the results of statistical investigation into
the output and cost of production in
Australian factories. Taking the whole
Commonwealth, the marrow of the proceed-
ings therein relevant to this allegation of
loafing is easily reached. The bald facts
arc these: —
1908. 1913.
Wages paid . . . . £19,884,367 £33,606,087
Light and fuel .. 2,176,876 3,240,901
Raw material used. . 60,957,626 96,407,477
Cost .. ..£83,018,869 £133,254,465
And the difference between this cost and the
value of the product represents the margin
left for profit and interest, being the added
value created in process of manufacture, and
it comes out this way:—
1908. 1913.
Value of product ..£99,529,631 £161,560,763
Cost 83,018,869 133,254,465
£16,510,762 £28,306,298
Which discloses that every pound expended
in factories on wages, fuel, light and raw
material yielded in 1908 something worth
£1 3s. llfd., and in 1913 an article worth
£J 4s. 2fd.; also that the added value given
to raw material in the process of manufac-
ture was in the first year £38,572,005, which
meant an addition of £l5O per employee
engaged, and in 1913 the same added value
was £65,153,286, or £193 per employee. The
total value of the output of factories was
£387 per employee in 1908, and £479 in
1913. There is no indication in this that
the Australian is loafing in the industrial
field, that his output is decreasing or that
capital is getting a bad deal. The evidence
points in a quite different direction, a
conclusion which receives corroboration
from Knibbs, who casually mentions the
value of land and buildings and of plant
and machinery devoted to Australian
industry, the comparison for the respective
years being
1908. 1913.
Land and buildings. . £26,657,991 £36,872,720
Plant and machinery 25,927,763 37,256,958
And if all that and the rest does not mean
that the people who are snivelling about not
getting a fair return for wages in industrial
enterprise are as a general thing lying and
otherwise imperilling their souls then
figures have no meaning, and all evidence is
a farce and the truth is concealed in any
wild statement that may be blowing round
the streets.
* * * *
It is untrue that industries are languishing
or that men are deterred from embarking
upon new industries because the Australian
workman does not give value for the wages
he gets. What is true is that taken in the
mass the Australian workman has been giv-
ing an increasing return ; that the advances
he has received in wages have been recouped
to his employer and swallowed up by rent-
mongers ; that there are hundreds of men and
hundreds of thousands of pounds ready to
embark upon new industries to-morrow if the
importing mania, encouraged by the politi-
cians, were rooted out by removal of the
conditions on which it thrives; and that
industries which are hanging on by the skin
of their teeth would get a firm grip on the
national trade if the tariff were made what
it ought to be. Australian workmen cannot
compete and should not be asked to com-
pete against the dumpings of highly indus-
trialised countries with a large home market
and a surplus of output to get rid of, nor
to struggle with the competition of workmen
who are accustomed to dining from the smell
of an oil-rag and breakfasting on memory of
the aroma. Those countries and people
have their own standards, and with these
Australia has nothing to do. The rela-
tive cheapness of labor in Australia and
Timbuctoo is not the test Australia can
afford to make the measure of her industrial
life, nor the test of whether it will pay to
squeeze the grease out of a sheep’s overcoat
or to convert iron ore into steel. The crux
of the matter is whether it is better for Aus-
tralia to pay Australian workmen to do these
jobs or send the cash or its equivalent away
to another country for the purpose of getting
other, workmen to do it, thus giving oppor-
tunities for the profitable employment of
capital and labor there instead of here. Most
Australians think it is better —that the
nation can never be anything until this
policy is in full blast. Wood-and-water
joeyism teaches that the national' destiny is
to haul raw materials to the seaboard, and
with it feed the industry of other lands.
When in 1913 Australian workmen added
t° *"h e value of material worth
£96,407,4 < it looked to people who can see
beyond the conclusion of their noses and have
some regard for the industrial and economic
history of other countries that 65 millions
had been created in’ and saved to Australia.
To the wood-and-water propagandist the inci-
dent is merely a revelation of the insanity
which refuses to recognise the beautiful
effects which might have been produced by
shovelling all that raw material into Ham-
burg, and letting the stranger do the rest.
w * * *
Nationally Australia is loafing and has
been doing it for many years—loafing
on loan money and the p.n. and the political
job instead of facing the business of earning
her own living by living on what she earns—
of creating wealth for Australians as an
alternative to pouring interest into the
pocket of Cohen. And the outward signs of
this loaf are details of imports, a ruinous
accumulation of parasitic citizens, the degra-
dation of Parliaments and the demoralisation
of political parties to a stage at which the dis-
tribution of doles and jobs and the rolling of
logs has taken the place of honest endeavor.
If Australia was living as she should
be doing to-day Australian factories would
be intercepting and utilising that lanolin
the foreigner is getting, and would be
hunting round to capture quite a lot of other
ingredients, because, like every other country,
her labor and enterprise would be her chief
source of well-being and strength.
THE PARSON AND THE WET
CANTEEN.
I he sudden howl from the army ehanlr
at Liverpool camp that the wet *
would be seven devilpower worse than!?
present thirst which drives the
Billjim to the local pub is a howl !?
nothing more. It is unsupported bv °
sort of argument and unbacked bv
pint of evidence. On the plain , acts *
soldier in the camp, who was accuse ,
have his glass of beer while engagedini,?
nary citizen work, finds that at the dl '
when he has s iven up e,er“t h Sn| T?
national cause and is prepare to
hard training as a preliminary to 2°
and possible death he is treated 'like a srh ?
boy and denied ordinary rights to which?'
soldiery of all lands have been aeon!!, th j
Instead of having his glass of
the surroundings of his daily toil he S w’a
to go and look for it beyond the camp f"
compulsion is a grievance and produces !
recklessness which leads to all kinds 1
trouble; and the dry canteen stands' con
demned by the local experience over a net;,a
of 18 months. But when there is l L P £,,
to revert to the old, sane system, which ha
worked well in other military centres in!
parsons of the camp step forwart’ 1
assert that everything would be enveloneH
in seas of alcohol if the military authorities
themselves took over the business of attend
ing to the soldiers’ thirst. And a certain
number of people believe the statemen
merely because it is said in a very load
voice. “
* * * *
To declare that the wet canteen can’t he
run cleanly and without excessive boozing is
to throw a large-sized piece of dirt at the
military authorities and the officers in
charge. It means, in effect, that the parsons
concerned don’t believe the men at the head
of things to be capable of running a canteen
as well as the men in charge of similar camps
the world over. It means that in the view
of these chaplains the officers who are
endeavoring to turn the raw Billjim
material into good soldier stuff will sit down
and let the men guzzle without restraint
when the control of the liquor supply is
entirely in their hands and can be turned
on or shut off at will. Such an opinion
isn’t particularly complimentary to the offi-
cers, and is suggestive of a good deal that
the signatories to the remarkable epistle
appearing in the Sydney daily press would
hardly dare to put in words. If the military
men in authority can’t be trusted to run a
wet canteen decently, _ to. see that it is not a
means of excessive drinking and the cause of
riot and lack of discipline, is it reasonably to
be expected that they can turn out
efficient soldiers or keep any sort of
oversight of the troops in their charge?
That an officer should have little enough
hold on men who have been driven
to look for their daily beer over a
scattered area of 200 square miles reflects
no special discredit on his military
skill; but to say that an officer cannot deal
with the liquor bar directly under his own
moustache when the keys of the place are
in his own pocket, the camp police at his
call and the power to inflict punishment
resident under his own hat, is to insult him
and declare that he does not know the rudi-
ments of his business.
■* * * *
But what sort of chaplains of fighting
forces are these who shout in the ears of
the world that they are afraid of a glass of
beer and tremble at the popping of a cork?
The soldier-parson who knows his work and
believes in the force of the religion he stands
for ought to be able to stand up against
bigger things than the lure of a tent with
a barrel in it. He must have a pretty poor
opinion of the attractiveness of his own
moral and religious teaching if he admits
that it goes flat and becomes inoperative the
moment men are brought under the ordinary
conditions wdiich obtain in daily life in the
majority of civilised countries. The stalwart
devil-wallopers of other armies had no such
fears. They did not demand that the men
under their spiritual charge should be nut in
moral strait-waistcoats and fixed in stocks
ot puritanical coercion before they could
attempt to instil into them the gospel which
they claimed to be the sovereign remedy for
all human ills. On the contrary, they got
right against the Devil on his own ground
and put up a wrestle that won the respect
of the troops and possibly of the Devil also.
On their own showing, the chaplains of
Liverpool camp are not of the old strenuous
breed ; and the question arises whether the
Commonwealth Government is w T ise in pay-
ing out good cash to warrior-gospellers who
won’t take the ordinary risks of peace, but
demand that the camp shall be arbitrarily
reformed before they take on the job of
morally reforming it. If our other military
officers were built on that plan we should
probably hear them demanding the abolition
of armaments before they consented to lead
their men to war.
The plain fact stands—and it would be
folly to ignore it—that the military chaplain
of to-day is the suburban pulpit-banger ot
yesterday. lie is raw at his job —as raw as
the latest bottle-oh recruit —and his opinion
on military matters is of no value. The views
which these raw. untrained and utterly in-
experienced religious camp-followers of ™e
Australian army hold are simply those
which they have found it advantageous to
hold among their mild and amende Sunday
flocks in the various Bethels where they
expounded the Word. Not one parson in ■>
has really thought out the drink problem,
but practically every parson of certai
denominations finds it expedient as well
profitable to rail at the curse of liquor an
apnlaud those who would ladle alcohol in
oblivion with an ineffective pitcnto •
Putting the noise made by the parsons
one side as merely the bleat of inexpenen
attempting to assert itself over reason,
gel down to the bedrock fact that the s
dier’s liquor problem has not been P r °P ■
dealt with at Liverpool. The dry canteen
has been tried and has miserably fallen.
is up to the military authorities to try
wet canteen, which from long expen
elsewhere has not produced the evils
parsons recklessly allege.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Enemy Consolidating the Ground
Already Won.
Lord Sydenham’s Commission on Venereal
Diseases handed the British Government a
final report on the subject last week, and
appears to have mentioned that it was time
to do something in a hurry, which is another
reminder that after the war John Bull and,
incidentally, Australia are going to have
many other problems on hand in addition to
the soldier who lost a leg and the widow’s
pension, and getting back to work. Syphilis
was getting into the national blood stream
at a fairly rapid rate during times of peace.
Since the war broke out it has been getting
there in a staggering manner. An Austra-
lian soldier, writing to The Bulletin from
one of the military hospitals devoted to
treatment of this disease, says :
Venereal disease is running riot through this coun-
try, especially London. After being cured from
wounds or sickness in one of the nursing homes an
Australian is given furlough, and after being handed
anything from £2O to £5O back pay, turned
He seldom has anywhere particular to go ; but the
tlossies’ parade-ground is never far away, and one
of them is usually brushing his arm as he puts his
notes in his pocket. Nine times out of 10 unless he
has friends to go to he reports back to hospital a
lot worse than when he went out, and is drafted off
to one of the hospitals improvised for treatment of
his trouble. Through stress of circumstances these
places are a long way from what they ought to be,
the number of patients outnumbering all anticipa-
tions. Many men sent away cured find themselves
after a week or two still bad. I knew nothing about
this plague before, and want no more experience of
it. For Heaven's sake, warn our lads, for unless they
are armed with knowledge this horror will ruin all
who escape the bayonet and shell.
Now, what is Australia going to do about
this business and everything related to it?
Dnly one State in the Commonwealth (W.A.)
shows any inclination to handle the national
menace in an effective way. • In the others
tt is either side-stepped or tinkered with.
Australia had a good supply of venereal
disease before the armies mustered. There
is much more of it now, and there will be
t 1 ‘fffge additional supply later on. Syphilis
is the chief cause of innumerable ills, of
blindness, insanity, paralysis, peritonitis and
other forms of human suffering. To disre-
P rc * its menace is as wicked as to try and
huddle it out of sight. To approach it with
(css thoroughness than plague and smallpox
is an illustration of how reckless society may
be regarding its own safety. When the war
ends Europe will probably be in a terrible
* with syphilis, and, unless Australia
ln C « S it fearlessly and without reference to
he purity ’ brigade, then we have only
swapped one enemy for another.
The Anarchical Tendency of
Law’n’order.
“Gent” rises to address the jury:—
The usual inquirer, after quoting Bernhardt,
Clattsewitz and perhaps Treitzschke, places the Hun’s
failure to civilise himself to the debit of militarism
and its alleged principles of force and terrorism.
This is wrong. The German nation is not like the
French, a nation of soldiers, but a nation of law-
yers. All the objectionable features of their methods
are due not to militarist teaching, but to the fact
that the German mind is the most perfect specimen
of the lawyer mind yet found outside Bengal. The
lawyer-mind is a growth produced by the practice
of a profession, and its peculiarities are the inevita-
ble outcome of the nature of that profession. Hav-
ing been a Gent. One for years I am qualified to
speak. The client who presents a veneer front of
the finest old oak to the public invariably exposes a
very murky cupboard for inspection by the lawyer,
and, let it be remembered, this is the only part of
human furniture that the lawyer ever sees. Every
person who calls to see a Gent. One wants to pur-
chase deviousness. From this can be excepted the
sale of advice which is often worth the 6s. Bd.
charged for it. Anything for which more is charged
is valuable only by reason of qualities other than
its apparent respectability.
If the Gent. One belongs to the talking branch of
the profession the goods on sale consist of the
suppression of the truth, the false suggestion and
the oratorical device. If he belongs to the scriven-
ing branch the client expects him to furnish docu-
ments which mean than they say, or logical
word-nets which will enmesh or hamper somebody.
No mind thus engaged all day for many days in a
week can accept the ordinary man’s views about a
fair deal. Therefore I can never understand how
the most respectable practitioner can shut his office-
door and walk straight along a footpath. It is pro-
bably accomplished by the mental application of the
saving clause.
The first distinguishing mark, then, of the lawyer-
mind is that it is not controlled by the code of the
average man. The next feature is an enormous
respect for detail, meticulous attention to which is
necessary in a profession mostly dealing with
minutice. Next is the tendency to rely on certain
strange clothes and ceremonial trappings which
early lawyers cunningly considered to have a psy-
chological effect on the unlettered public. Allied to
this is a dignified absence of humor. Next is an
almost superstitious respect for an appearance of
logical soundness however real the underlying absurd-
ity may be. With this is mixed an Asiatic venera-
tion for the literal meaning of phrases and words
without regard to their context or the obvious varia-
tions in sense produced by their setting. Lastly
there is the preference for the stratagem to the
plain course of action, due to conviction in the legal
mind that human nature is governed by no motives
but illiberal ones.
These are the exact spiritual characteristics of the
German nation, and its system is the certain result
of obsession by the lawyer mentality. The commercial
and financial trickiness of the Hun could never have
emanated from a nation of slogging militarists. In
its ingenuity, its detail, obliquity and very mistakes
of construction it is lawyer-made in essence. And
it has often been successful, too, just as the artifice
often comes off in law. Prompt action is necessary
to prevent a recurrence of present stupidity. There
should be an interdiction of legal practice in all
civilised countries. Details of punishment for sur-
reptitiously practising as a lawyer or for the inser-
tion of ambiguities in documents could be settled
later.

To Scab or Not to Scab?
Milne, N.S.W. Assistant Commissioner for
Railways, has been worried over a placard
which appears in the railway workshops.
The printed advice runs :
Don’t scab upon the unemployed by working hard.
Slow work means more jobs—more jobs mean less
unemployed—less competition means higher wages,
less work, more pay. Slow down! Slow down!
Don’t be slaves!
The beautiful industrial theory contained in
the request explains a good many things.
It explains, first and foremost, that the
people who drew it up are strangely warped
in their view of the State and beerily mixed
as to the nature of slavery. Their attitude
is in point of fact exactly that of the slave
and opposed to every notion of the free man.
Any slave who ever breathed would have
subscribed heart and soul to the ideas ex-
pressed. Having nothing to hope for beyond
a crust to eat and little to fear beyond the
daily kick and the bi-weekly walloping, he
naturally regarded the minimum of toil as
an object worth striving for. With the free
man it is otherwise. He knows that he is
a cog in the machinery of the community;
he believes that his work counts in the sum
of things ; and beyond his mere wages as the
reward of service he toils in the fixed belief
that by doing so he acts a man’s part and
helps the nation forward. The man working
for a private employer, more out of necessity
than choice, may acquire the habits of
thought of a slave without any particular
effort; but the man getting good wages as
the direct servant of the State must be
pretty empty if he doesn’t see the
fallacy of the slowdown notion. Logic-
ally, if little work produces more jobs
and consequently more wages, no work
at all ought to produce all the jobs
necessary and all the wages wanted. The
universal strike would, on this reasoning,
be the one thing needful to plant an indus-
trial paradise by the rivers of beer under
the broad shade of eternal beef and never-
fading picture shows. Of course, Billjim
of the Railway workshops doesn’t think that
or he would put the ide* in practice at once.
What he does believe and know is that the
State is on a false financial bottom and kept
going by the aid of the pawnshop ; and that
while cash is being squandered by the State
which the State is not earning, somebody is
likely to pay his w T ages whether he works
fast or slow. The fact seems to be that the
borrow-and-splash policy of Australian
Govts, is not merely pauperising the people,
but bringing them to base and slavish habits
of thought.
“The Old Soldier”: “Rag” (B. 2/3/T6)
is quite correct when he says that the dis-
charge of the 1000 men who failed to attend
the parade on the day after the famous “riot”
in Sydney meant a clear loss of £30,000 if
they had been, on an average, a month in
camp. I have been explaining for the last
two months that, if a man had spent only a
night in camp, his discharge meant some
loss, and that it* would be infinitely better
both for the man himself and the country if
he were compelled to carry out his contract
of service. One much-needed reform is the
abolition of “officers’ schools.” It would be
far better to have all ranks trained together
as soon as they are dismissed recruits’ drill.
There 'is no school like the school of regi-
mental duty. It would be quite easy to
weed out the hopelessly-incompetent officers,
who would be, I am pretty sure, a small
minority, and those who were not incom-
petent would get to know their men better
than they can at present. It is nonsense
to pretend that the British recruit is a lamb-
like creature who gives no trouble. He
can be violently insubordinate if he detects
the least sign of weakness in those who are
set over him. My own impression, from my
experience in dealing with Australians, is
that they are not so apt to make trouble, as
the men of most European countries.
I have seen something of military mutinies,
and the point of my argument was
made clear to me at Etah, India. A nativ<
infantry regiment had made up its mind to
plunder the treasury, where there was a
great deal of money, and go off to Delhi.
One morning the men collected all their
officers’ buggies and horses, and told the
owners that there was to be no parade, but
that they might go. They went —all but
one little subaltern.- He told the regiment
that he wasn’t going, that they were his chil-
dren and he would not let them disgrace
themselves. He kept in front of them in
their disorderly march to the treasury. They
shouted at him and hustled him, but did not
hurt him, and when they came to the build-
ing, which was strong and held by an
armed guard, he called to the guard to fire
on them, even if he were killed. The guard
did not fire, but they would not open the
gate, and as an entrance could not be forced,
and the regiment knew they were not wanted
at Delhi without money, they decided to
recall their shamefaced officers, who were
not far away, and postpone the mutiny. If
the mutineers hadn’t known that little man
intimately from long association, they would
have slain him for a foolish interferer at
the start of the march. The only w y ay to
know soldiers is to live and work with them.

“Bad Australian” : Although the polyglot
press of Buenos Aires puts the question of
the land operations in the world war before
my cosmopolitan neighbors from every possi-
ble point of view, no definite conclusion can
be arrived at as to the real state of affairs,
but recent happenings off the River Plate
make it pretty plain that the British navy
has a terribly long arm. The howls and
lamentations of the local Germans over the
capture of the ITesidente Mitre by a British
warship a few days ago is about the loudest
uproar ever heard in this part of the world.
She is one of a fleet of four owned and
manned by the German crowd who sent the
Cap Trafalgar to harass British shipping in
the early days of the war. Unfortunately
for the Cap, before she had time to get busy
on unarmed ships she met the Carmania, a
British auxilary cruiser of equal weight and
armament off the coast of Brazil, and in 40
busy minutes the Carmania blew her to
glory. Since that time the Presidente Mitre
and her mates have been trading on the
south-east coast under cover of the Argen-
tine flag; but immediately after the capture
of the Mitre the others scuttled for the near-
est “ports,” where they are now hanging out
head to wind with steam up ready to get
out to sea when the first sign of heavy
weather shows. When they do get out some-
thing is likely to happen which will not be
appreciated in Berlin. The Germans have
several papers printed in Spanish and pub-
lished in Buenos Aires for the sole purpose
of influencing public opinion, and these papers
and a number of hired agitators have endeav-
ored to make the most of the co-called “in-
sult” to the Argentine flag; but this little
move cut no ice among the people who count,
and a public meeting convened by the agita-
tors was promptly squelched and the organ-
isers subjected to a severe reprimand by
“Autoridad.” Every thinking Argentine
knows that the railways and practically
every work of importance in the Republic
were built by British money directed by
British brains ; and he also knows just what
Germany has done. The humorous part of
the proceedings was provided by the German
representative in B. Aires when he entered
a most emphatic protest against the “out-
rage,” forgetting in his blind wrath that the
Argentine people were the sole custodians of
the national honor. The bottom fell out of
Herr Blutwurst’s show amidst the scoffs
of the multitude.
The Tory press persists in the same old
fraudulent foolishness of representing the
methods of party politics to be peculiar
to the Labor party only and not common
to all the warring Parliamentary sects. Says
Melbourne Argus :
When the Labor party is in power whatever is
decided upon in secret Caucus must pass in Parlia-
ment. Honest, open, public voting would frequently
defeat the measures decided upon in Caucus.
The fact that Jo Cook & Co., or Peacock
& Co. in Victoria have their little Caucus
gatherings, lobb.v understandings and other
secret compromises is not remarked by the
Argus. All anti-Labor voting seems honest
and open in Tory press eyes, no matter how
privately it may have been fixed up. A
Coalition Party is allegedly composed of
convictions, in the interests of the State,
but the harrowing scene of its sacrifice is
not revealed to the public gaze. The party
suffers in secret, like the Labor Caucuvians,
and they don’t send for an Argus inter-
viewer and say:—
Personally I disapprove of the Bill I voted for last
night, but my conscientious scruples were melted by
the warm pleadings of expediency.
They just go on vaguely talking of their
party’s cause, and the necessity for holding
the party together and presenting an un-
broken front to the other party. Every
measure of purely party origin must
have their support whether they prom-
ised it in caucus or out of caucus,
otherwise they couldn’t hope for party
nomination or the party vote at next
election. These reiterated references to
honest, open public voting in Parliament as
though it were the time-honored practice of
“Liberal” politicians are unscrupulous
appeals to Tory ignorance, bigotry and
stupidity. Every reasonable Liberal knows
that the Argus is exposing the dishonesty
of Liberal partyism when it says: “If the
dissentients in Caucus and the Opposition
voted against a given measure it would very
likely fail to pass.” Of course it would,
because the Opposition caucus party is
solidly endeavoring to turn the Government
caucus party out. But why is. the Opposi-
tion assumed to be more unanimous in its
secret convictions than the other solidly
voting party? How about the dissentients
on the Opposition side who openly and
publicly profess antipathy to the Bill they
privately approve and are loyally voting
against? Between the two sets of base
slaves of the Party-vote system there is not
the proverbial pin to choose.

“Chug”: I agree with your remarks (B.
2/3/T6) on the “Sobriety of Beer” as seen
in the light of the records of the world’s wild
and whirling progress down the path of
the ages ; but one effect of the gentle stimu-
lant which has made so greatly for modera-
tion appears to have been missed. When
the recent military strike occurred in the
great Mexican camp outside Vera Cruz, and
the soldiers, commandeering the trains,
rushed riotously to tear the fronts out of
the pubs of the American city, it might
have been reasonably said that the troops
were a trifle out of hand. Yet big as the
trouble was, the greater number of the
rioters reported for duty the next morning
at Larequipa camp; and the officers had
little trouble in getting them into shape
again. Now, if there had been no liquor
about, if that Mexican riot had been a cold,
dry affair, conducted on the high moral prin-
ciples of thirst alone, would those soldiers
have been in their places again next morn-
ing? Don’t you believe it. The riot would
have still been going on, and Vera Cruz
would have been under martial law and full
of treasons, stratagems and fireworks. This
gives me an idea. Beer was the medium by
which Kings in the great days gone con-
trolled their armies in the field. Discipline
scarcely existed; military methods were
crude; mutiny meant wholesale murder; but
beer was plentiful and made up for the lack
of a lot of other things. When the pent-up
rage of the troops was getting near to burst-
ing point, the King, watching the symptoms
develop, waited his time, and at the exact
moment gave the royal command, “Bring on
the beer !” There was no stint of it. Any-
body who wanted to could bathe in the
tubs. Next morning the sore heads mus-
tered and the thick tongues that had talked
mutiny were silent. The trumpet blared;
the drums beat. “March,” said the King,
and the host lurched forward with a black
eye and a chastened spirit. In civil life
beer was the only policeman ; in the military
camp it was a whole provost-marshal’s staff.
T. E. Day, now an irrigation surveyor for
South Australia, but before that Chief
Government Surveyor for the Northern
Territory, told an Adelaide interviewer that
if the money spent on the coastal district of
the N.T. had been spent on the inland
southern portion “we should have to-day a
thriving pastoral population south of Bar-
row’s Creek.” Then the' man who knows
piled it on like this: “There are splendid
pastoral areas north of the McDonnell
Ranges right through to the Davenport
Ranges, approximately 120 miles on the east
side of the telegraph line, and also on the
west side for many miles north. The great
drawback to development is the isolation.
Given railway communication even to Alice
Springs, it soon would become a thriving
pastoral district, to say nothing of the pos-
sibilities of closer settlement on the rich
flats in the ranges. Fruit and vegetables
grow splendidly at Alice Springs. Dairy
cattle do well there, and I have tasted first-
class butter. For all this country we must
look to the pastoralist, in the same way that
al 1 the settled districts of Australia have
been pioneered. The climate is good, and
white women can live there —a very neces-
sary factor in the settlement of any country
by white people. Provided with rapid means
of transportation, I am confident that the
southern part of the N.T. would become one
of the best meat-producing areas in the
world, and a far more profitable investment
than the .northern part of the Territory.”
Ail the Territory needs is a little less isola-
tion and a little more intelligent administra-
tion. As The Bulletin has said many
times before, the place can’t be proved by
dumping a few Government officials presided
over by a horse-doctor on the coastal fringe
and waiting for chance or Providence or the
Ilevil to do the rest. The present half-
hearted occi.*?)ation is remindful of the key-
less drunk who was bequeathed a fine house
and afterwards said the place was no good
because he had had such a comfortless time
on the doorstep. Until the Territorians get
inland and up on the heights, Administrator
Gilruth is not likely to be more than the
master of a white elephant stud-farm.

When brought up to scratch over the order,
he was sending to America for railway cars
King O’Malley, the “rooster” in charge of
Australia’s Home Affairs, solemnly assured
a deputation of Parliamentarians that the
transcontinental line would require to be
fitted with carriages in which the public
would not be killed if the train, while fleeing
across the desert at 60 miles an hour, fell
over an embankment; also that the cars
could not be made in Australia, but could
be made if the material was available;
further, that the material could be procured
but the cars were wanted by October. It
was a vague, confused kind of yarn, and
next day Cabinet rushed to the rescue and
got the Kink out of a knot by announcing
that his plan for giving the Yankee a job
had been vetoed. If trains are going to
tumble off the Western line because of their
excessive speed O’Malley had better see
about restraining the loco, drivers. Anyhow
the anticipated calamity is not even a serious
reason for importing American rolling stock,
and the fact that steel cars made at New-
port are in use on the Melbourne suburban
lines is a moderately reasonable guarantee
of its being possible to obtain some more
made by Australian builders. All that is
required are the plans.
Erzeroum.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here, where the carnival of blood and death
Ilad marked the track of Islam from old
time,
And peace, with raging lust and burning
breath,
Had ploughed the bloody furrows black with
crime,
The city stood, fronting the winter snow,
And flashing back the gleam of pallid suns
From her high windows, while her close-pent
woes
Shuddered and wept behind black-throated
guns.
Others had hope. The Turkish crescent
waned
Bv the blue lappings of Agean seas ;
The sword that once a red world-triumph
. stained
Was weak to breaking. Other lands had
ease;
But, set amid the mountains of his doom,
Helpless to strike and feeble to endure,
The lost Armenian crouched in Erzeroum,
An abject outcast past all aid and cure.
The waking thunders of a world in arms
Rolled through the passes, fraught with little
cheer.
The mountain air was full of wild alarms —
Europe was far and Islam’s thousands near.
The frowning white Caucasian peaks looked
down
On ragged battle lines the strife made red.
And a grim whisper crept about the town
Of Turks that slew and Cossack hosts that
fled.
The long months dragged their slow and
mirthless way
Down to the dreary winter edged with death ;
The bitter snow on hill and valley lay,
The north breathed terror in its icy breath.
The silent guns, stern-lipped, remote and
proud,
Watched o’er the fortressed heights, but,
more than these,
The vast defences of the storm and cloud
In fierce battalions swept the mountains’
knees.
Sudden the bugle shrilling in the night!
Red lightnings flare across the trampled
snow,
The keen blade gleams, and through the
ghostly fight
In flying ranks the wild, grey horsemen go.
Dost "in the whirling drifts the foemen reel,
The driving bullets edge the roaring gale;
And up the death-strewn steeps the con-
quering steel
Presses to triumph in the dawning pale.
Others had hope. The world-wide battle
swayed
To east and west. Keen woe by land and sea
Wailed for the conquering march so long
delayed,
And the drum throbbing on to victory.
There, set amid the mountains of her doom,
Closed in the iron circle of her fear,
The morning woke for ransomed Erzeroum
On broken Islam and the Cossack’s cheer !
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

Waiting for the Noise to Stop.
After another week of furious fighting
around Verdun not much more was known
at the moment of writing than had been
announced earlier in the fray. The Hun
was still persevering with his attack, the
French continued to hold him up, confidence
was expressed that the assault would fail
to reach its objective, and a fierce scrap was
still raging for possession of Douaumont,
occupation of which was apparently vital to
either side. All the rest of the literature
on the subject has borne the imprint of
heavy censorship and has been mainly specu-
lative and prophetic, there being even such
confusion in the evidence as to make it
difficult to judge whether this battle is really
of the magnitude it at first seemed to be.
It was announced, for instance, that after
13 days’ fighting the German losses in killed,
wounded and prisoners were 100,000. If
this were correct, the battle of Verdun, car-
ried on over a front of 25 miles, had up to
that date been relatively a much less san-
guinary collision than several of last year’s
battles in Flanders alone. It is, too, diffi-
cult to reconcile these figures with the
repeated statements that the German attacks
have been lAade by heavy, massed bodies of
men and swept away by machine-gun and
high-explosive shell fire. Possibly these
losses represent the estimated wastage on
the north-eastern sector where the greatest
strain has been. If that is so, the German
was being terribly punished. But the safer
conclusion is that the outside world knows
mighty little except in a general way of
what is happening in these disturbances until
it is all over and the new situation measured
up. One encouraging feature of the pro-
ceedings was that there had been no sudden
liberation of glad flags at Berlin over the
achievements in the West, and as the Hun
has an incurable habit of decorating the
landscape with fluttering colors on the
slightest provocation, it may reasonably be
concluded that at Verdun there had been
nothing for him to enthuse about except
accumulated corpse. But he produced multi-
tudinous flags when the Atlantic raider
Moewe turned up last week at Wilhelms-
hafen, and —provided the yarn about the
mysterious craft is dinkum —it looks as if
there was this time something to say “Hoch”
about, and that the Kaiser, in distributing
ironmongery amongst the crew, was doing
a fair thing. This phantom Moewe put some
pepper into life on the ocean wave while she
was at large, and by getting back to German
waters—if she has got back and the whole
thing is not a tale —has shown that attempts
at more comprehensive excursions can be
expected later on. Meanwhile there is a
story about the German dreadnoughts mov-
ing off somewhere, of Turkey being anxious
for a separate peace, of Bulgaria getting into
a state of revolt and of Roumania being
still on the verge of joining the fight.
Later on the same intelligence will arrive in
a great hurry some more and create tre-
mendous excitement for the 74th time.
“Hamer” : An organisation with a genuine
grievance to-day is the Merchant Service
Guild, whose members are responsible for the
safety of the ships that wander round the
Australian coasts. In 1910 the Guild got an
award which vastly improved matters; but
since then there has not been any betterment
in the brass-bounder’s conditions. The
Seamen’s Union, whose award expires this
year, was amiably given an additional 255.
per month all round for its members when it
approached the shipowners with a few firm
remarks about the high cost of living and
the scarcity of men, while the stewards and
pantrymen swept up an extra 30s. per month.
The donkeyman on a steamer now gets 255.
per month more than the third officer, and a
fireman ss. per month more. Both these
toilers have a chance of earning overtime,
while the officer merely has a chance of
working overtime. The Guild, having been
unsuccessful in getting the shipowners to
treat its members as they did the members
of more pugnacious organisations, has been
prominent on the front step of the Arbitra-
tion Court, waiting for that tribunal to invite
it inside. Just as its turn seemed coming
the other day the Broken Hill men created
the trouble which caused Pearce to suggest
that, in the interests of peace and quietness,
iheir troubles should he given precedence of
all other business. So the patient Guild,
being an organisation that does not hurry
up the procession by waving the stfike blud-
geon, is still waiting in the cold, while its
members tramp the bridge and envy the
care-free cove who heaves coal into the hum-
ming fires and has no undue scruples about
raising Cain if he thinks he isn’t getting a
fair deal.
Portion of the remarks made by the Coroner
after giving his finding on the death of the
soldier Brown, who was knocked down and
kicked to death by a mob of others at the
hospital camp on Milson Island (N.S.W.) :
Some of the soldiers who have given evidence
disgrace our army, and now that this inquest is
concluded I hope they will be discharged.
Why? A more sensible course would be for
the Commonwealth to deprive soldiers in
hospital through tlieir own carelessness of
pay while they stay there. That is the
British rule, and its enforcement would
automatically stop gambling as well as
encouraging patients to get well and resume
duty. In any event the supervision in such
a place as Milson Island ought to be more
effective. It should, at least, prevent
patients murdering each other over a game
of two-up.

Auditorium Frederic Coleman Lectures
One of the articles in the New Thought
creed is that if a New-Thougliter wants to
do anything all that is required is to affirm
her belief that she will do it, make up the
sort of mind that a New-Thougliter lias, and
she’ll succeed. Thus, in the New Thought
play, “The Victory,” produced for the first
time before an audience of New-Thoughtists
at the Little Theatre (Sydney) on Saturday,
one of the characters makes up his mind
that he will sing, and apparently he sings,
though the audience is not permitted to hear
him. It seems a pity that Julia Seton,
the author-in-chief of “The Victory,” did
not take advantage of this plank in the New-
Thought platform. For all she need have
done was to say 1,960,427 times that she
would write a play, and “The Victory”
would have been a play. As it is, the charm
works only as far as the first act: after that
it may have been New-Thought (which is.
apparently, Blue-Thought), but it appeared
to be chiefly interval interspersed with
stump-speeches and stage-miracles. There
are only four characters in the piece,
and the plot deals, as seems inevitable
in all religious plays, with the naughty
Old-Thought siren and the deceived Blue-
Thought wife. You can’t keep sex out
of Blue-Thought. The husband is a
fashionable hypnotist, and most of the
play that isn’t interval is given up to an
extremely realistic presentation of him in
the toils of a member of the oldest
profession in the world. Exhibit A
is a pair of lady’s garters with dia-
mond clasps; and the one contribution this
exhibit makes to Blue-Thought is that the
unredeemed still wear those articles of attire.
Exhibits B and G are two miracles—-the
raising of a paralytic girl and the curing of
the hero, afflicted with locomotor ataxia as
a result of too much and too seductive Old-
Thought.
* * * *
Had it not been for the excellence of the
cast. “The Victory” would have been a
defeat. Herbert Ford and Agnes Keogh as
the passionate lovers act with a realism
seldom seen on the stage. They paw and
“FACTRY “A NDS.” AT SYDNEY
PALACE.
Billy the Boy (Jack Kirby ) decides to
conquer Benno the Boshter (Maurice Lynch )
by biting his ear.
cuddle each other with great and abiding
enthusiasm. At this point the Old-Thought-
ers begin to believe in Blue-Thouglit.
In the succeeding act the hero gets the
reward prophesied by his des#rted wife. As
the locomotor ataxist, Herbert Ford is a
compelling figure of horror. Eily Malyon
makes the utmost of her inconvincing part;
and it is only her personality that saves the
miracles that took place in Sydney Domain
from appearing ludicrous. Frederick Ward,
who produces the play, admirably personates
a derelict who is reformed into a Domain
orator. The season is to continue, and
Frederick Ward, who has taken a long lease
of the Little Theatre, promises other pro-
ductions that will not be so Blue-Thoughty.

Eleven weeks gone and “So Long, Letty”
is still going strong at Her Majesty’s, Syd-
ney. Moral: Give a show enough powdered
legs and it will run for ever.

The box-office of the Criterion Theatre
is still “Under Fire” from massed attacks of
the public.
Equilibrist Harry De Coe, who supplies
one of the four new turns at Sydney Tivoli,,
has been specially imported to make nervous
patrons jump. He amuses himself by build-
ing a trembling pyramid of chairs and tables
and seating himself negligently on the top
rail of the Impossible. lie implies by his
careless attitude when aloft that he has half
a mind to fall back with a sickening crash
and shatter the base of his skull; so when
he has quite finished and got back to stage
level, people with nerves wipe the cold sweat
off their foreheads and thank the Lord they
haven’t to witness it every night. Mil-
dred Grover, a robust U.S.A. comedienne
with a voice of beaten brass- takes charge of
the house for awhile and is cheerful about it.
Musette, a lady violinist, sings, fiddles and
dances at the same time, and is a bigger suc-
cess than the heavily-moustached gentleman
in green satin who used to gravely step-
dance while playing a harp. Pearl and
Irene Sans present a featherweight fashion
turn which has some faded singing in it.

Another Boucicault revival, “Arrah-na-
Pogue,” at Sydney Adelphi on Saturday
night provided Gerald E. Griffin with reason-
able excuse for exercising his dignified tenor
on some more Irish songs. The season con-
