“Twelfth Night” on Saturday drew the
biggest crowd of the season to Melbourne
Princess’s. A lot of people who had missed
Wilkie’s admirable work in “The Merchant,”
“Othello” and “Hamlet” came to see his
Malvolio, and the crowd must have in-
cluded some of that large section of the
public which wants a “larf” even if it has
to go to Shakspeare. The dissipation
was duly provided. Malvolio makes
his first entrance preceded by four
grotesque flunkeys who seem to have
stalked out of comic-opera. Which spice of
buffoonery further commends the show to
popular taste. The comedy was born funny
in parts, the acting achieves hilarity fre-
quently, and certain items of superfluous
extravagance are thrust uoon it. The noisy
gathering in the kitchen of Olivia’s house had
most to fear from comparison with Mel-
bourne’s recollections of other companies in
the same scene. John Cosgrove as Toby
Belch, and Faulkner as Aguecheek got
through their orgie fairly well, although they
carried the tail end of it to the verge of
mere absurdity. Cosgrove soars a little less
vehemently than many another Belch, and is
not so obtrusively full of liquor all the time,
lie is best in the garden scenes, and his
management of the duel between Aguecheek
and Viola suggests the unfuddled mind in a
highly disreputable body. Faulkner, in the
grateful character of the foolish knight, is
equally conventional and convincing. There
seems to be only one sort of Aguecheek in
stage tradition, and Faulkner being thin
enough for the character, is just It. Prob-
ably Shakspeare thought of the thirsty ass as
a flabby citizen inclining already to fat, but
the painters have depicted Sir Andrew as
a human sardine with no oil about him, so
theatrical art insists on keeping him thus
ia the picture.
* * * *
Champney has only one qualification
for the unthankful • part of the clown,
and he makes it his apology. He
sings pleasantly on two occasions.* Miss
Harvey is a merry and bright little Maria in
whose diction there is no sharpness, and
whose smile bears no malice against the
man she has got her knife into. Fred
Fabian answers all requirements as Fabian,
the manservant who helps to accuse Mal-
volio of lunacy. The other characters —
the strictly sober ones—are effectively played,
with the one exception of the beauteous
Countess Olivia. Miss Sydney presents a
hard, unwinsome countess, instead of a warm-
blooded young vision ready to fall upon the
neck of the first pretty boy who makes a
pretty speech to her. Evidently she disliked
the lovesick Duke on account of his whiskers,
whereas Viola adored him for his whiskers!
Walter Hunt speaks the lines of tb
poor dear Duke with all due tenderness an.l
indiscretion. 1 red Francis is a tine
( apt. Antonio; Marcus St. .John i s ’ ano tW
honest mariner, and D. Belbridge scores
particular success as Viola’s twin-hmrtL*
Most of his success, of course, £ h,
simple fact that he and the lady muu?
indeed be easily mistaken for one another in
the dusk, with the light behind them— Di-
vided they held their tongues. But there
is one critical moment when the twins nro
fessing profound unconsciousness of the faet
that they couldn’t be mistaken for one
another in broad daylight, rejoice mutually
in the fate which has preserved both of them
from a watery grave. Belbridge puts much
feeling into his share of this strain on one's
imagination, and the present writer
never knew the reunion of Viola and
Sebastian to impress an audience as it did
last Saturday.
* * * #
Allan Wilkie is easily the most picturesque
and forcible Malvolio who has come this
way since the long-ago. No accomplished
actor, able to look the part, can go
wrong in it up to a certain point—unless
he g.oes wilfully wrong. But Wilkie adds
personal magnetism to his equipment as a
mummer, and when at last the degraded ego-
tist is brought before the Countess in the
capacity of an alleged lunatic, his mental
distress can be felt by the audience. Miss
Hunter-Watts divides the honors with Wilkie.
Her Viola is a most interesting girl-boy, a
tendency to drop her voice at the end of a
speech and to slap her leg to express boy-
ishness being her only faults. Her struggle
with natural timidity in the duel scene is
convincing. The staging is good enough and
the little orchestra deserves a word of
thanks.
** * *
A second hearing of “Gipsy Love,” at Mel-
bourne Her Majesty’s, confirms this paper’s
previous impression that the music makes
ample apologies for the futility of the narra-
tive. There are some sweet melodies in the
picturesque show, and the orchestration
generally is sufficiently good to keep the ear
from listening too closely to the trivial love
affair of Joszi and Ilona. As in the first
Melbourne production, Derek Hudson plays
the swaggering gipsy canoodler with tile
romantic ringlets and earrings of great mag-
nitude. while disclosing the feelings of his
faithless heart in a baritone of throaty
emphasis. The whole conception is some-
what suggestive of garlic and hair-oil, and
there is also the actor’s habit of hanging his
head and addressing impassioned words to
his decollete shirt-front to further spoil the
illusion of devil-may-care gallantry which is
alleged to turn Ilona’s virgin thoughts from
home and duty. Ilona, as played by Ethel
Cadman, is a melodious object of public sym-
pathy from the moment she succumbs
to the glucose message of .Toszi’s violin. But
she never lets anyone suspect that she is
revelling in the adventure. Rather she
implies the miserable enjoyment of one who
is increasingly conscious of her foolishness.
The part of Lady Babby is liberally treated
with Florence Young's amiability, the actress
also worthily doing her share in the dance
duets with Hudson and Leslie Holland. Hol-
land’s two or three hours as Dragotin have
depressing periods when his comedy descends
to sheer clowning; but John Ralston repeats
his wine song with the aforetime success, and
Reginald Roberts’s tenor lifts out the charac-
ter of the jammy Jonel. Phil Smith is too
brief a pariner for the Ingenue whom Minnie
Love makes so tall and charming. For the
purpose of exchanging endearments, Smith
must either stand on a chair or Miss Love
must submit to temporary curvature of the
spine. Minor parts are filled by Mrs. George
Lauri, Miss Addle Lennard and C. R- Ban-
tock, while Charles Albert and a damsel
whose name is not mentioned on the pro-
gramme dance “fetchingly” in the second and
third acts. Next Saturday “The Belle of
New York" will be revived for a week.

This is another consecutive week of "It
Pays to Advertise” at Melbourne Royal,
where bluff is the soul of business and the
talk is all about soap and dollars when it
doesn’t mark the connection between dollars
and soap.

Another Melbourne show
The exhibition of the late E. Phillips Fox’s pictures
decorates the walls of the Athenieum Gallery with
the colors of Joseph’s coat. The place has not looked
so gay since Rupert Bunny exhibited his wife’s col-
lection of attitudes and teagowns. Sun-splashed
“THE WAYBACKS.” AT MELBOURNE
KING'S.
One of Dad's daugh-
ters. (The artist
apologises to all
country ladies.)
Dad's son, a fine e-T-
ample of a young
Australian. (The
artist will not be at
home to country
callers.)
nudes, ladies decorated like zebras, girls in bright
green and boys in vivid scarlet ; landscapes, sea-
scapes and figure studies, all exhilarating and in-
teresting. Fox delighted in setting himself techni-
cal problems for the sheer intrinsic joy of solving
them. Only a painter who loved art for work's sake
could handle the problems of reflected light and
direct sunlight as he does in “The Green Parasol.”
The influence of light on color was to Fox the most
absorbing of studies, and his treatment of sunlight
and flesh tints in several open-air nudes —notably
“Summer,” which was shown at the autumn Salon
of 1913, and “The Butterfly” is masterly. “The
Arbor” and “Lamplight” are pictures in which seri-
ous technical difficulties have been triumphantly
overcome, and “Tired” and “Reclining Nude” are
beauty. There are also some fine portraits, including
Marshall Hall (Fox and Hall, both recently dead, had
a great admiration for one another's art), Baldwin
Spencer and Rankin Lyle, although in the last-named
the noisy green and scarlet of the professorial robes
scarcely give the face a chance of appreciation.

Melbourne Bijou suffered from another in-
flux of Yankee artists on Saturday. Hazel
Morris, a twangsome ragtimer, headed the
rush and showed that she was only distin-
guished from other offenders of her type by
her appalling habit of pulling her face out of
shape. Early and Laight also imparted
nasal inflections and a good deal of humor to
a beery sketch ; but the Glockers said little
in support of their nationality, their job
being to whirl vessels of water around their
heads with an industry worthy of a more
expensive liquid. The rest of the bill was
mainly Amurkan, the pick of the barrow
being Rose Lee Ivy.
The Bulletin doesn’t know what the
Heart of a Painted Woman is like, but
Hoyt’s, of Melbourne, profess to have the
latest information about it. The organ is
throbbing all this week through a five-reel
production featuring Olga Petrova, a Yankee
film actress noted for her luscious appoint-
ments and beguiling eye-play. Vivian Martin,
another Amurkan of a daintier and different
kind, is the other celebrity screened at Hoyt’s
Theatres this week. Vivian demonstrates her
powers as an actress in a story entitled “The
Arrival of Perpetua.”

AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.”; One word more about George
Musgrove’s lucky venture with the “Tam-
bour Major” co., a matter of so very long
ago that no present explanation can be
readily accepted as correct in all particulars.
“Kaniva” (B. 27/1/HO) revived an ancient
allegation by envious rivals of “Muzzy” to
the effect that he practically “jumped” his
uncle Lyster’s claim to the co. whilst he was
bringing it out from England, and that
Lyster “never recovered the stock, and died
a , few before the notable production.”
“Fax” in the same issue of the B. said that
Muzzy was commissioned to engage the co.
by his uncle, whose death thrust all the
responsibility and most of the profits upon
the lucky emissary. And now Mr. Woinar-
ski —son-in-law of the late Musgrove— states
the family tradition. According to this,
the enterprising young man went to
England “on his own,” with £2OOO borrowed
from a generous friend, and a promise of
another £lOOO if required, lie engaged the
co. on the strength of this not very large
capital, and was bringing the enterprise to
Melbourne when his uncle Lyster died. Says
Mr. Woinarski, in conclusion : “The point I
want to emphasise is that not a penny of
Lyster’s money was in the successful ‘Tam-
bour Major’ venture.” Now I would be
the last person in Melbourne to side with the
scandal-mongers of whom “Kaniva” spoke,
for I know some of the allegations to be
false, but the point I want to emphasise is
that George Musgrove engaged the co. to
appear at Lyster’s theatre, so he couldn’t
have been then starting in direct opposition
to that old-established operatic manager.
My casual informant of the facts was the
late Charles Truelove, who came from Eng-
land to Melbourne on the same boat with
the co., and remained on friendly terms with
members of it for long afterwards. True-
love was an amateur musician of some mark,
and 30 years ago he gave me to understand
that the T.M. Co. had regarded Lyster’s con-
nection with Musgrove as their guarantee
that all would be well with them in the
event of the venture proving a failure. lie
also told me that the news of Lyster’s death,
received at the first Australian port the
boat touched, was a great shock. It created
some consternation. One result was that
he (Truelove) was offered a share in the
venture, but to his subsequent sorrow he
declined to chip in. After arriving in Mel-
bourne he was engaged to sort-up the great,
accumulation of operatic scores and loose
music, printed and in manuscript, which the
enthusiastic Lyster had stored at the Opera
House. This story, which exonerates the
late G.M. from any suspicion of having
played his uncle false, is quite, consistent
with the statement that “not a penny of
Lyster’s money was in the venture.” But
I guess that his managerial reputation and
interest as lessee of the Opera House had
been in the venture when G.M. undertook
it.
“Zuzzuz” : The scientific researches of the
learned “Harrison O.” (B. 24/2/’J6) inspired
me to go out and Royal Commission Flossie.
I find she is incredibly and incurably maud-
lin. She rarely extends her sympathies even
to a musical show. Blood diluted with
marital aberrations, and pseudo-religion
forms her ideal of dramatic pleasure. Her
reading, often quite considerable and due
doubtless to a vast amount of leisure, is of
the same brand. I questioned in all 23 prac-
titioners, and secured the following interest-
ing data: Favorite dramatic production in
Sydney to date—“ The Sign of the Cross”
(16) ; “The Village Priest” (4). Three
never visited the theatre, unless out of pro-
fessional politeness. All, however, liked
the “pictures”; though placing t'he
Tivoli and National before these. The
Follies were unanimously applauded. Of
reading matter the following is a resume
(three votes to each in order of preference
of writers) : Effie Adelaide Rowlands, Charles
Garvice and similar milk-and-water, mostly
feminine (15) ; Victoria Cross (10) ; Elinor
Glyn (9) ; Marie Corelli (8) ; Hall Caine
—“The Woman Thou Gavest Me,” etc. —
(7) ; American magazines (6) ; Thomas
Hardy (4) ; French translations (3). All the
ladies liked cheap prints of the “Supple-
ment” variety ; all had photographic collec-
tions of their friends; and all liked ragtime.
One can’t drop into Marlow’s Melbourne
theatre these Shakspearian times without
being forcibly reminded of the up-to-dateness
of Immortal William. The highly-respectable
Malvolio, reproving Toby Belch for his
booziness and sinfulness in general, is just
the cold-blooded moralist who would close
the pubs altogether if he lived nowadays.
Jolly Maria is the 16th century equivalent
of the woman “sport,” whose opinion of Mal-
volio is founded on the fact that he has
never wanted to hug her, although she is
such a dead-easy hug. And “Hamlet”!
Note the modern significance of the King’s
policy in sending for Rosencrantz and Guild-
enstern to discover the secret cause of his
nephew son-in-law’s queer behavior! He
puts a couple of German-Jew spies on the
job.
Arthur Shirley, of Australia, has reached
the top in the U.S.A. motion-play busi-
ness, and is now picked to play lead in a
tremendous flicker entitled “Fall of a
Nation.” Eighteen months ago he starred
in Australia in “The Shepherd of the
Southern Cross” and “The Silence of Dean
Maitland.” Then the local slump in picture
manufacture drove him to U.s., where
he was snapped up first by Kalem, then by the
Universal Co. He is one of the 2000 movie
“stars” of U.S.A. who own a motor-car. I
don’t know of any in Australia. Another Aus-
tralian, Louise Carbasse, has joined the Uni-
versal Film Co., and is appearing under her
marriage name, Louise Welch.
“First Old Man”: Harry Lauder, 1 see,
has been trying his hand as a maker of
maxims, thinking, no doubt, they will come
in useful in these economising times. “Mither
wad na waste,” says he piously, “and I
should only be disgracing her memory if I
wasted.” Whereupon he launches a half-
score platitudinous sentences at a spend-
thrift world. Some specimens: “There is
as much pleasure in reading a bankbook as
a novel.” “It is more exhilarating to feel
money in your pocket than beer in your
stomach.” “Always get good value from
tradesmen. They watch that they get good
money from you.” Very excellent advice, no
doubt; but a Yankee comedian of my
acquaintance was, I think, terser. I was
seeing him off to his native land, and as he
wrung my hand he remarked, “So long, bo.
Take plenty of water in your whisky, but
don’t take any bad money.”
“Private Boxer”: The much-expelled nig-
ger, Jack Johnson, has now been outed in
England, the authorities in that long-suffer-
ing country having ordered the black bruiser
off the premises owing to his conduct towards
the chorus-ladies in the cast of a revue in
which he and his latest wife were appearing.
So the unspeakable is moving along, mines
and torpedoes permitting, to Buenos Aires,
where he alleges he is going to fight for
£lO,OOO. During his last tour through Great
Britain he talked nothing but fight. With
colossal “hide” he elected himself a special
recruiting-officer, and from his elaborate
motor-car exhorted the gaping populace to
get out and shoulder a gun for King George,
not forgetting to mention incidentally that
great work was being done in the firing-line
by the big gun the Tommies have named after
himself. It was about time he was thrown
out on his cauliflower.
“8.J.” : Ada Rehan has passed out at New
York. She was born in Ireland, but went to
Uncle Sam’s country when a youngster,
making her first stage appearance, at 13,
in “Across the Continent,” written by her
brother-in-law, Oliver Byron. It was under
Augustin Daly that she did her best work,
playing 200 parts in N’Yark and London. As
a Shakspearian actress her delivery of blank
verse won the admiration even of Bernard
Pshaw. In fact, she must have been one of
the few first-class actresses who did not
yearn to have the “Dramatic. Opinions and
Essays” burned by the common or garden
hangman.
GREGAN McMAHON. directing a Mel-
bourne Repertory rehearsal.
“M.P.A.”: Arranging a country tour for
a dramatic organisation is a more difficult
task every year owing to the hold the mov-
ing-picture show has on the country ’alls.
The picture-man, as a rule, is unwilling to
give an outside show a look in. I am at
present advancing “On Our Selection,” and
find it mighty hard work to place the show
on one-night stands for a run of,
say, 28 towns in 2;) nights. For instance, in
one big town two local picture syndicates
combined, one having a lien on the only local
hall for three nights in the week, besides
having a monopoly of all the hoardings in
the place and the absolute services of the
billposter. At this place the billposter
demanded payment at the rate of 15s. per
hour, and cash up in advance. Yet though
the pictures in the country towns are
terrors as opposition to the average touring
show, dramatic or otherwise, they knock
nothing off “The Selection.” The fact that
the picture show charges 6d. and Is. doesn t
stop Cocky from weighing out 45., 3s. and
2s, for the thing he wants. But the difficulty
in fixing a tour nowadays indicates that the
time is not far distant when the country
tour for an organisation like Bert Bailey s
will be a thing of the past, unless it is pre-
pared to play on terms with the picture
people.

Died in Sydney last week, at under ->O.
Ilarley Mackenzie, commercial lawyer and
man about town. lie had a remarkable
circle of acquaintances and diversity of inter-
ests : he was the bachelor known to every
society miss and matron, and he was. the
hard-headed director of cos. which mined
coal and ran fashionable hotels. The charm
of his manner he probably inherited from
his father, one of the best-known Sydney
doctors of the last generation.
“Old Salt” : A queer result of the war is
that the deep-water berth at Circular Quay,
Sydney, where not long ago the Bremen,
Grosser Ivurfurst, Barbarossa and other
craft of the Norddeutscher Lloyd used to
lie, is now occupied by sailing ships such as
the Chinese barque Alexa and the Yankee
schooner Winslow. It is 30 years sinee sail-
ing ships used to berth regularly at the
Quay. Then the Manly ferry started where
the old N.D.L. berth is now, and the North
Shore ferry from the opposite side. This
thoughtful arrangement was made so that all
ferry-boats might cross each other’s bows off
the P. & O. wharf. The space between the
two ferries was in those days occupied by
sailing ships—the Cutty Sark, the Brilliant,
the Yimeria and other ocean fliers which
used to come for wool. Those were the times
when the steam tram had just arrived to
fight the ’bus and when excur-
sionists to Mosman (6d. each way) often
got lost in the wild scrub of Middle Harbor.
All these relics have disappeared—and back
the windjammer comes to the Quay !
New Fellows of the Royal Society (Eng.)
are J. H. Maiden and Prof. Pollock, of Syd-
ney. Living Australians who previously
received this ribbon of the scientific world
are Profs. David, Haswell and Wilson, of
Sydney Uni.; Orme Masson, Baldwin
Spencer and J. H. Michell, Melbourne; Stir-
ling, Adelaide; ex-Sydney Profs. Liversidge
and Threlfall; ex-Melbourne Profs. Martin
and Dendy; ex-Adelaide Profs. Horace Lamb
and W. H. Bragg, and those brilliant Aus-
tralians Dr. Grafton Elliott Smith, the
anatomist; Dr. David Bruce, famous for his
African sleeping sickness investigations, and
Dr. Walter Rosenhain, the metallurgist.
Maorilanders who are F’s.R.S. are Ernest
Rutherford, Benham (Dunedin) and
Leonard Cockayne (Christchurch).
The room in which Melbourne City Council
meets has one good thing about it; its deco-
rations and furnishings are Australian made
and of Australian matm-ial. It would be
hard to beat them anywhere. The furniture,
panelling and other woodwork are of Tas-
manian blackwood, and Gippsland marble is
used in the walls. This marble, by the way,
is also used in Melbourne’s new Public
Library, and in both cases with distinguished
effect.
“The Bishop” : Many and ingenious were
the dodges at Anzac for drawing Johnny
Turk’s fire, the most successful being that
known as the “jam-tin stunt.” Bags full of
tins would be collected in hillside trenches
overlooking those of the enemy. At a signal,
accompanied by such yells as only charging
Australians are capable of, these bags would
be hurled over our parapets, whence they
would roll with a muffled clatter down the
hill. The outburst of rifle and machine-gun
fire which always followed was generally
fierce enough to give beach-parties the idea
that a genuine attack was in progress.
One day last week a strange American
steamer staggered into Sydney Heads, and
when the pilot clambered aboard and asked
Brother Jonathan whether he had a cargo of
motor-cars or comic papers, Brother ,T. casu-
ally remarked that he was bound for Adel-
aide with 4000 cases of gunpowder and 2400
cases of gasoline, but as the inflammable
liquid was spilling all over the explosiveness
he reckoned it would be safer to clean things
up in Sydney harbor than start monkeying
with the situation at sea. Whereupon the
pilot did a rapid calculation to discover how
much energy would be produced if someone
dropped a match in the right place and
hurriedly brought the vessel to anchor in
Rose Bay. On the shores of that marine in-
dentation are some of Sydney’s most exclu-
sive residential areas, and for a few days
the inhabitants were about as close to a
record display of fireworks as if they had
been living in the suburbs of Verdun.

A CASUALTY.
FAIR ONE: “A scrap of shrapnel to blame ?”
HERO (lamely): “ Er — no. A slip in the
tango.”
“Haka”: The police recently bagged five
men in the entrance leading to the back of
a M.L. pub and charged them with being
on licensed premises after hours. Four pleaded
guilty and were fined, but the fifth man
entered a plea of not guilty. The entrance,
which provided access to the back of the pub,
did similar duty for the public library which
adjoined, and No. 5 adroitly explained
“that he was spending the evening on the
library side.” As the police had not taken
any footprints the case was dismissed. Now
did the foolish four forget the library was
there, or was the lucky last man the only
one who knew of the library’s existence?
At the end of 1914 the barque Antiope,
after rolling over the ocean since ’Go, was
bought at Newcastle by a Maoriland coal
co., sailed to Wellington (M.L.) and theie
transformed into a hulk. Since then ships
have become valuable commodities, and
not long ago fresh masts and innards weie
thrust into the weary tub, and she is now
carrying a load of timber to Australia from
Kaipara. The venerable craft has had a
queer history. In the long ago she ran
regularly round the Horn from Sydney to
London and back again the other way. Then
she was in the South American trade. In
the Russo-Japanese war the Japs took her
as a prize and sold her to some Yanks, and
they put her on the Puget Sound timber run.
New, though old and tired, she can earn for
her owners as much as most tired racehorses
can earn for Israel.
“D.F.” : Out in the Tasmanian bush where
the mind of the mainland visitor grows moss
in a week there are dishevelled inventors
busily making airships and torpedoes and
patent cheese-toasters that are operated by
lightning. One genius lately completed a
contraption that was to be a chair in the day-
time, a bed in the evening, and a steam
plough whenever required. It worked well
for a little while as a chair-bed; then
one night when the inventor was reciting
a recruiting story, the machine suddenly
transformed itself into a bed, and the half-
strangled inventor had to be rescued from
his own Juggernaut with a pitchfork. Dur-
ing the rescue operations the thing gave off
vicious steam-plough symptoms, and had to
be quietened with water. The inventor is
now endeavoring to convert the ruins into
an aeroplane, the motive power of which
will be Christian Science.
* * * *
“Chasuble”: The Christmas Bulletin sur-
prised readers by advocating a National
Church. A large minority believe in it.
Lockington the Jesuit (the most eloquent
preacher in Melbourne) has just been declar-
ing that the Catholic Church will be the
National Australian. This talk is not
relished by Catholics at large, who don’t con-
sider themselves strong enough as yet to in-
dulge in it. Not so long ago I wrote else-
where in favor of an Australian National
Church. The principal local Catholic
newspaper slated me, and so did the
late Cardinal Moran, declaring that it
was an insidious Anglican move. How-
ever, I will persist that there is to be a
Church. For one thing we don’t want this
eternal begging-box rattling. There must
be a dignified State-paid clergy, with no
cadge. I don’t mind being the first arch-
bishop of it if my salary isn’t to be made up
with euchre parties and jumble fairs.
* * * *
“Murkah” explains:—
Of course, when I telegraphed to Bill “weather
unsuitable postpone,” the telegraph office delivered
him something about “Mother not being suitable,”
and made him think I had taken to
drink. So it is not surprising that
business houses have contracted the
habit of repeating important or tricky
words, and send their messages thus:
“Send case striped repeat striped
paint for barber’s repeat poles.” They
thus increase the chance of not order-
ing a case of whisky. Bill’s yowl
front the bush to “Meet me arriving
after to-morrow” has also been altered
to dodge the increasing casualness of
the national monopoly. Bill now
carefully adds the day of his antici-
pated coming, and urges Mary to
“Meet him to-morrow Friday.” Pre-
viously his telegram, on account of
taking numerous rests, was not deliv-
ered until Friday, and Friday was
then to-day and Saturday was to-
morrow, and an anguished Bill hung
round the station waiting for Mary,
who didn’t turn up till next day. The
absence of any noticeable mark to
indicate that a telegram has been in
the refrigerator for 24 hours or so
requires attention. Every message
should be plainly endorsed “This
telegram was lodged yesterday (or
last week), but owing to somebody’s
ineptitude is not delivered until
(here follow time and date).” This would at any
rate give a man a chance of knowing whether
Friday was to-morrow or Saturday the day before
yesterday.

“Yarrum”: The ancient mariner who
wrote of the old Murray (B. 23/12/T5)
must have skipped several drink-stained
pages of the log. Here they are :
The Murray was built about 1857, and traded
awhile to the Murray mouth in S. Aus. After a
spell in this trade she was converted without benefit
of clergy to a sailer, and with her canvas spread
arrived off the Hokitiki (M.L.) bar during the height
of the gold rush down there in the ’6o’s. Her cargo
was foodstuffs and spirits. The bar being unwork-
able and supplies of spirits in the town very
short, cheerful instructions were sent to beach the
boat. This was accordingly done, and for a consid-
erable time the drink-ship remained high and dry. She
was eventually bought by the Anchor Company, who
A FAMILY FAILING.
Harold Eugene Kingston, 18, a bugler, was charged with stealing a gallon of rum.
ltem of recent not in Sydney.
SHADES OF THE RUN CORPS: “Come over here."
converted her back to a steamer. She then began a
steady and respectable career as a passenger boat. Any
experienced travellers of the ’7o’s and ’BO s would
have given her a first-class reference. Her yacht-
like lines, combined with a good spread of canvas,
gave, with the steam power, a good turn of speed,
and many exciting races were run by the old packet
against competitors. Not proving large enough
to carry all her admirers, her owners had her cut in
two and lengthened by 20ft. This was in 1887. But
the travellers of those ancient times advertised her
virtues so widely that the waterfront was thronged
by people with carpet-bags clamoring for passages,
and every time she put forth there were disappointed
voyagers cursing on the wharf. If marine architec-
ture had been equal to it the Murray would have
grown into a 13,000-tonner by now ; but the passen-
ger business gradually got beyond her, and in 1895
she was sold to Capt. Conty, acting for N.S.W. buyers.
History tells of a famous brass band which sailed as
the Murray’s crew. In port they were always sure
of a welcome, and on the coast it was no uncom-
mon occurrence to see rival hotelkeepers bidding for
their discord before the boat tied up.

“W.M.”: One of the Rabaul heroes had
landed back in Sydney and set to work to
overtake his arrears of beer-drinking. At
about midnight he was forming an islet in a
torrent of muddy gutter-water. Presently
his dream was shattered by the boot of a
policeman knocking him gently in the ribs.
“What are you doing there?” asked the voice
under the helmet. The soldier sighed con-
tentedly and looked up. “It’s this way,
mate. When I was up at Rabaul they fed
me on so much tinned fish that I’ve grown
scales, and I was just going to swim up
this blanky river!”
“Hori”: A soldier who was tired of the
war cut his throat in Tauherenikau camp
(ALL.) and was carried to the hospital at
Trentham camp, 40 miles away, where he
duly died. The body was laid in a hut and
a sentry posted. In the twilight, a sergeant-
ccok, or a cook-sergeant, decided to have a
look at deceased. He found the sentry,
who had been unrelieved for about
ten hours, fast asleep. The striped cook
did not wake him but entered and knelt to
look at the corpse. Just then the sentry
awoke and, peering inside the morgue, saw
what he took to be the dead arising.
That was enough for him. He started for
home while a surprised sergeant-cook
watched him tearing through the dusk on his
way to Stewart Island.
“D.”: A wounded Anzac, writing from
Abbey Wood camp, Kent, says: “Most of our
Australian officers seem to be fine men. I
saw, however, a horrible exception—an
undersized rat, liis tunic decorated with
cheap-jack, gilded badges that were certainly
not regimental. His slouch hat, with an
unnecessary number of emu feathers, leg-
gings. spurs, etc., might have passed muster ;
but he also carried an enormous stockwhip,
instead of the riding-crop which mounted
officers use.” But teas he an Australian
officer? A few Australian detectives in
London to interview “officers” with stock-
whips and boomerangs would be well worth
wb±4e.

the opinion of some German newspaper being
cabled. Here’s an.estimate of them:—
The Berlin Tagf.blatt: Democratic, Radical,
friend of Jews. Strongly anti-militarist before the
war, and its editor, Wolff, still goes as far that way
as he dares. Vossische Zeitung: Editor, Georg.
Proprietors, Ullstein brothers, five young men, own-
ing newspapers and periodicals. Very old paper;
dignified in peace, but furiously Anglomaniac in
war. Cologne Gazette : Semi-official, entirely under
Government’s thumb. Frankfurter Zeitung: Owned
by Simon brothers. Like the Vossische, in former
moderation and present fury. Chief financial organ.
Hamburger Nachrichten: A thorn to the Kaiser
befove the war, but driven by the sentiment of its
city to “Gott strafe England.” Harden’s Zukcnft
(future) : By many degrees smartest of all papers.
Suppressed lately. Lokal-Anzeiger, Berlin; Once
the popular halfpenny paper under Scherl, the great
journalist who founded it. He sold out to the

PULP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Anyhow, it seems a poor exchange to take a noble
forest and turn it into the modern newspaper.
Sydney Evening News.
Prismatic in the noontide, glinting fire
When the low sun burns down the western ways,
The leafy places full of green desire
Are as a harp on which the soft wind plays.
Tiie world with all its wheels, remote and far,
Grinds out a noisy music of no tune,
While peeping through these boughs a happy star
Laughs at the capers of a cloud-chased moon.
Here comes the Sibyl Autumn, wise with dream,
And writes her yellow magic on a leaf,
An old, clear message as of lights that gleam
Far from the dusty ways of unbelief.
And here erect, unflinching through the year,
The new bud bursts its sheath, unheard, unseen,
And flaunts against the winter’s icy spear
Its full and happy rain-washed evergreen.
Come hither with the axe. These limbs must fall.
Out on the fays that dance within the shade!
Death to the mating birds that pipe and call 1
There is no progress in this arching glade.
Those tangled roots must feed the famished pen
That tells to all the market price of lard ;
This gnarled trunk must shout aloud to men
The Labor triumph in the Smellville Ward.
This silver-coated bole that lightly sways
To high winds piping under skies of blue
Must advertise the latest thing in stays
Or virtues of a freak imported shoe.
Look up ! . The branches meeting overhead
Are like the roof of some cathedral aisle—
Beat them to pulp! On them shall yet be read
The gloating record of a murder vile!
Yes, pulp the lot! Right-thinking people wait
To learn at breakfast if the time is ripe ;
To groan at spites that still beset the State
And soak their fatted intellects in tripe.
Progress is great! We bow before her shrine.
We offer incense of our toil and sweat.
A grime of inky words on pulp of pine
Replaces those green shades the birds forget.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

From an Australian doctor in Flanders :
\t war? Well, you wouldn’t have thought so if
vou had seen us sitting round the fire this afternoon
in our billets eating delicious kippers we had just
grilled. Later on we had a first-class dinner, punc-
tuated with whisky and topped off with coffee, liqueur,
brandy and cigars while the gramophone reeled off
“Where My Caravan Has Rested.” “What price the
horrors of war?” scoffed one of the happy circle.
And just as the laugh died away someone entered
with news of the padre who had made us roar the
previous afternoon with the tale of his capture by
Boxers and how he ransomed himself, dirt cheap, for
a fiver. “What’s that?” called the man in the
corner. The messenger turned his way and gravely
repeated his message. “He had the top of his head
blown off this afternoon while reading the burial ser-
vice over some chap.” I walked to the door and
looked out over a world of mud and ruins lit by the
flares of the unresting enemy. The crashes of his
artillery that I had temporarily forgotten seemed to
have a new note of triumph. We were at war all
right.
* * * *
The youthful warriors whose job is to
remove refuse from Melbourne city battle
streets had their little “strike” last week.
They demanded a rise of wages from 21s.
to 255. The Pickers-up Brigade marched
through town some 50 strong, and upset a
Dago fruit barrow in Queen-street. After
which they sang “Rule Britannia” and bom-
barded one another with peaches and stock-
in-trade. When the secretary of the Muni-
cipal Employees’ Union urged the army to
resume discipline, their leader, a major-
general of about 15, exclaimed: “Wif all
these motor ’ogs abowt a man cleaning the
roads takes ’is life in ’is ’ands, and it’s worf
25 bob a week.” Which suggests the advisa-
bility of meeting the cost of a rise in the
wages of the Pickers-up Union by a special
tax on the road-hogs of Melbourne.
* * * *
A royal commission on North Terrace
reserves in Adelaide has been rather unex-
pectedly justifying its existence by consider-
ing the larger question of railway extension
—which is not at all before the public at
present, but will have to be considered by
posterity. North Terrace is the mile-long
top of a natural bank. Between it and the
river is a flat, not yet much used as to the
eastern half; but if the present North
Terrace railway station is ever to leave off
being a dead-end, and puts out a feeler to
the eastern suburbs —which at present have
no railways at all —along that fiat it must
go. Just how the Botanic Gardens and park
will then be affected is worth considering by
anyone who thinks for the day-after-to-mor-
,l'ow. Also, on the “bank” already mentioned
are the old barracks, the Destitute Asylum
and Government House. The first two are,
at great expense, being transferred to far
more suitable sites outside the city, and it is
not unlikely that future Governors will be
sent to a benevolent home in the suburbs,
too. Any spaces thus left vacant are likely

is approached, a long-sighted Commission has
a chance of doing some really good work.

“Te Babarantiko” tells a somewhat mixed
yarn :
The Captain Brown of “Masarcus” (B. 24/2/’l6)
is evidently the James Brown of the Cocos Island
treasure-hunting expedition which left ’Frisco in
July, 1903, to recover £10,000,000 or 10s. which
had been buried by pirates on the ocean speck.
Brown is supposed to have joined a treasure-hunting
schooner at Jamaica when he was 19 ; he is about 86
now. Having found the treasure,
the crew became more dissatisfied
than ever. They turned pirates
(all except Brown) and took
three “Australian and Californian
specie ships,” so adding another
£2,000,000 to the cash-till. Then
they got the burying habit again
on another island. In the process
nearly all hands were obliterated,
and the ship was fired and
beached. Brown and two others
escaped in the long boat with a
trifle of £60,000, and headed for
Australia. But only Brown came
out of the boat alive. An ordi-
nary person would have become a
shipowner or a grocer in a small
way and gradually turned the
buried treasure into gilt-edged
securities. But not Brown. He
had the burying instinct too
strongly developed, and after
sinking his fortune underground
again he went away and worked
in the mines. Finally he faded
to the U.S.A., where a lot of
long-shore pirates took his small
cash and tobacco-pouch. That
made him fall back on the
buried gold, and he floated an ex-
pedition to go and look for it,
he being guaranteed a quarter of
the returns. The expedition left
for Honolulu in the schooner Her-
man, and on arrival was held for
debt. The bo’sn had to go back
to ’Frisco to raise the wind. Then
Brown became remorseful, and re-
fused to sail for the treasure
island. He said there was a curse
on the treasure, and that the
ghosts of dead men were gloating
over it. He wouldn’t even give
the position of the island to the others and let
them risk the ghosts; so the expeditionaries
could only use unpleasant language and go home.
And now old Brown is at it again!
’Midst the horrors and excitement of war
news Melbourne has found time for a fugi-
tive interest in one of those good, old-
fashioned murders
where the motive is a
matter for speculation
and the identity of the
murderer so clueless
that you never know
whether they’ve got
the right man until
after he’s hanged. Or
so it seems at time of
writing. On the morn-
ing of February 2S a
Melbourne taxi co. was
rung up by a man or
woman or somebody,
who ordered a car to
be at Clivedon Man-
sions at eight o’clock
next morning. The
voice gave the name of
L’Estrange, but at
Clivedon Mansions’ ex-
pensive “flats” nobody
named L’Estrange
abides, nor had any-
body ordered a car.
Three hours later the
vehicle was found
rooted on the road to
Templestowe, about a
mile from the terminus
of the Kew electric
tram, and the dead
body of the chauffeur
was lying under a rug.
The corpse had four
bullets in its head and
neck. A sandbag lay
on the running board,
but apparently it had
not been used. There
had been no robbery,
and there was no evi-
dence of the person-
ality of the “fare.”
All was quiet along the Potomac.

“It.Y.T.”: What a backward crowd these
Germans are with their poor little 17-inch
howitzers! In 1534, or thereabout, Henry
VIII. had 19-inch shells flying around South-
ampton Water. This is what Stow says
about it in his ancient chronicle :
One Peter Band, a Frenchman born, and another
alien called Peter Van Collen, a gunsmith, both the
King’s feed men, conferring together, devised and
caused to be made certain mortar pieces, being at
the mouth from 11 inches unto 19 inches wide, for
the use whereof they also caused to be made certain
hollow shot of cast-iron, to be stuffed with fire-
work or wild fire; whereof the bigger sort for the
same had screws of iron to receive a match to carry
fire kindled, that the firework might be set on fire
for to break in pieces the same hollow shot, whereof
the smallest piece hitting any man would kill or
spoil him.
“Briner” turns on the historical tap :
Limelight has been focussed on the Cocos or Keel-
ing Isles ever since the Sydney smashed the Emden.
They were discovered 300 years ago by one Keeling.
The name Cocos comes from the profitable cocoanut. No
use was made of the isles until nearly-100 years ago.
One J. C. Ross, who started as whalers’ boy, became
harpooner, and ultimately rose to possess a ship of
his own. In Java, 1825, he met Alexander Hare,
who also owned a ship. They agreed to settle on the
Cocos Islands. Ross went first with his party and settled
on Selma Island. Hare then came and made his home
on Bras Island. His party included a number of
brown women, and the presence of the ladies caused
a disagreement, Ross being a strict Presbyterian who
ultimately imported his wife and family. There are
about two dozen islands of assorted sizes, the biggest
being a strip six miles by half a mile. The Emden
rusts on Keeling. After nine years’ occupancy of
Bras, Hare was driven away to Singapore by Neh
Basir, a Malay, offspring of a couple of slaves given
to Hare by the Rajah of Banjer. After this Ross was
recognised as general ruler of the Cocos. Vessels
called there more frequently and included some of
the quasi-buccaneer sort. Ross died in 1854, suc-
ceeded by his son, J.G., who married a Malay prin-
cess. They had a family of seve i boys and two girls.
His ruling son known as Ross 111. died in 1910. At
present there is Ross IV., Governor under England,
which annexed the specks in 1857.

TO DORA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Just a hairpin on the floor,
With a strand of yellow hair
Twined about it—nothing more ;
But l had not seen it there !
Why, my Dora, will you use
Those frail hairpins, thin and long—
Most calamitous of clues?
Maud’s are thicker, short and strong 1
From the mirror when you left
Powder specks I flicked awajq
And arranged in manner deft
Ev’ry sign of disarray,
Looked with care the room about,
Thought that all was as before,
But our sin has found us out—•
Just a hairpin on the floor!
VICTORIA.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
“F.H.”: Sky-pilot Lee, of the Goulburn
Valley, speaking recently at a Merrigum
(Vic.) recruiting-meeting, declared that he
was being boycotted for his efforts to shake
up the recruiting barometer. But the boy-
cott isn’t cooling him off at all. At Merri-
gum the other night he unwound this:—
In one shire 750 men said they couldn’t go because
they were growing wheat for the soldiers. They
lied, and they know that they lied. They were not
growing wheat for soldiers, but to line their own
pockets. Farmers have cheap labor in their sons.
Merrigum, where these elevating remarks
were made, lies among the rolling western
farm-lands of the wealthy Valley. Parson
Lee advocates conscription; and conscrip-
tion certainly would, for one thing, do away
with the persecution of patriotic critics.
KNEW HIS LIMITATIONS.
CANDID FRIEND: “Well, old man, honestly
I think it’s ROTTEN.”
ARTIST: “ Indeed! What can you know
about pictures? You never painted one."
CANDID FRIEND: “True; but I know a had
egg when I meet it: and, believe me, old
man, I’ve never laid one."
Under-secretary L. 11. Sholl, retires from
S. Aus. Public Service in June after 58
years, during which time he saw a long pro-
cession of Chief Secretaries move on. When
he drops out on long leave the Chief Secre-
tary’s Department will be reformed and a
Premier’s Department created, with Jack
Sincock as secretary to the chief. Harry
Blinman, of cricketing fame, will probably
be under-secretary in Sholl’s place. Conse-
quent changes will occur amongst the staff
at Parliament House. These are already
to some extent foreshadowed by the appoint-
ment of Fred Johns (leader of the Hansard
staff’) as secretary to Royal Commissions,
and of Rodney Cockburn (deputy Hansard
leader) as Secretary of the Parliamentary
Committee on Railways.
CORRECT.
“Where does a bloke ’are ter go ter get a
job, Mister?"
“Victoria Barracks."
“Vic”: Curates and so forth are boiling
oyer the announcement that the distant Arch-
bishop of Canterbury Jias been invited to
recommend a Bishop of Ballarat. It is a
miserable and stupid act of snobbishness when
it is remembered that Bishop Green, who
retires, was trained in Melbourne’s Trinity
College, and was the first of the clerical
battle-line which was continued with Arm-
strong, of Wangaratta; Stretch, of New-
castle ; Long, of Bathurst; Sadleir, of Maori-
land ; and Stephen, of Hobart. If any bright
young fellows think of entering at Trinity,
let them pause and think hard. What sort
of Judges would we get if they were im-
ported from England? There is never any
talk of getting an Englishman for a “hard
shop.” But as soon as there’s a prospect of
some ease and good pickings, the foreigner
arrives with his carpet bag filled with holv
willingness to sacrifice himself for the moral
uplifting of the “colonials.” Bishop Long,
of Bathurst, is the man I size up for Bal-
larat.
The estate of Barr Smith, Adelaide’s
veteran merchant, squatter and philanthro-
pist, sworn for probate at a trifle over 11-
million, is the largest South Australia has
seen since the old days of the copper mag-
nates. J. H. Angas left about £750.000.
John Darling had gross assets of two
millions, but. a net estate of one. Barr
Smith’s legacies to a variety of charities total
£40,000.
Judges Murray (on moving up to the Chief
Justiceship) and Buchanan (on getting a
permanent instead of a temporary appoint-
ment as Judge) have been formally congratu-
lated by the South Australian Bar, with the
youthful Attorney-General, “Jim” Vaughan,
as spokesman. Murray, in responding, had
a kindly remembrance for old John Downer,
lately dead, who led the congratulations when
Murray was first made a Judge, and who
should —like Josiah Symon—himself have
been one if Way, Boucaut and Bundey had
not kept up a record partnerships in the
three judgeships which are all S.A. seems to
need. Nobody, however, said anything
about Vaughan’s own action in not grabbing,
as Attorney-General, the first judgeship that
became vacant. That kind of political
decency is almost expected nowadays; but
any time up to 10 years or so back it would
have caused general astonishment and a
hurried visit from the family doctor'.
A real veteran of railways was H. C.
Mais, just dead in Melbourne at 88. He
was “articled” to one of the great Brunei’s
engineers over 70 years ago, when the steel
lines were beginning to creep all over
England. In the ’so’s he was engineer to
the Sydney Railway Co., and in the ’6o’s to
the Melbourne and Suburban Co. Then the
wise principle of State ownership came in,
and he had 20 years as engineer-in-chief to
the South Australian Government. Since
that he had been, till his health broke down,
a consulting engineer in Melbourne, with a
special gift for acting as arbitrator in rail-
way disputes.
Having introduced himself to the world on
February 29, High Court Judge Duffy gets
a birthday once in four years. He was 64
last week. His colleague, Judge Powers, was
63 on March 3.
“H.”: The most distinguished of all
women journalists has just died in England.
She wrote a lot of European history; but,
as she pathetically said herself, history writ-
ten thus is “like snow falling on water.”
Emily Crawford had lived in Paris since
1853, and she was grown up when she went
there. Her husband (by Thackeray’s own
showing, the original of the journalist War-
rington in “Pendennis”) was appointed by
Charles Dickens, first editor of London
Daily News, its correspondent in Paris.
His wife helped in the work, and carried it
on alone after he died in 1885. She wrote
regularly for New York Tribune, and also
for Calcutta Englishman, Chicago and San
Francisco papers, and especially for London
Truth. Labouchere, representing the Daily
News, got to Paris in 1870, just in time to
send the Crawfords away and get shut in for
the siege himself. He gave the lady a free
hand when he started his own paper. She
wrote for the first number of Truth, in
1877, and she had an article in the journal
the week she died. For nearly half a cen-
tury, from Victor Hugo onwards, she knew
personally, or knew all about, nearly every
prominent person in Europe. She was
probably the only Briton to be twice — 1870
and 1914- —driven from Paris by the German
advance. The one good point she could see
about the present war is that it will stop
the marrying of German princelings and prin-
cesses into the English royal family.
Truth, giving Mrs. Crawford six columns of
obituary notice, admits the suggestion once
made about her: “She presumes on the privi-
lege of her sex; if she had been a man she
would have been horsewhipped a dozen
times.” But she must have been a sound
journalist; in 39 years of reckless personali-
ties she only once landed the paper in a
libel action—and that case was dismissed.
The S. Aus. Government will have a num-
ber of public-service billets to fill shortly,
chiefly on account of officers retiring at tne
age-limit. Alf Day, secretary to the Rail-
ways Commissioner, and Goodman, general
manager of the Tramways, are freely backed
for the Commissionership. For the
Police Commissionership Major Smeaton,
INLP., Sergeant-Detective Fraser and Colonel
Weir are in the running.

G. G. H. IRVING.
City Solicitor Dawson, Sydney, has filed
Ins pleas in the Court of Final Appeal. Ilis
father. Robert Dawson, came to NSW
with Governor Gipps in January, 1888, and
7 en '?• station in Argyle as a jackeroo.
in Ins father arrived and purchased a
run' in the Monaro, and young Dawson under-
took the management. The big pastoral
slump in the ’forties settled the business
when in ’47 the Government established
a Court of Petty Sessions at Cooma young
Dawson accepted the position of C.P.S. at
£lOO a year. In ’57 he was promoted to be
Police Magistrate, and retired in 1887 after
40 years’ service at the one court. This
must be nearly a record. Just-dead Sydney
Dawson was born at Cooma in ’64, educated
at Newington, articled to G. C. K. Waldron,
and when the latter died in February 1)9
succeeded him as City Solicitof.
Adelaide Botanic Gardens will shortly
have a new director, Dr. Holtze being close
up to the retiring line. W. Draper, F.L.S.
at present director of the Government Gar-
dens at Delta Barrage, Egypt, is mentioned
for the billet. Of course, nobody in Australia
has the necessary qualifications.
The sons of Canon Grime, of the
Anglican Cathedral at Newcastle (N.S.W.).
are doing their best to hold Australia’s end
up in the present war. One was in the land-
ing at Anzac, and, after losing an eye
through a splinter of Turkish shrapnel, is
now in England. A second, who is a lieu-
tenant in the Australian artillery, is in hospi-
tal in Egypt. A third holds a commission
in an English cavalry regiment, and is at
present at Bisley (Eng.), teaching Kitch-
ener’s new army some wrinkles about
machine-guns; and a fourth is in camp at
Newcastle, preparing to move on to the
interminable Front.
Chaplain Bickersteth, who lately left his
billet at Melbourne Grammar School to
enlist in England, has duly arrived in his
native land. lie took with him a singular
commission. An old pupil offered the
Grammar School £lOO for a new chapel-bell.
After searching Australia for five minutes
and finding no local article that made the
right kind of noise, the. school authorities
asked Bickersteth to buy a bell for them in
the dear old Motherland, and in the intervals
of Salisbury Plaining the earnest young
cleric is at work, ear down on the trail,
seeking the correct sound in the suitable
size and shape. The Bulletin regrets that
Bickersteth got away before it could have
a breathless word with him. Martha
Washington, who cleans the office and hangs
it out to dry, complains that if some clothes
pegs are not got from England the paper
will have to be tied on the line with flax.
Canon John Cue, of Holy Trinity
Cathedral, Wangaratta (Vic.), is about to
do his bit. Tie is packing his appliances as
an Anglican chaplain and will shortly move
on to war with an Australian force. The
rectory will be kept warm for him in his
absence.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The bugle called. They went away.
They fought, they died, they won to fame.
They saw the Turkish lightnings play ;
Their hands were strong to smite and slay,
And honor loved their name.
We praised their deeds at breakfast time,
We praised them in the afternoon,
We praised them at the midnight’s chime,
We praised them- both in beer and rhyme
Beneath the sun and moon.
We called them us, and said that we
Had faced the shot and shrieking shell,
That we had wrought for all to see
Deeds that must live in history
And pubs and trams as well.
We praised us loud at break of day,
We praised us when the sun had set,
We praised us when we knelt to pray
In solemn bars where none might stay,
We praised us dry and wet.
The bugle called. The blood was raw.
We cursed the foeman’s craven heart.
Tremendous glory we foresaw,
And mentioned with elastic jaw
Our great Australian part.
The wagging of our tireless tongue
Was set to one eternal string.
We boasted as when earth was young,
When beer-soaked harpers nightly flung
Their soap-wreaths round the king.
The years went by; the ages grew;
The war was shut in history’s bag.
The world forgot what we could do,
And future epochs only knew
The Great Australian Brag.
N.S.W.

In view of the much higher costs in the
coast service and the drought on the North-
ern rivers, the co. has done remarkably well
to have kept up the 9 p.c. rate without cut-
ting into reserves. Further, however profit-
able it has been to most shipping concerns,
the war can’t have benefited this one much ;
on the contrary, by putting up the price of
most stores it has probably cost the co.
money. With a shipping co., however, the
visible reserves are not much to go by, as a
rule: you want to know how much is struck
off profits to cover wear and tear. It can’t
be told for certain in this case; but this may
suggest something. As at Dec., 1913, the
surplus of liquid and semi-liquid assets over
liabilities w T as £81,298, and now it is
£103,245. That shows a nice accumulation
iu two years in spite of the higher dividend.
But that must not be regarded as all profit
in excess of the dividend. Possibly a bit of it
may be the proceeds of sales ; and a lot of
it may be called for presently to make good
the two years’ depreciation of steamers.
Nevertheless, giving all that in, the figures
indicate pretty clearly that, in spite of
adverse conditions, the co. has been holding
its end up very well.
The next comparison makes a 10 years’
survey:—•

There have been big changes in those 10
years—and there will probably be as big in
the next 10. Some will certainly be to the
co. s advantage. The chances are that even
the railway development on the North Coast
will do it good. The rail is bound to create
settlement; and that and a reasonable
amount of. attention to northern harbors
is all this co. wants. In any case
land settlement is likely to flourish
during the next few years. After the
current boom the cities are going to feel
sick, and whenever that happens people get
virtuous and discover that we want more
producers and fewer parasites. And that
is the beginning of all political wisdom.

Somewhere in the middle of Java there is
the palace of Raden Mas Somethingorother
Itab. Once Raden Mas led the writer of
these sermons into an upper room—a miser-
able little dog-box of a place in the midst
ot much garish splendor. “Every day I
come up here to think a while on how some
others live who are less fortunate than I,”
this old Javanese potentate explained. There
is an upper room at The Bulletin office
full of old records; and once a month at
least Willy Holman should go there to read
such things as this:—
Shareholders will be glad to observe the continued
growth of deposits. These with debentures and accrued
interest now amount to £1,390,281, and our own
capital is £214,863. The investment of this money
in satisfactory securities has required care, and your
directors have pursued a rigorously cautious policy.
The directors recommend the usual dividend at the
rate of 124 p.c., with a bonus of 14 p.c. Their
Policy has been to get plenty of funds in hand to
strengthen the reserves, so shareholders would, he was
sure, be satisfied with 14 p.c.
That was how the chairman of this Aus-
tralian Deposit and Mortgage Bank apolo-
gised in 1891 for paying only 14 p.c. Just
the same things w r ere said by the boomsters
of those days as one hears from the boom-
sters of to-day. But luckily there is a dif-
ference. The boomsters of to-day are not
the banks: they are—of all people in the
world! —Labor members. Not one in a
hundred of them has had any training in
finance, or knows anything of financial his-
tory. That is why they ought to be taken
up find shown first the speeches of men say-
ing just the same things as the Holmans
and Scaddans are saying to-day, and then
the report of the smash, and of the subse-
quent long squeezing-out of calls, and of
the final reconstruction, when ordinary
shares were reduced from £5 to Is. And
they ought to inquire who got the other
995. In 999 cases out of 1000 it was the
cunning speculator, the parasite. He got
the_ hard earnings of thousands of poor sub-
division buyers, and thousands of moderately
poor shareholders in mortgage cos. He is
the person who always benefits by a boom.
To-day we have the awful spectacle of the
Labor party madly pursuing a policy which
will fleece the worker and fatten the syndi-
cate and the speculator.
Certainly the writing down from £5 to Is.
was a trifle too severe; it might have been
sufficient if it had been from £5 to 3s. As
it is, a special reserve has been accumulated
out of the surpluses on sales, which reserve
is for the benefit of the ordinary and
deferred shareholders. This shows the pro-
gress : —•

Meantime, while the business has been grow-
ing in this extraordinary manner, no capital
has come in from shareholders. In fact,
some has actually been forfeited, and the
total is now only £1462. Thus for every
£1 of share capital the eo. has over £25
of reserves. There has hitherto been some
doubt as to the real value of those reserves.
But this year’s accounts, etc., make things
a bit plainer. The directors say that “the
usual amount has been written off for depre-
ciation” ; yet the reserves have not in-
creased, which shows that they are not
merely an accumulation of amounts written
off against wear and tear. It looks as if
the main purpose of the co. is to market
the shareholders’ milk, etc., to the best ad-
vantage, direct profits from the shares being
quite a secondary matter. That being so,
the manner in which profit has been hoarded
is admirable; and in that respect the co.
is a striking example for Australian co-
operative concerns, which usually hamstring
their managements by their greed for imme-
diate and direct profits.

This remarkably profitable concern paid a
half-crown bonus in October; but the present
accounts don’t mention it, and the previous
directors’ report didn’t hint that it was com-
nig. Attention might be given to this
matter when a new Companies Act is under
consideration. On the published accounts it
merely appears that £9247 has dropped out
?*. two reserve funds, no indication at all
being given of what has become of the
money. The table accounts for £7500; this
paper doesn’t know what happened to the
rest, nor does any shareholder who may be
unable to get inside information. It hasn’t
apparently gone merely in writing-down
assets: it is hard cash that has disappeared.
Here is a comparison :

over 40 years old it is only during
the past 10 or 15 that the Vale of Clwydd
has done any good at all. Till then there
had been excessive competition, which Wil-
ton at last ended when he formed the Lith-
gow Coal Association to handle the output
of this and other Western collieries. That,
the extension of railways in the West and
the establishment of industries at Lithgow
and thereabouts gave the co. a new outlook
on life; and the very small capital with
which the poor little thing was originally
content has allowed of some fine fat bonuses.
Two or three years back the co. launched out
also as a brickmaker. But it isn’t going to
say much about that. Twelve months ago it
credited P. & L. with £BBl on brick factory
account, the next half-year £995, the past
half-year £217. It doesn’t look much on a
capital outlay of over £12,000. Perhaps it
isn’t intended to look much. Still the State
yards have thrown a bomb into a very
pleasant little family party, and in the dull
time that is coming in the building trade
this £12, 000-odd isn’t likely to earn big
money. Already there is more than one idle
brickworks between Sydney and Lithgow.

The co. depends almost wholly on the Mel-
bourne Beer Combine, which isn’t a Combine
in the ordinary sense of the word. The
Carlton and United Breweries, Ltd., really
bought all these Melbourne cos. out, and this
Shamrock and similar concerns are now
investment cos. which couldn’t brew beer
even if they wanted to. True, this co.’s
brewery and malt house at Abbotsford were
not wanted and were accordingly not taken
over; but the rolling stock has gone and the
longer the premises remain unused the more
impossible will it be for the so-called com-
bine to break up, as ordinary combines have
a habit of doing. Still, though the old cut-
throat competition is thus gone for good,
new competition may possibly push in.
There is a little already—from the Co-opera-
tive Co. Apart from that, however, the
chances of the “combine” being hit by com-
petition are not serious, because the steady
movement against the liquor business is
likely to shoo money away from it. The
bigger trouble may come from the restraints
put upon the pubs. For the second half of
the past year the “combine,” which had got
into the habit of paying 8 p.c., pulled down
its dividend to 6 p.c. for ordinaries and 7 p.c.
tor prefs., so apparently it has been feeling
the joint effects of drought and nine o’clock
closing. And it is dead certain that we
uaven’t yet reached the end of the changes
in liquor legislation. Still, this and other cos.
m the business have one great advantage
—they have been given plenty of warning.
Luring the past year, for instance, one of
this co. s freehold pubs was closed under the
Victorian Licenses Induction law; yet there
has been no loss, except in regard to the
facilities for future trade. “The property
has been sold and the amount realised
together with the compensation awarded by
the Reduction Board, just about equals the
amount standing against the property in the
co. s books.” The co. hasn’t much left in
f f ct J ap , art fron ? the old brewery
tilb,UyU), it hasn t much in anything except
i ar T v • shares - Their value depends
almost wholly upon their earnings; that is
to say, the C. and U. B. capital is to only a
small extent represented by tangible assets.

In each of the past two terms £15,000 has
been cut out of gross profits to provide a
sinking fund for the new steel debenture
issue. This amount is not included in the
table. But a small matter of £15,000 is
neither here nor there in the accounts of a
co. like this. Nobody knows to within a few
thousands what profit really was made. For
instance, among the assets are “Iron and
steel products on hand, £127,001,’’ and “Ore
concentrates, etc., on hand, £153,036.” That
presumably, is the estimated value of the
co.’s very large stocks in excess of the ad-
vances drawn against them. But the est,-
mated excess is largely a matter of how
somebody was feeling. Lead is quoted now
at about £33 a ton. If the man or men who
had the settling of the balance-sheet were
feeling very well, they might have reckoned
on getting £3O per ton for the stuff on which
final settlements had not been made. On the
other hand, they might have reckoned o n
getting no more than £22. That difference m
the estimate would probably make a dilig-
ence of £30,000 or £40.000 in the profits, bo
the figures for any one half-year are not oi
very much consequence.

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.— Advt.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd,
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.— Advt. [2

establishment of iron and steel works, and

total book value of plant and machinery is
£1653 lt>o. of which the iron and steel sec-
tion now represents £1,448,500. That shows
how completely the complexion of the co. has
changed during the last year or two. About
all the co. has left of its old assets (apart
from the rapidly wasting mine) is its name
and its organisation; and its organisation
has undergone many changes. John Darling
is dead and Muecke, who succeeded him as
chairman, has dropped out; H. V. McKay,
the harvester man, has brought in quite new
blood ; David Baker has come from America
to manage the steel works. G. D. Delprat
continues, of course, to be the plotter-in-
chief ; and his mana is great. Still even Del-
prat can miscalculate. “At first. ’ he says,
was expected that three open-hearth fur-
naces would be able to deal with the entire
production of pig-iron. This, however, has
proved not to be the case, and it was decided
to add another four open-hearth furnaces.”
Seven furnaces to do the work that it was
thought would be done by three! Still. Aus-
tralia simply must have iron and steel
works; and if we can’t run them success-
folly under present conditions, then we must
alter the conditions. But it is quite possi-
ble that this co. will have varying fortunes
before that fact is realised. Meantime, for
perhaps six or eight years, it is likely to get
very material help from the remnant of the
old mine. » * *

As at Dec. 31 the co. had on hand and in
suspense metals and concentrates valued at
£96,978; and in view of recent high
prices the chances are that something more
than that will be got on final settlement.
War profits have fattened the figures during
the half-year. In Britain the Government is
casting an Arctic eye on these profits, and
especially on profits made out of metals ; and
if the war lasts much longer something sud-
den may happen on both sides of the water.
This co.’s profits for the half-year are more
than twice as great as the average for the
previous seven years ; and if three people out
of four were not making more money now
than they do under normal conditions, such
a result would be regarded as simply intoler-
able. Of course the reckoning will come
by-and-bye. We are simply dipping into our
future earnings to pay these high prices. In
the days to come the Broken Hill cos.
likely to suffer with the rest. Hence the
extra profit they are making isn’t all bunce.
But if the past year’s figures are not much
to go by, a line may perhaps be got from
those of the past seven. Here they are in
the rough

That is about 10s. 6d. per ton of ore. In
addition to ore the co. has beeii getting rid
of its accumulated tailings, and there was
good profit in that until the war stopped the
export of zinc concentrate to Belgium,
where most of it was smelted; but other
things are coming along to take the place of
the old tailings dumps, so the profit made
in the past seven years may fairly be
assumed to be profit made from current ore.
And on that basis, as there are still
3,350,000 tons exposed, one can roughly
assume that there are £1,750,000 of dividends
in the mine. In addition there are more or
less liquid assets running to £001,594, or
£353,650 in excess of liabilities; also there is
an unknown quantity of ore in the mine
below the deepest point at which ore has been
opened out and measured. There are 200.000
shares in the co.; and the foregoing estima-
tion would give each of them an ultimate
value of well over £lO.
The manipulators of the copper market
have been checked to some extent by the
British Munitions Department, which has
earmarked the output from Australia —about
40,000 tons per annum—at a price equiva-
lent to £lOO for electrolytic. The arrange-
ment is to hold good while the war lasts.
* * * *
The oil-flotation process has revolutionised
low-grade copper-mining in America, where
plants to deal with millions of tons of ore
per annum—some as low as 1 p.c.-—are now
in operation, and it is likely that a similar
result will follow in Australia —practically
the home of the process. Mount Lyell has
installed an experimental plant capable of
dealing with 600 tons per week. Part of the
plant has been given a trial run, and the
official report states :—•
Satisfactory full scale trial runs were made on the
flotation plant on February 17 and 18. About 80
tons of ore, assaying 5.13 p.c. copper were treated
each day. Concentrates assayed 13.9 p.c., and tailings
0.47 p.c., the recovery being 94 p.c. The mill is
working splendidly.
The Fort Bourke and Chesney mines, which
belong to the Great Cobar Co., present excep-
tional opportunities for the use of the oil
flotation process on an extensive scale, but
in the present weak financial position of that
company it can hardly be expected that this
will be done. The salvation of the combined
mines lies in this direction. It is up to Pre-
mier Holman, representing the State as part-
owner, to get the advice of the Mines Depart-
ment experts and see that the proposed policy
of ore-extraction, which will mean the extinc-
tion of the main mine, is promptly deviated
from.
* * * *
Westralian Chamber of Mines is making
a noise about the shortage of labor at Kal-
goorlie, and says that if things do not im-
prove soon some of the mines will have to
close down. The Chamber attributes the
dearth of men to the refusal of unionists
to work with enemy subjects and the num-
ber of underground hands who have gone
to the war. It also complains that in addi-
tion to a shortage of capable miners there
is a slackness and slowing up on the part
of a number of employees, which almost
amounts to a lazy strike. Some of the
men are alleged to make a practice of
applying for work, going below, doing little
or nothing, cheerfully accepting the sack,
and next day giving an encore at another
mine. They have thus been able, by giving
all the shows a turn, to get a week or two’s
full pay without doing any work at all.
The labor shortage, the Chamber says, has
caused the aggregate output of the district
to fall 21,000 tons, the gold output £26,000,
and the wages total £6500 a month.
* * * *
From a Bulletin man :
Have just had a glimpse at the new development in
the Edna May Central. Some 160 ft. of driving east
and south and 40ft. between the two faces
has been done on a freaky, irregular shaped
body of ore with mullocky intrusions, and for widths
ranging up to 20ft. values are officially given as
17dwt., with ounce ore showing in the east end.
This is the best part of the formation, and is pro-
bably the main make of stone. As a result of this
work and the driving of crosscuts from the two
levels below to cut the downward continuation of
the shoot, and also on the strength of a bullish re-
port that the next clean-up is going to paralyse the
bears, the market valuation of the show has appreci-
ated to £ 150,000.
A look underground shows that for a long time the
mill has been mostly kept going with low-grade wash
from the 73ft. level, and before the new find was
made the mine was in rather an emaciated condi-
tion. For two years it had failed to realise the
profit its controllers predicted. Now its admirers
whoop that it is going to pay monthly divs. of Is.
They are optimists. As a result of the development
co. can at the outside count on about 15ft. of
“backs” and a further 70ft., or thereabouts, if the
shoot lives and maintains its size and value down to
the next level. This means that at the most 8000
tons will be available in the area of ground already
explored; and estimated on the official value of the
ore net profits will not exceed £20,000.
* * * *
Coolgardie (W.A.) is crowing over the fact
that good crushings are being obtained from
the ore raised and left behind by the Mel-
bourne co. which threw up the option on the
Bulla Bulling leases at Gibraltar. But the
show is only a squib, and would have broken
the back, and probably the heart, of the co.
had it stuck to it. Gibraltar is no place
for a co. to go.
* * * *
From Laverton (W.A.) : Ida H. has got
some rich ore and indefinitely prolonged its
life; Mary Mac’s bottom level is showing an
improvement; British Flag, which Peacock
and others optioned and turned down, has
got another patch of specimen stone. The
last lot yielded £2855, and that now being
taken out is expected to yield as much if not
more. Golden Spinifex’s flotation is said to
be assured. This show, as stated in a
recent par, was prospected some 10 years
ago, but after £7330 had been dug out, water
hampered operations and compelled the
owners to clear out.
Pioneer Tin, Bradshaw’s Creek (Tas.) :
Tin Won.
