Plentiful water enabled the co. to increase
its output and reduce costs in the past half-
year. If tin remains at present price dirt
should average Is. per lb., and with costs a
shade over 4d. there should be a comfort-
able margin for dividends. Cecil Ryan does
not state whether his barges are tackling
the upper drifts. If they are, the grade of
the wash should improve during the current
term; still a tributary is needed to restore
former values. In the co.’s Argonaut pro-
perty the wash is shallow in the creek bed,
and has run at slightly under lib. The
barge is now tackling the terrace ground and
finding it a tough proposition. Yet the
w r ash is deeper and the values better than
Ryan anticipated.
* * * *
Want of power is still the bugbear of the
Cassilis, Gippsland (Yic.). Now A. A.
Summerhayes, the new manager, proposes
to utilise the headwaters of the Cobungra
by means of a cheap dam, but he wants
State help in the work. As the co.’s slimes
plant is expected to show £2OO profits
monthly, a bit of nerve is required to ask
for assistance these times. Still the chances
are that Cassilis will get what it seeks.
What then? About 5000 tons stand in the
stopes to be beaten out from the Cassilis
and the Ceresa. Reefs for 300 ft. have been
driven on an 8-inch shoot at the No. 5 level
ou the Ceresa. The grade is about 15dwt.,
so there should be profit in it. The co.’s
game is to sink deeper to get additional
backs as well as to . crosscut the country
between the Ceresa and Cassilis shoots. The
old plans showed three or four parallel
makes of quartz. Summerhayes urges that
an old-fashioned prospector be put on the
job of searching for a fresh shoot of gold in
these formations.
* * * *
An air of indebtedness is about the Row-
lett North Woolamai coal-mine (Vic.). This
show was left by Peter Mcßride when Min-
ister of Mines to bask beside his State coal
mine. Its backers have found out that a
coal seam doesn’t necessarily mean imme-
diate profits. Capital expenditure last year
cut into nearly £15,000, of which the State
contributed £4500. This loan will have to
be repaid, as well as £3989 to sundry credi-
tors and £1730 for advances against coal.
The management, however, reckons it won’t
take long to get clear, especially if the
threatened increase in the price of coal is
made. But, even so, labor difficulties,
faulted country and narrow seams will con-
tinue to trouble the co.
* * * *
The Victoria United, Ballarat, is a matter
of more than sentimental interest to the
Government. Months back, the old co. hav-
ing given up the struggle, the Minister of
Mines undertook to keep the workings from
getting flooded while a new co. was being
formed. But the shares didn’t go off, and
the old show continued to cost £4O a week
for pumping—until a few days back, when
the Minister decided to let go.
* * * *
Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) reports that
there is ready for blocking out ground which
contains 270 tons of tin oxide. At pre-
sent price this would be worth about
£32,000; and all the metal should be won
within six months. The vendors of the mine
having been paid off, the directors anticipate
paying regular quarterly dividends.
* * * *.
The C.S.A., near Cobar (N.S.W.), sent
2440 tons of copper ore to the smelters last
year. Average assay was 14.7 p.c., worth
£ll 15s. with copper at £BO. The sale of
this ore realised £16,075. At the close of the
year the co. had £4986 in cash and £4438
owing for ore. Under present conditions a
substantial profit can be made by shipping
14 p.c. ore, but the co. can undoubtedly make
more by smelting locally, because it has a
big supply of basic fluxing material. These
considerations prompted the Board to pur-
chase a furnace. Altogether 4689 tons of
ore were raised in 12 months.
* * * *
Mount Bischoff (Tas.) has fallen from its
high estate, for, despite the high price of
tin, the past half-year did not show suffi-
cient profit to pay a dividend. Ore treated
was 60,188 tons, producing 1246 tons of
metallic tin. The North Valley flats have
been well tested and show a return equal
to 0.33 p.c. of tin. Ore reserves are esti-
mated at 1,665,000 tons, equal to 10 years’
life.
* * * *
The Bruni Island Petroleum Co. (Tas.)
is going ahead with its oil effort in spite of
Dr. Wade’s gloomy forecasts. G. R.
Andrew, who is running the show, has bought
a drilling-plant in Sydney, to be delivered
in a month’s time, and is getting a driller
from Timor. Meanwhile he is building a
«rdam and fixing up odds and ends.
* * * *
The Goobaragandra mine (Tumut,
N.S.W.) isn’t on Stinking Creek after all —
which is very right and proper, for it doesn’t
deserve to be. The reputable experts who
have reported so favorably on the
mine must be believed until the battery tells
its tale, and the shareholders, who have re-
frained from asking for money from the pub-
lic, deserve that the tale shall confirm the
experts’ opinions.

WARNING TO DYSPEPTICS.
Many interesting paragraphs have recently
appeared in the press concerning the remark-
able antacid properties possessed by Bisu-
rated Magnesia, and thousands of people now
regularly take half a teaspoonful of the pre-
paration in a little water after their meals
as a safeguard against indigestion, dyspepsia,
heartburn, flatulence, etc. It appears, how-
ever, that in several instances other forms
of magnesia, bearing somewhat similar
names, have been used, often with very
unpleasant results. To guard against dan-
gerous substitution and ensure getting the
genuine product as recommended by physi-
cians, readers should ask their chemist dis-
tinctly for Bisnrated Magnesia-, and insist
on being supplied in bottles having the full
name —Bisnrated Magnesia— blown in the
glass.—A dvt.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, March 6, 1916.
My Dear Arini, —
Colonel James Burns, head of the ship-
ping firm whose plaid funnels smoke in a
hundred nigger harbors, got off the Malwa
at Melbourne to have a crack with blither
Scot Munferguson about his nine months’
travels. ■ The Colonel went away via the
U.S.A., and at N’York was transferred from
a White Star liner (which wasn’t risking
the thrust of the torpedo fish just then) to
a Yankee ship with the sign of the Almighty
Dollar painted all over the sides and illumi-
nated at night by strong electrics.
In Europe he found everything different
from what it ever was before —so different
that women who had never fingered more
than seven-and-six a week in their peaceful
lives were getting a couple of pounds at
munition-making. Carrying the personal
assurance of the G.-G. that he was a re-
spectable old gentleman, he went to the War
Office and asked what about a look at the
Fronts in France and Belgium. Everything
was going swimmingly when sombeody re-
membered that civilians were for the moment
absolutely barred. Then the shipping man
recalled that he wasn’t a civilian, but a
colonel of the old Parramatta lancers. He
had only 24 hours to get the wardrobe to
tit his new-old part, but the Army and Navy
Stores managed it, and Colonel Burns
joined 1200 other officers and men and left
Folkestone on a troopship whose deck was
invisible for khaki and lifebelts—one of the
latter making a pouter pigeon of everybody
on board on account of the recent suspici-
ous behavior of a vessel of a sort which
might lay mines, said vessel, by the way,
flying one of the “neutral” flags.
The Colonel left behind him in London
two daughters, who spend most of their days
in bucking up invalided Kangaroos. He had
the good luck to meet his two boys when
the Malwa called at Egypt. Both of them
enlisted as full privates, but one of them
earned a commission at Anzac and put in a
spell as A.D.C. to that General Walker
whom Ashmead Bartlett quoted as authority
for so many things.
Charles Mackellar, M.L.C., is seriously ill
in the. big house that overlooks Rose Bay’s
mosquito fleet. He is the veteran champion
of the State child, and a citizen N. S. Wales
needs to encourage back to health.
A Sydney man, Flight-Lieutenant Cecil
Brinsmead, R.N., was killed in action a few
weeks ago. Young Brinsmead, only 22 when
the end came, used to be in the mercantile
marine. Then he soared in the Royal Navy’s
section of the aviation service.
T. G. Russell, a Christchurch (M.L.) mag-
nate, stayed here just long enough to hear
about the six o’clock beer storm. The
affluent Russell has a Governor’s screw,
without the dreadful consequences of hav-
ing to free feed several thousand people all
the year round. The M.L. man hurried on
to Hobart to meet a married daughter. She
is an anxious grass widow with a husband in
the firing-line.
Nina Edmond, eldest daughter of The
Bulletin’s late and beloved chief, steps
up to the altar on March 14. The lucky
man is Captain A. C. Moyes.
The Verbrugghen family lias made un its
mmd to settle in leafy Wahroonga. When
the pleasant Mrs. Henri has been there a
little time she’ll hear a discreet whisper that
‘ colonial” is properly only applied to an
inhabitant of Fiji or Greenland.
Medlow has been energetically conducting
a series of patriotic gatherings. The hills
give a zest to woman’s insatiable desire to
do something for the great Cause, even if
it s only to click together a lumpy-toed sock
A war-fund party to be “addressed” by
Senator Gould is the latest evidence of what
our sex will do for the Empire. Incidentally
Mrs. Vivers is getting back her roses on the
Blue Mountains.
Douglas Reid, eldest son of Australia’s
late I ie Commissioner, is practising khaki.
He hopes to experience its serious side with
the R.F.A.
David Low’s virile black-and-white show
at Anthony Horderns’ Gallery has two or
three particular specimens of his work to
encourage art burg Jars. One is a drawing
of C. J. Dennis (“The Sentimental Bloke”)
walking on a carpet of clouds. I under-
stand this is all the footing poets require on
the road to Olympus.
The rain pattered down on the weddin<>-
day of Dr. Roger Hughes and Eileen Maher*
but it didn’t damp tbe festive air of the
dressy event. The bride’s frock was
elaborately simple. A Honiton veil that
had graced the altar years ago upon
mother fell like a snowflake over a
foam of white cr§pe de chine and lace. The
’groom gave his Eileen a diamond necklet
and pendant which glittered amongst the
fluffy finery of the dainty corsage. Chief
bridesmaid Marjory Allison, of Coomoo-
Coomoo station, was frocked in peep-o’-day
pink silk, veiled in cloudy white drapery.
The Tom Hughes’s parental abode, Cran-
brook Cottage, was lent for the bridal feast.
R. L. Baker has had to run up the white
flag and declare the beauty competition
“off.” The difficulty of getting judges that
he considered equal to the occasion dis-
heartened him. One hundred and thirteen
girls who had, in four days, decided to take
their chance were disappointed.
At the Stadium on Friday evening, March
10, soldiers, are going to show us what they
can do in the way of trimming millinery.
Fanny Durack is billed to give a thrill on
the same night. The champion lady fish
will leap into a specially-built tank. This
entertainment is the preliminary to the out-
door carnival (March 11) to rally the Light
Ilorse Comforts’ Fund.
Dr. Mary Booth, one of the vice-presidents
of the Voluntary Workers’ Association,
handed round light refreshments the other
noon to the energetic women who are lending
their aid to the men at French’s Forest.
Journalist Harry Gullett comes this way
on a business trip. Since he left us he’s
acquired a wife, and a perambulator to give
Gullett junior an airing. The family
accompanies dad, who is booked for a series
of lectures on the war in Europe. First
lecture, Town Hall, March 11.
Amongst many enthusiastic Australian
war-workers who chanced to be in England
when Wilhelm got into action is Elsie
Greene. The N.S.W. woman is the eldest
daughter of late pastoralist Greene, of
landra, who made a pile in wheat. There
hasn’t been much doing for voluntary toilers
that this spirited patriot hasn’t had her
capable hands in. Just now they’re scour-
ing saucepans, or frying eggs in a canteen in
France.
Will Ogilvie’s verses, “The Australasian,”
have been reprinted on pictorial cards. The
scene that heads these stirring stanzas is
labelled Anzac.
The former Sydney D.T. chief, Dr.
Frederick Ward (now in London), is the
proud parent of a son who’s been mentioned
in despatches. Ward junior has been with
the R.A.M.C. in France. So far he’s
escaped with comparatively slight damages.
Young David, son of our only Uni. Pro-
fessor David, has also been mentioned “for
gallant and distinguished conduct in the the
field.” Dad recently rolled up his cuffs to
have a swipe at the common enemy. Young
David has been with the Cameron High-
landers in Flanders.
Governor Strickland opened the soldiers’
beautiful rest-home last week, Graythwaite.
The generous donor of this mansion for con-
valescents, veteran banker Tom Dibbs,
lurked modestly where he listened to the
volley of thanksgiving. Premier Holman
made the best attempt to thank the fine old
citizen and his wife for their magnificent
present.
The wife of Cullen, C.J., in dove grey,
formally handed over the big house to the
Government, and J. O. Fairfax said a few
words on behalf of the Red Cross. Heavy
rain came down in torrents, and voluntary
aids, squeaking like young mice, charged the
lawn to save the light refreshments which
had begun to float above moist white cloth.
Cadets, with that presence of mind which
marks the boy, hastily fortified themselves
with forage and retired under the tables.
Wary women who had seen the storm coming
and had put on their old, church-going
clothes, remained calm. The outdoor party
fled like white muslin ghosts across the
sodden garden, while the inside company
trotted round to inspect the well-equipped
wards. Mrs. Tom Dibbs, at her old sweet-
heart’s side, got a rousing cheer for her part
in the gift.
Adrian Knox, K.C., gave a brief account
of his stewardship in Egypt to Red Cross
dames on a sweltering afternoon. The pant-
ing company arrived at the Club rooms about
3 p.m., heard the foreword by Mrs. Mac-
kinnon, and slaked its thirst with copious
draughts of tea. This was paid for (very
properly) by the consumers. In an
adjacent room Australian Marguerites
at spinning-wheels kept the yarn flying
for the knitters. Mr. Knox’s turn
came on at four o’clock. His address boiled
down to the glad tidings that Australian
women have sent forward too much rather
than too little. At the finish, tall Mrs. Laid-
ley arose in some majesty to return thanks,
incidentally extinguishing a small visitor
who had taken the floor, presumably for the
same purpose. It was an impressive spec-
tacle.
The Neville Griffith brothers have put up
a good record for one family. Captain J. N.
Griffith is with the R.A.M.C. in France.
His brother Francis sustained slight dam-
ages in the Gallipoli rush. A third has
given his life in the tragic Dardanelles.
Mrs. Arthur Tower is organising a benefit
for the 13th Battalion Comforts’ Fund.
Colonel Miller, now O.C. at Liverpool, has
chosen as the date March 20. “Billy’
Miller, of the Union Theatres, has offered to
film a show at the Glaciarium, and returned
officers and men of the 13th are going to
do their little lot. Every shilling goes to
help the men in the trenches.
Some time ago a man from the North
wrote to say that Australian tinned stuff stood
the tropics without damaging its flavor. The
British War Office, in backing up this state-
ment, proved its earnestness by placing huge
orders for Australian jam, and has already
got away with something like 11,000,0001 b.
It looks as if Australian industries are going
to get the chance they have been languishing
for as a result of the war.
Wandsworth (Eng.) General Hospital has
been good Australian, both in its patients and
nurses. One of the latest Sydney women
to join the staff is Isabel Shiell.
Sister Iv. Heriot, from this continent, has
been mentioned in dispatches. She gained
this distinction in retaliation for her work
in France.
Soldier Balfe has been recently pulling
himself together after his damages at the
Front. He is a member of the Irish family
which gave the composer of “Bohemian
Girl” to the world. Part of his leave he
spent in a pilgrimage to the grave of this
famous relative. A chat with the fiery
John Redmond was another item of interest
to the Australian.
Carrie Lancely, the Gore Hill (Sydney)
soprano, has apparently revoked her in-
tended trip home. For the present she is
putting in all her spare time warbling to
the bandaged Kangaroo in England. Dad
sits on a comfortable cheque-book, so his
songbird can take war times without any of
the sleepless nights that afflict most members
of the hard-hit profession.
Women do go it when they get into farm-
ing togs. An intelligent infant writing from
a N.S.W. farm-school in the country says:
“It’s such fun to see So-and-So in a short
tunic skipping round the pigs, while Such-
and-Such [another school-marm], dressed
like a man, smokes a pipe”! The man with
the hoe fades across the landscape to-day
before the lady dominie chewing negro-head
behind the plough.
The Lady Edeline Hospital for babes is
now more accessible to visitors. A wharf at
Steele Point lands them close to the Vau-
cluse home, where once the Wentworth
family lived. At the second annual meeting
held recently the committee took stock of its
ways and means and found them satisfac-
tory. Dr. Patou conducted the proceedings
from the chair.
A brand new Feminist Club is now added
to women’s varied occupations or pastimes.
It is about six weeks old, and seems to be a
fine bouncing offspring. This week, a Miss
Simpson from U.S.A. will take the floor
and enlighten the congregation in the matter
of the Social Problem. The S.P. is an old
friend, but Uncle Sam’s daughter may have
some inside knowledge about it.
The Francis Chapmans and their only
treasure, Evelyn, are about to leave for
Maoriland. They hail from the land
bottled earthquakes though their recent abid-
ing place has been Sydney, varied by excur-
sions to Europe. In spite of the troubled
state of France, Evelyn may get back to
art study in Paris when the Huns are
whipped back to sausage headquarters. Mean-
while a souvenir of her ability, “Blue and
Gold,” decorates our National Gallery.
This time it’s the 30th Battalion Comforts
Fund. The committee has the light of
battle (for socks, etc.) in its eye. Mrs.
Clark, wife of the 0.C., is president. The
battalion got to Egypt in time to eat its
turkey on Christmas Day. Its Sydney
friends have already crammed about 60
cases with comforts which are now on the
way. Like the daughters of the horseleech
they cry, “More —give more!” To keep
them quiet a gift evening on March 16 re-
quires attention in the Oxford Hall. Tinned
tobacco and other foods are urgently re-
quired. Cash will do.
Planter Burkitt, now a lieutenant in
Liverpool camp, lately returned from the
Solomon Isles. He left his copra behind, but
didn’t forget his pretty wife. (There are a
few thoughtful husbands still.) After some
years in the shade of the cannibal palms the
lady is gluing her nose to the shopwindows.
From “Rosna,” who writes in between
Zeppelin bombs: —
The Maoriland Arthur Rhodes’s son Tahu, who is a
captain in the Grenadier Guards, was knotted to
Helen, eldest daughter of the ex-vice-royalty Plunkets,
at the Guards’ Chapel, Wellington Barracks, London.
It was a “quiet” wedding, which is to say that there
was no reception, but half the peerage was in the
church, marquises, viscounts and such being as plenti-
ful as the decorations. Crowds of wounded M.L.
soldiers, brought in Red Cross vans by khaki-clad
amazons from the M.L. hospital at Walton-on-Thames,
were early at the show, and the bridesmaids distri-
buted favors among them. Three other Plunket
girls, Eileen, Laline and Moira, and Miss Julia
Forbes, were bridesmaidens, and Plunket babes
Ettine and Kiwi (who was born in Maori-
land) trainbearers, while Captain Lloyd, of the Grena-
dier Guards, lent his moral support to the groom.
The Marquis of Anglesey, who is the richest peer in
Britain, was also handy and lent the newly-wedded
one of his stray castles or cottages or mosques for
the honey period.

The Tolish Relief Committee has a show
of goods ready for the long trek to baby
Kosciusko this week. Take the lift at h
Hamilton-street, City.
The Cremorne branch of the Red Cross
Society is preparing for a bold attack on
the poor old public. North Sydney Comedy
Club is to lead the campaign, and it has
“The Private Secretary” in rehearsal. The
performance is fixed for March 11, an un-
fortunate date because it bumps against the
big carnival at the Agricultural Grounds.
Concerning the work being done by British
women in place of men who leave their
billets to rage upon the battlefield, a re-
turned wanderer says that there are about
18 petticoats in the famous Kew Gardens.
In summer their hours are from 6 a.m. to
G p.m., with a spell-oh for meals. Gumtrees
iu pots are the favorite Kew shrines of
Australian travellers. One homesick Syd-
ney girl, who managed to get a few dried
leaves from these pampered growths, writes:
“I threw them into my boarding-house grate
and seemed to get a whiff of the dear bush-
land from their aromatic scent as they fizzled
into smoke.” The “boss” at Kew—to the
fierce indignation of the spade ladies—is an
alien enemy. But the easy-going British
Government refuses to recognise him as any-
thing other than a noted botanist. So he
still pockets £lOOO a year from the patient
taxpayers.
Brigadier-generals who recently came
under the limelight include Embarkation-
officer Lee and the Acting-State Commandant
Ramaciotti. In their cases the rank appears
to be temporary. Sapper Lee, formerly in
the Permanent Engineers, was one of the
smart officers who had to retire through
collision with the age-limit. This law ought
to be re-made with elastic sides. It passes
the limited intelligence of woman to see
tottering old Judges climbing up to 80 or so
autumns go on dealing out law, or what
passes for it, when efficient (at least before
the war) youngsters of 60 in khaki have been
officially slaughtered.
Since writing of Jack Dyer, one of the
youngest majors in uniform, another honor
lias come his way. The Sydney lad (he is
only 22) has been decorated by the French
nation with the Cross.
The war has a weird influence on feminine
fashion. A new autumn toque, armed rather
than trimmed with two great wings, pointed
like an eagle’s pinion, is in the offing. 1 '
don’t know where the offing is, but I suppose
it’s more or less adjacent.)
The silvery-haired Paul Dufault is ex-
pected in Sydney in the early days of winter,
lie throws his baggage off a ’Frisco steamer
in Auckland before Easter, but commences
his concert tour at weepy Dunedin, la"'
line Bindley, soprano; Florence Scapini, tn«
brilliant M.L. youngster with the fiddle; and
the patient Whittle at the piano, are to sup-
port the tenor. Manager Freddy Shipman
is at the helm.
Minister Arthur Griffith will (d.v.) open"
new Free Kindergarten in Chippendale eai)
in this week. This school buds under the
sign of the Golden Fleece.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

CURRANT LOAF
Ingredients —A ozs. butter, 2y gills Nestle’s Condensed
Milk , I/, lbs. flour, 2 eggs, 4 ozs. currants, ly ozs. candied
peel, 1 teaspoonful sugar, 1 oz. yeast.
. Method —Mix the flour, currants, and chopped candied peel
together. Put the yeast into a cup with the sugar, and stir
until it becomes a liquid. Warm the butter and milk together
in a saucepan, add the beaten and yeast. Make a hole in
the centre of the flour, pour the liquid in, and beat well with
the hand for fifteen minutes. Put into two buttered half-
quartern tins, leave in a warm place until the dough has risen
sufficiently to fill the tins, then bake in a rather hot oven
for one hour.
CURD CHEESECAKES
INGREDIENTS: pint NESTLE’S CONDENSED MILK,
3 eggs, 1 oz. flour, 4 ozs. butter, 4 ozs. currants, brandy, short
pastry, nutmeg.
METHOD: Melt the butter in a pan, stir in the flour
smoothly, then the milk, beaten eggs, and currants. Stir unti
the mixture boils and becomes very thick, then add nutmeg and
brandy to taste, and let it get cold. Roll the pastry out toabout
an eighth of an inch thick, line some patty pans with it, put a
teaspoonful of the mixture in each, and bake in a rather hot oven
for fifteen minutes. If any of the mixture is left over it should
be stored in an air-tight jar.
CARA/.IEL RICE PUDDING
Ingredients 3 ozs. rice, Yi pint Nestle’s Condensed Milk ,
rind of a lemon, oz. butter, 2 eggs. 4 ozs. brown sugar.
Method Put the rice into boiling water and let it boil for
ten minutes ; then pour off the water, add the milk and grated
lemon rind, and let it cook gently for half-an-hour. When
ready, pour into a basin to cool, then add the butter and the
beaten eggs. Meanwhile, put the sugar into a small pan with
a tablespoonful of water, and let it boil (without stirring it) until
quite brown, but not burnt, then pour into a pudding mould
or basin and spread it all over the inside. Fill the mould with
the rice mixture, cover with greased paper, and steam for an
hour, then turn out on to a hot dish, or, if liked, it may be
served cold.
RICE SHAPE
INGREDIENTS : 4 ozs. rice, 3 tablespoonfuls NESTLE’S
CONDENSED MILK, 1 oz. castor sugar, pints water, flav-
ouring to taste.
METHOD: Wash the rice and blanch it in water, then
drain and place it in a saucepan with the water and milk, and
simmer till thoroughly cooked. Allow to coot a little, then pour
into a wetted mould. Turn out when set, and serve with jam
or stewed fruit.
MERINGUES
Ingredients —/, lb. castor sugar, Nestle's Condensed Milk,
lemon or vanilla essence, whites of 4 eggs.
Method - Whisk the whites of the eggs until very stiff in-
deed, then add the sugar and lay the mixture in egg shapes
with a tablespoon on a baking tin which has previously been
rubbed with a piece of white wax. Dredge the meringues
with castor sugar, and place them at once in a cool oven.
Bake until the meringues are a pale fawn colour and feel
quite crisp, then remove the soft underneath part and lay
them back in the oven to dry. When cold, fill with flavoured
condensed milk, and press them together in twos.
APRICOT PUDDING
INGREDIENTS: 1 lb. stewed apricots, pint NESTLE’S
CONDENSED MILK, slices of bread, 2 eggs.
METHOD : Line a buttered basin or plain round pudding
mould with bread, then pQur in the hot stewed apricots, and fit
a round piece of bread on the top. Put a plate over it, lay a
weight on the top, and leave it until next day ; then turn out
and serve with custard poured over and around it. Fcr the
custard, heat the milk, pour it on to the beaten eggs, pour into
a jar placed in a pan of boiling water, and stir until it thickens.
Allow it to get cold, then use as directed.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melboubne, March 6, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
A big, overdressed crowd flocked out to
Flemington to see the Newmarket Handicap
w r on on Saturday. It was officially listed
as the first V.R.C. autumn meeting, and as
the dressers had their autumn regalia in
the wardrobe they put it all on regardless
of the warm weather. It was pathetic. The
prize example was a Riverina squattress
who had a neck-to-knee tapestry of fox
skins and a grey-wliite woollen cap adorned
with ram’s horns.
A girl bound in khaki cloth and puttees
wore a stand-up collar of seal- and fox-fur,
which hid her face to the eyes ; and the niece
of a gorged bookmaker tottered about with a
whole wolf-skin across her shoulders. Mrs.
Clive Leonard was swathed in well-cut black
velvet. Her sister, Miss Reynolds, in black
glac6, had a sable and silk scarf round her
shoulders. Pretty little red-haired Mrs.
Roberts (she is a niece of the Darnley
countess) walked in light white cloth, with
a wild necklet of feathers. The Weedon
dame put grey feathers round her throat and
a fiery scarlet hat on her head; her dress was
navy blue, and had spreading flounces. One
of the daughters of a Warrnambool squatter
wore a silk belltopper garlanded insanely
wdth asters. Mrs. Sternberg was comfort-
able and neat in fawn gabardine, and Mrs.
Peter wore the coolest of black-and-white
tailorings, with a cute hat decorated fore and
aft with pompoms. L. Madden—the only
one of the family present—was bolstered
up tightly in black and wore a wide coster
hat laden with spreading red feathers. One
of the Fenner girls wore black. Jess Fair-
bairn sported black wings in her hat; one
of them stood 12 inches in the air, while
the other flapped furiously above her left
ear. Mrs. Alawa Buchanan balanced a rooster
on her hat and her husband was under a grey
pudding-bowl boxer. They were a brave
sight going down the lawn. Mrs. Lockwood’s
velvet coat was five yards round the waist-
hem, and her perched green hat measured
about seven inches round the ridiculous
brim. A fashionable young bride was in
something fur-trimmed and knee-high. It
was fastened straight down the. back from
neck to hem. Mrs. Pratt hovered awhile in
white and then went on to a dinner-party
with the Fred Shipmans.
Tremendous preparations are being made
for the Red Cross Carnival and Fair which
is to take place in the new military hospital
in Kooyong-road, Caulfield, on Saturday,
March 18. This hospital is said to be by
far the largest of its kind south of the line,
and the large grounds are peculiarly suited
for charitable bushranging. The usual
“opening” will be performed by the G.-G.,
encouraged by Lady Helen.
The Best dame has put on the warpaint
and is out on the trail looking for money for
the Camberwell Red Cross. She took coun-
cil with the Mayor and other intelligent
citizens, and the result is to be a three day
carnival which will begin on March 23 in
the Canterbury Gardens. Make a note of it
in your social ledger.
A man writing from Egypt tells that
Sliepheard’s Hotel at Cairo is like Menzies
before the war. Mrs. Alec Hay and
Lady Loughborough (once Sheila Chis-
holm) are the reigning beauties. The young
husband of the latter lady (he is only a
junior sub.) did a rather cute thing lately.
He was with a garrison which was holding
out against the Never Mind What. They
discovered that the telegraph wires were
being cut. He went off on his own and
wired to one in authority at Cairo: “Don’t
trouble sending reinforcements; 30,000
arriving by sea.” The horrified enemy held
off, and in the meantime the badly-needed
help had time to come up. When the
sender of the wire was identified he was
recalled to Cairo and officially cursed.' His
troubles!
Kath Osborne, Mrs. Harry Chisholm,
Mrs. Les Wills-Alien and the Pat Osbornes
are also “amongst those present” at Shep-
heard’s. Whilst dining there the other night
my private ambassador heard a croaking
voice saying his name. He turned and
found it was coming from the lips of Charles
Orr —scion of the Macleay-street (Sydney)
house. It appears he was missing in Gal-
lipoli and has temporarily lost his voice after
many hardships. Arrivals from Anzac were
often in rags. Jack AntilJ sat down to the
first decent meal he had had in months,
wearing short pants, such as runners wear,
and a very dirty old uniform jacket.
From the above you might think the army
lived at Shepheard’s. Not so. Our work
is hard and incessant, but we make for that
hotel on our weekly day off to bathe, change
and have a three-volume meal.
The vice-regal Fergusons have been on a
visit to Molesworth Green, who hangs out at
Greystanes, near Bacchus Marsh. Moles-
worth is somewhere in the senior 80’s ; but
he drove his visitors about in his favorite
drg-cart behind a fidgety pair of liigh-step-
pers. Most of the squatters round about began
on a bark-hut basis and built the palaces
when the money came ; but the fine old Greene
homestead- or abbey has lifted its battlements
above the sheep since the first.
Captain Foxton, one of the most accom-
plished and agile Genthelps introduced to
this part of the world; has thrown the shawl
aside, moth-balled his belltopper and warned
the Hun that he is about to begin.
The beautiful widow, Mrs. Roderick
Cameron (she was a Sydney girl born Linde-
man), has started off with her baby and
two maids to brave marine and. submarine
perils on board the Mongolia. But first she
“knocked” Manly by appearing with a
Buster Brown crop. All American small
children are cropped Buster-wise, with
a big ribbon bow high on the left
side of the head. But for a grown-uo
and a relict at that, and still in weeds!
Well, Manly said it was no wonder this war
was sent, my dear.
Marjorie Williamson came of age in
January, and thus drops in for an extra pot
of money. Probably this is the reason of
the family’s somewhat unexpected return to
Australia. The trio will arrive .early next
month.
The Lachlan Beatons and Miss Ruby
Adams ha*ve gone to Tasmania for a short
holiday amongst the ripening apples.
The Old Melbournians collected at the
Grammar School the other afternoon and
told one another they were getting greyer
and fatter —this in the intervals of playing
the old games they used to get excited
about. Henry Cornish was so delighted
with his performance on the cricket field
that .he bought tea for everybody in the
vicinity and a few round the corner, a very
noble performance considering that tea was
double the usual price, for the good of some
patriotic fund.
Mabel Waldock, daughter of the late
George Jenkins, the knightly. Clerk of Par-
liaments, has come back on a health-hunt-
ing visit from Ceylon, where her tea-planter
husband makes his income. She will spend
some weeks in a private hospital while he
takes his rheumatism further on to the
healing mud baths of Maoriland.
Adelaide wafts a few items :
The Y.M.C.A. has added to its long list of good
works by building a reading- and writing-room for
the men at the Mitcham camp. The opening cere-
mony was performed by the usual vice-regal trades-
man, with some additional remarks from Camp-
Commandant Stuart and Premier Vaughan.
Mrs. C. E. Hayward, whose son has been assaulting
the Hun since the kick-off, has booked her passage
by the Mongolia. A further vacancy in our tea
circle has been made by the departure of the David
Wilkies to Maoriland.
The Wallman-Oldham wedding oceured at Wood-
ville on Saturday without casualties. The bride’s
belonging are inextricably mingled with the history
of the State, the Oldhams having got here shortly
after the aboriginals. She is a granddaughter of the
Mr. Halcomb who succeeded to the clerkship of the
Legislative Council at the time of Federation, and
was Assistant-Clerk long before that.
The South-East will miss Mrs. E. T. Lea, of Robe,
who died, after over 60 years in S.A., at the end of
the week. She was a woman who would rather make
a neighbor happy than buy a new bonnet—and she
could have bought hundreds. Some of her money
came through the famous Lea .and Perrin firm, and a
good deal from her knowledge of the land and shrewd
management of her affairs. Her husband died when
she was quite young, leaving her with a son and
daughter, both of whom survive her.
Dr. J. F. Souter, who went with a transport to
Egypt about four months ago and was taken ill in
Cairo, is dead.
Lieut. M. M. Cudmore, of the R.F.A., who had the
bad luck to be wounded not long ago, has been men-
tioned in dispatches. Also, Captain James Way, who
was mentioned some time back, has got his majority
and the Military Cross. Surgeon-Captain Flecker,
who is in command of the 2nd Auxiliary Hospital at
Heliopolis, is another who has gone up a step.
The discovery of oil in the South-East is an event
that occurs as regularly as strikes in N.S.W. The
latest is a find of petroleum in a locality which is
supposed to be kept strictly secret, but is alleged
to be near Carpenter’s Rocks.
The Home for Incurables is to be enlarged by the
addition of a new wing, and I wish it was to be
rechristened as well. No refuge should bear a name
that kills hope at the entrance gates.
Adelaide musicians have given a farewell concert
to Merle Robertson, who goes to London on an Elder
Scholarship. She is 17, with fine wrists and good
power of pianoforte execution. As for the violin, she
has won two local scholarships; also, she possesses a
neat gift of composition, and uplifts a promising con-
tralto voice which may be useful as a side-line.
Allan Fairbairn, of the Newtown (Gee-
long) family, is to marry Hazel Storrer at
the local lark on March 15. They’ll be
honeymooning in Tasmania by St. Patrick’s
Day.
Solicitor Backhouse, of Melbourne, has
served Jeannie Stewart with a sentimental
wnt,_ and the case has been set down for
hearing before a minister in the near future.
After the ceremony at Holy Trinity the
invited at the Fincham-Higham wedding
goose-stepped to the Georgian room at the
Oriental for a helping of food. Bride
Muriel Higham comes from Westralia,
but her husband, Sergeant Randall Fincham,
is the son of bank-manager Fincham. Ran-
dall is presently going to Duntroon to study
for his field-marshal s certificate.
Stanley Ricketson, a bright young mem-
ber of ’Change, who got into khaki early, is
conveying hack to Australia a lieutenancy,
the D.C.M. and two wounds. Soon after
his welcome he will conduct Gwen Browne
to the altar. Sister Doris Ricketson, who
was absorbing education by the quart in
Switzerland when war upset supplies, is
visiting Uncle Sheppard in Java on her way
home.
A wedding of interest in Maoriland and
on this side happened in England towards
the end of December, when Captain Bob
Ramsey, on the staff of the First Australian
Division at Gallipoli and elder son of the
late Bob Ramsey, of Melbourne, took
to wife Mabel Alice, only-daughter of G. D.
Greenwood, a wool king of Amberley and
owner of various fleet horses.
The William Jaffrays, of Armadale, have
been having a diamond wedding. The
bridegroom is a veteran of the Customs, part
of the remnant of the old Victorian brigade
which had its good billet ready made when
it was decided to dabble with a national
tariff. For a quarter-century Mrs. William
has been a pillar of the Toorak Benevolent
Society, that amiable but somewhat short-
sighted concern which still works its homely
miracles in the Toorak “village” and the
remoter, crowded purlieus of Prahran. The
old lady is also a long-established power
behind the Government in its conduct of
the Girls’ Training Home at East Mel-
bourne. That institution is one of the few
instances of Socialism in the home upon
which Toorak looks with an approving eye,
for it yearly produces a considerable quan-
tity of 18-carat domestic help.
Belgian baritone Goossens is the proud dad
of a particularly well-dressed baby, the Aus-
tral Salon having composed the trousseau
some months ago. The Salon mostly lays
itself out to encourage Art; this time it evi-
dently thought it would encourage nature for
a change.
On Friday night the borrowed Auditorium
re-echoed the thunderous applause of re-
turned soldiers, who were out to make their
first concert a success. The vice-regalities
sat in the centre of the circle surrounded by
the best-cut khaki. Lady Helen in black had
a red bouquet on the ledge before her with
red streamers falling into space; Maggie
Stirling, on the stage, intoned impressively
in white and black lace; and Carmen Del-
prat fiddled beautifully in a cocoanut-ice
costume. A variegated chorus of soldiers’
sisters, with a girl in bright purple in the
foreground, sang “Till the Boys Come
Home,” and another chorus of Boys Who
Had Come Plome roared cheerfully and coo-
eed. Signaller Prichard brought on a small
grey mascot monkey with a tail as long as a
bill of costs to help him sing a modern adap-
tation of “Egypt.” and bugler Phillips sur-
prised and delighted the house with some
bird imitations that brought the bush right
into the crowded hall. It was a show that
had n' lie of the cold-storage atmosphere of
the ordinary concert.
Elsie Davies, a singing Victorian who
was dispatched by the concert met lr d to
Eurone for further study, is back again in
Melbourne. It is reported that she sang
successfully in various grand-opera houses
before the guns drowned all other top-notes.
An Auditorium concert is being arranged for
March 11. .
V illiam Laver, the new Ormond Professor
at the Melbourne University Conservatoiium,
has not forgotten his old friends. Staff-
appointments at the Con. for this year in-
clude Harold Cinclare Smith and Frank
Homewood, both of whom were bright stu-
dents under Laver in the remote past. Some
20 years ago Harold Smith came to Mel-
bourne for piano-study from Swansea, a
Pasmanian east-coast village, and gained his
diploma at the Conservatorium in Marshall
Hall s early days, winning the Ormond exhi-
bition during each of the three years in the
course. Pie has a graceful piano-stool man-
ner, and his fund of theoretical knowledge
should make him a useful teacher. Home-
wood is a Williamstown native, not perhaps
so brilliant as Smith, but capable of striking
sparks out of the piano in exercises which
under Harold’s manipulation, would merely
show a gentle incandescence. Bertie Zel-
nmn, who used to teach the fiddle in the late
Franklin Peterson’s time and resigned when
Marshall Hall came on deck for the last few
months of his life, is also back at the
Conservatorium.
The new prospectus of the University Con-
servatorium contains particulars of the
teaching staff for 1916. There are a few
well-known names, among them that of
Edward Goll ( special pianoforte soloist and
chief and special study teacher”), Elise
Miedermann (singing) and Louis Ilatten-
bach, of the Leipzig Conservatorium (violin-
cello). Ihe Ormond Professor (William
Laver, of Australia) is alleged in the pro-
spectus to have spoken thus in making the
annual report : “I regret to state that, owing
to the conditions prevailing, the Conserva-
torium will lose one of its most valued mem-
bers—Mr. Edward Scliarf.” As it is writ-
e{|’» c j lle shall be taken and three others
, thls case lt: lo °ks as though it was
the one taken who got left.

If you have been following music and the

AN e won’t be able to get along the street
tor war-lecturers presently. Frederic Cole-
man. F.R.G.S., is the latest attempted spell-
binder, and one of his chiefest treasures is a
letter from a British major-general to the
effect that his war hordes trod on Coleman
every time they turned to face the foe
between August, 1914, to the following May.
Smith-Dorrien also takes up his pen to thank
Coleman for having given him a lift in his
car on the morning after an early battle of
the war. Mrs. Coleman and a young daugh-
ter arrived with the lecturer, who came this
way via the Cape and Hobart.
W hen the warrior comes home with his eye
in a sling lie finds various festivities await-
ing him. Hugh Mclntosh turned on the
Tivoli Follies the other day for returned
braves, and during the interval Vera Pearce,
in Red Cross uniform, wandered round the
auditorium distributing flowers and ribbons.
Thelma Raye also went a walk-about in a
crinoline arrangement of amber silk and
some rice-powder.
Authoress Katherine Susannah Pritchard
has had to reply to so many speeches of late
that she is becoming an orator. She is also
becoming one of our best-fed since she
returned from London with the prize for
“The Pioneers.” Her latest meal was pro-
vided by Premier Peacock at Parliament
House. The tragic moment was when a
foolish guest inquired if Katherine were an
Australian. lle\ was immediately thrown
under the table and fed with bones.
11. V. Mc-Kay has supplied the Defence
Department with a quarter of a million of
horseshoes made at his smithy, and is quite
willing to supply any other orders. I men-
tion this because some mere male contri-
butor to The Bulletin stated that “heavy
horseshoes are practically unobtainable in
the Commonwealth.” I know better. I
have seen at the McKay* works hundreds
of fiery shoes rushing up a perpendicular
race 20 feet toward the ceiling. They look
like hundreds of little red devils scrambling
for freshly-caught souls.
An iron-roller named Vaughan started
making horseshoes round the corner from
Princes’ Bridge. He happened on a lot of
trouble at the time with employees and, in
a huff, turned the key in the factory door
and dropped the industry. Being a rich
man lie was able to indulge his huffiness.
The cultivation of the extra military eye-
brow on the upper lip is causing some of
the young trainee officers to lose their beauty
sleep. The poor things can only raise the
sort of fluff that comes on old puddings. In
days gone by the military female-killer wore
a sort of drooping switch or scalp-lock under
his nose; but the modern fashion is to wear
something that looks as if it had come out
of an actor’s make-up box. I used to think
the war would end before the warriors would
have time to grow special whiskers for it:
but here we are, in the second year, invent-
ing new fashions and conventions for the.
crushing of the Huns. (At least I hope it
helps to crush them.)
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“Spraggs” : We’d had a big night; every-
one made a speech, and toasts were drunk ahead of
timetable. About 11 p.m. McPherson went round the
room and was bidding all hands “Good nieht.”,
“What 1” says Regan, “You’re not going home so
soon?” “No!” hiccups Mac; “I’m just sayin’
good nicht while I can see ye all.”
* * * *
“Ino”: Here’s the cure for a horse’s sore
shoulder. Put three-quarters of a pint of linseed or
castor oil into a quart bottle, add a quarter-pint of
turpentine (must be of the best). Mix well. Pour
in three drams of sulphuric acid and leave it un-
corked and at peace with all men for an hour. Then
put a quarter-pint of the oil used, plus a cake of
camphor cut as fine as possible and moistened with
turpentine, into a vessel. Float the vessel on boiling
water till the camphor is dissolved, and mix this
with the contents of the quart bottle. Keep closely
corked and away from burglars. Apply with a
feather as often as convenient, say three times a day.
Don’t use the same feather twice; take of the mix-
ture only what you want and put none back; and
don’t put the feather in the bottle. This treatment
will cure shoulders, no matter how long they have
been sore, girth galls, saddle galls, pole evil, fistulas,
ulcerated sores, sitfasts, corns and all kinds of
wounds on man or beast.
* * * *
“Cleveland”: Dropped into a hatter’s camp
before the drought broke out Boulia (Q.) way just
in time to learn something valuable. His gills had
a cut of boiled beef attached midway upon a 10ft.
stick. At intervals of about 20 minutes he would
get up and carefully turn the stick end for end. I
stocd it as long as I could and then he showed me
the trick. What I saw was a string of bewildered
meat-ants just finishing a crawl down the pole.
On arrival at the bottom they waved their whiskers
furiously and swore venomously, while the scouts got
busy and again located the meat. Then a frenzied
dash up the pole ensued. “Yer see,” said the
hatter, as he carefully turned the stick, “ther silly
cows don’t know whether they’re cornin’ or goin’, an’
so long as yer turns her reglar ther ants don’t get
a look in.” He paused reflectively. “’Tain’t no
good for flies, though,” he added.
* * * *
“M.C.Y.”: To my mind the finest of all
the West Australian wild flowers are those about the
Phillips River district. There, besides the kennedya,
wild mignonette, “cockatoo-claw,” “spiders,” “dog-
face,” and others equally well known, almost every
bush and tree has its blossom. The two most beauti-
ful are the hibiscus, which spreads its long, spike-
foliaged trails, massed with big, delicate, heliotrope
blossoms, all about the gullies, and the Quailup bell.
The latter grows on a single stalk from a foot to
four feet in height, carrying from three to five bells
(I have gathered seven on one stalk). It is as big
as a Canterbury-bell, and perfect in shape, with a
double row of petals, the inside ones a lettuce green
and those outside sometimes a darker green, but
usually purple or dull crimson. These dainty, deli-
cate-looking flowers keep fresh in water for weeks.
I shall never forget driving in the early spring from
Ravensthorpe to Hopetoun. For mile after mile, on
each side of the track the plain was one gorgeous
carpet of flowers, crimson, blue, white, yellow; and
so thickly massed that not a sign of a green leaf
could be seen.

“Searchlight”: Some of the giant tallow
woods at the foot of the Dome mountain, the highest
point on the Dorrigo (N.S.W.), occasionally lose
their foothold and go hurtling into the depths
below. One that had been weakened by the
scrub-fires lately toppled over, and as it was over
eight feet through there was a rush of business in
the valley. It cannoned into another vegetable of
the same variety, and took it along with it.
Together they cut a track nearly a quarter of a mile
long down the mountain-side and as straight as a
clothes-line weighted by a dead elephant.
* * * *
“Eddyson”: Waddy possessed only one
notable, and that was Perry’s black bull. It was
known to bull specialists and dairymen generally as
Prince Charming, but in and about Waddy, where
he lorded it like a kaiser, he was simply Perry’s
bull. Henry Perry, who owned the bull, also owned
land and herds at Cardigan, and an extensive milk
walk in Ballarat; but he was distinguished for
nothing but his bull, and had neither hopes nor
aspirations apart from it. He was wrapped up in
the animal, and was never so happy as when mani-
curing the brute, or polishing and staining its horns,
or putting additional lustre to its hide, which
always gleamed like black satin. You saw little of
Perry apart from the bull. If he went for a walk
he took the bull with him, as another man might
take a poodle, and the sight of Perry sauntering
through the township, gravely attended by his majes-
tic bull, its hoofs gleaming like amber, its horns
as smooth and dainty as finished ivory, its hide
speckless and shimmering, and a bunch of three rib-
bons tied to its sinuous tail, was a common one in
Waddy, yet a spectacle that never failed to interest
and delight the simple inhabitants. Perry, who had
no theme of conversation apart from the bull, would
linger and discuss Prince Charming with any person,
however lowly. He frequently led the bull into
Stubbs’s bar, and loitered for hours there, expatiat-
ing on its physical beauty and temperamental charm,
and the bull was content so long as he received an
occasional tributary pint in a tin dish. Perry’s bull
appreciated beer. On Sundays they always took
long walks together; but they did not go to church,
because bulls were not admitted. Perry’s wife died
while Perry was exhibiting Prince Charming at the
Ballarat Agricultural Show, and when Perry returned
with the triumphant bull, a first prize and a special
order of merit along with a certificate for the best-
kept bull in the show, he was genuinely sorry. “I
could easy have got home an hour earlier,” he said,
“and it would have pleased Annie to hear about
Charmin’. Now she’s gone where she’ll never know.”
And Perry wept. He insisted on leading the bull
in his wife’s funeral procession. He and the bull
were chief mourners, following directly behind the
hearse, and the bull had his horns dyed black,
and a bunch of cigpe on his tail. After that
Perry took the bull into the house. He had the
spare bedroom pitched and fitted up for Prince
Charming, and used to sit with the bull of evenings
for company, always with an old silk handkerchief in
his hand to polish any spots on the bull that seemed
comparatively dim. At this stage Perry made his
will, leavipg £4OOO to the bull to provide for his
declining years and pay a valet to attend to his
wants and see that nothing dimmed the lustre of his
skin or the perfection of his sybaritic toenails. But
Perry s bull died first and Perry was never the same
man after. He had the skin stuffed, and buried
the beef with almost royal honors in his orchard,
putting a bronze monument over the grave. For
years he devoted his leisure to polishing the remains,
and when at length he passed away he left strict
orders in his will that the stuffed ox was to be
planted over his own grave in place of a headstone.
But the cemetery authorities refused to permit a bull
to officiate as a monument within those sacred
precincts, and the last time I saw Perry’s bull he
was shored up on the verandah of a firm that
specialised in conditions powders for cows. Perry,
I have no doubt, is still turning in his grave.

THE MYSTERY OF LAST NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With silver sword the morning struck
The eastward fringe of night;
A sudden red showed in the ruck
Of clouds that turned in flight;
I heard a voice, in sleepy tones,
And in the dawn-light clear,
I saw the burly form of Jones
Adorned with bottled beer.
“I wonder where’s the motor-boat?—
There’s no sign of the wreck!”
Jones murmured, as he sharply smote
And broke a bottle neck.
The wavelets, full of laughter, raced
Along their sandy bed,
Jones drank his liquor, sombre faced,
“Poor Robinson!” he said.
Smiling, I recollected things
Though I was sorely grieved:
We were, through last night’s happenings,
Of Robinson bereaved.
The motor-car had madly dashed,
Ungoverned, to the sea,
And Robinson—who must be smashed—
Leapt not with Jones and me!
But Jones made noises in his throat
While thus I put the case.
Said he, “We brought the motor-boat
To get near Henry’s place.
A tempest swept us towards the sand—
I saw those waves come on!”
You leaped with me; we swam to land —
Poor Robinson is gone.
We could not on the point agree.
When we Had drunk our beer,
Both full of grief for Henry, we
Sought out a hostel near.
We tried to settle how we might
To break the news contrive
To Henry’s wife, when into sight
Came Robinson —alive !
Said Henry: “Fancy meeting you!
Why I rode here, of course!
It was a silly thing to do—-
I somehow lost the horse.”
He jeered at Jones quite bitterly,
When he assayed to show
His view of things, till Jones said he
Had been too drunk to know!
Then Robinson declared that Jones
Was just a lying hound ;
And Jones remarked, in hostile tones,
He wished he had been drowned.
I reconciled their views with wine;
But while these dreamers jar
I dread to meet some friend of mine
Who’s lost a motor-car!

“Two Seas”: It’s a lamentable fact that
the average bushman hasn’t even the most rudimen-
tary knowledge of ornithology. AVas watching a
man the other day blazing away with a breech-
loader at a swarm of blue martins that hovered over
his cherry orchard. “What on earth are you shoot-
ing them for?” I inquired. He looked astonished.
Indignation was writ all over his corrugated visage.
”1 wouldn’t have a blanky cherry left if I didn’t
down ’em,” he spluttered. “There’s a nest in every
tree.”. “Ever seen any of them picking at the
cherries?” I asked. “I don’t give ’em time if I
can help it,” he retorted. Then I explained to him
that he was destroying his best friends—birds that
never touched cherries, or any other fruit, but, on
the contrary, chased away from the trees they nested
in parrots, starlings, sparrows, leather-heads and
even the deadly persistent parrakeets, who will strip
a cherry-orchard bare in a few hours. When I had
gathered up a few of the slain martins and shown
him that their crops only contained the remains of
insects the bird murderer was convinced. “Always
thought all birds ate fruit,” he remarked regretfully.
“And look at the good powder and shot I’ve wasted
on ’em.” Like the bushie who assures one that it is
impossible to poison gohannas, the chump never
thought of investigating for himself. “It’s only
what I've been told, you know,” gets on one’s nerves
in the bush. For my part, it is only the high price
of pork that prevents me testing the popular
belief that a pig cuts its own throat when it
attempts to swim—or is it fly? I’m not sure
which it is, but it's probably a delusion, anyhow.
* * * *
“Broadie”: Let me edge in my toughest’
bird. He is the musk duck, and he is so tough
that thq, only way to consume him is to powder
his carcase on a grater and use it as snuff.

“F.H.R.”: Recently I challenged any
Abo-liars that had ever met or knew a person who
had been cured, or had himself been cured, of
snakebite by Morrisey, Fox, Davis or other so-
called sngke specialists, to speak up. I conclude
that no one has had the benefit of these “cures.”
Therefore, taking into consideration that snakes are
still going strong, I again urge Governments and
Ministers for Education in particular to see that a
proper strychnine outfit is installed in every country
school at once. With children walking through the
bush to schools situated up to 60 miles from a
doctor it is wicked that no remedy should be handy.
The Commonwealth Committee on the
Northern Territory railway extension got some
lurid details about white ants and their work up
there during its sittings in Adelaide. AVhen it
heard that they had eaten a box containing billiard
balls, and had then made a plucky start on the
balls themselves, chairman Riley remarked that S.A.
should have given that sort of information before it
got the Commonwealth to take the Territory over.
However, the following day it® was announced that
even down south the same old ant has got into
the wooden water-main on Yorke’s Peninsula, just
opposite Adelaide, though the main is full of water
and coated on the outside with hessian soaked in
bitumen and tar.

“Milo”: A cedar tree recently sold at
Peeramon, Atherton Tableland (N.Q.), had a girth-
measurement 4ft. from the ground of 34ft. When
carved up it yielded 32,000 superficial feet of timber.
I remember a cedar tree on the Upper Richmond
(N.S.AV.) which had to be ringed to a depth that
could be conveniently bottomed with an axe before
a specially-made 14ft. saw could be used. But the
super contents were less than those of the Peera-
mon vegetable by some few thousand feet.

“Leo Lear”: Re “Rita S.’s” par. (B.
10/2/’l6). I don’t like to disagree with a lady,
especially one who writes of our birds, their useful-
ness and beauty; but, Rita, we’ve no woodpeckers in
Australia. The birds so called are probably tree-
creepers or tree-runners. The former are more fre-
quent and are found throughout the Commonwealth.
Besides their useful qualities they are not out of
the chorus when vocal organs are being tried. The
tree-runners in contradiction to the creepers invari-
ably climb downwards after on a tree ; also
they haven’t managed to bridge Bass Strait. Both
species get their food on the outside of the bark,
whereas the woodpecker planks his bill through the
overcoat of the vegetation for the inside residents.
No doubt our ironbarks and similar steel-plated
growths discouraged the latter practice in Australia.
* * * *
“Bill Bedsox”: “Rag’s” statement in a
recent Abo. par that he has seen the fragrant
boronia growing on “desert” plains in the Golden
West is piffle. The true sweet-scented boronia is
like the date palm in one respect in that it must
have its feet in the water. I have gathered its
“sweet, brown blossoms lined with gold” in most
parts of W.A., where it is known to exist, and in
every case have wet my boots in the process.
The favorite lodgings of this particular variety of
boronia is the rushy flat which fringes the “paper-
bark” swamp of the South-West. Other varieties,
reds, yellows, striped, spotted and blotched with
white, are found on the plains, but their fragrance
is of the pole-cat order.
* * * *
“Barcoo”: There are nine species known
of the cassowary ( Cassuardiedce ) spread over the
northern fringe of the Australian coast, New Guinea
and adjacent islands. The true home of the birds is
the island of Ceram, lying west of N.G. The
species Casuarius australis, which frequents the thick
scrubby mountainous regions of York Peninsula is
peculiar to Australia and is the only species found
on the mainland. It is easily the most muscular and
highly-developed member of the family A very
large specimen would reach sft. skyward, and in a
good season, when the tropical wild fruits and
berries, which grow prolifically in the birds’ haunts
are abundant, a healthy cass. would scale well
towards 2001 b. eu

“Brad” appears as the crow’s only friend'
A word or two in praise of that much-maligne<i
cieature, the crow. After long observation of the
inky bird I have arrived at the conclusion that he
is a friend lather than an enemy of mankind Thp
crow has a great liking for insects that “hum” in
the olfactory sense, and for this reason he scoffs a
large quantity of sundries at which other birds
would draw the line. The number of eggs ant
young poultry devoured is next-to-nothing in propor-
tion to the insects our midnight friend gets through
He also eats mice and rats when they come his way
or when he goes their way Last year, when grass-
hoppers were abundant, I shot six crows that were
in the habit of mooning round my premises and held
a. post-mortem on each. The stomachs were full of
hoppers,” while one contained also a partly-
digested mouse. It is no easy matter to poison a
crow, as he is -able to swallow a bait and discharge
it again with as much ease as an importer swallow-
ing his principles and throwing up a groundless
aspersion at an Australian manufacturer.
* * * *
“Musketoon”: The best cure for sore
shoulders on a horse is to wash the wound with cool
water, and when dry rub with some brine. This
forms a dry scale over the sore, and a mixture called
“gall-cure,” which can be got at any chemist's, if
applied to the scale will keep it soft. This cure
was given me by Bill Rogerson, of Forbes (N.S.W.),
a man through whose hands hundreds of horses pass
in a week, and a well-known authority on all their
ailments. He reckons it is pure carelessness for any
man’s horse to have sore shoulders. If bathed in
cold water as soon as the collar is removed, and
the inside of the collar also washed, there would be
no sore shoulders.

when you get there?”
EMIGRANT : “Take up land.”

EMIGRANT : “A shovelful at a time.”
“Spreader”: I know perfectly well that
c-attle follow thunderstorms and that sheep feed
against the wind. But what beats me is, why the
jumbucks, in the northern States especially, have a
strong tendency to make south.
DESERT BOXES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bleached bones, commingling with the desert sand,
To whom did you belong?—
Some exile from a far, forgetful land?
Some outcast who went wrong?
Some fugitive who left the world behind
One black and bitter day,
Within your breast oblivion to find?....
Oh, Desert, say.
lias Piker’s pub his haven—and his Hell?
Did those piano strains,
In some sad, heart-corroding fashion, tell
Of Her across the Plains?
And when the Scarlet Girl red-lipped an air
Once sung far, far away,
Di.l he give dalliance to his last despair?....
Oh, Desert, say.
And did he trudge a-through the mulga scrub
Dike someone lost in dreams,
Ilis back toward old Piker Doolan’s pub—-
Its scailet and its schemes?
And did he circle in the next day's heat,
A speck within the grey,
W ith thirsting throat, and burning, blist’ring feet ?
Oh, Desert, say.
Was no one near? Did only winds caress
His forehead in the throes
Of death and agonies of loneliness?
Or did the quarking crows
Swoop down, and shut from his world-wearied eyes
The garish light of day,
The blue of the remote, unpitying skies?
Oh, Desert, say.
back o’ boubke.
R. J. CASSIDY.

Symptoms of aWeaK Bladder
A Very Common Complaint with Serious Consequences.
Thousands vSuffer in Ig'norance.
It is really remarkable how many people are suffering with bladder trouble, yet do not
know it. Numberless children are suffering with weak bladders, and their parents do not
know it, thinking all the time that the child is naughty. This is wrong. But there is an
even greater number of men and women, both old and young, who are suffering with bladder
trouble. They know there is something wrong, but what it is they cannot tell.
Just read these symptoms through, and see if any of them apply to you:—Nasty taste in
the mouth, bad complexion, scanty urine, restlessness at night, continual thirst, pains in the
groin with a burning sensation inside, inability to retain urine (bed-wetting), stone or gravel
in the bladder, too frequent desire to urinate, brick-dust or sediment in the urine, and an
irritable temper.
If you have any of these symptoms you should not neglect them, because if you do you
will find that instead of curing itself your bladder trouble will get worse. It has never been
known for a case of bladder disease to cure itself. It almost invariably develops. Slight
jfains may develop into chronic rheumatism and gout. A little darkness under the eyes may
develop into dropsical swellings. Cloudy urine may develop into gravel and gravel into stone.
Never neglect bladder trouble no matter how slight.

kidneys in passing the uric-acid poison from the system, this prolific source of bodily torture
is quickly but surely removed. When the kidneys and bladder are restored, and can pass off
this poison themselves, the trouble will, of course, disappear—the dragging pains will leave,
the joints cease aching, and the delicate organs will become soothed in their irritated and
inflamed parts. Then you really will be surprised how different all the world will seem to
you. Instead of drudgery, your household duties will become a pleasure, life will be brighter—
you will be brighter and happier, and so will those with whom you come in contact.

“Patricia B.” : A new Poverty Point has
sprung up in Sydney. Up to a few years
ago the precincts of King and Castlereagh
streets formed the meeting-place of the “rest-
ing” performer. Subsequently many of him
sought freedom from the insistent John
Hops by securing a club room. This has
now fallen into disuse, and the out-of-work
performer has selected Pitt-street (between
Park-street and the Lyceum Pictures) as his
unhappy hunting-grounds.
“Masetto” : “Jwosp” (B. 24/2/T6) is leni-
ent to Genevieve Ward when he makes her
67. That starts her off from 1849 ; so she
must have been fully six years old when she
married the Count de Guerbel in 1855. The
opera career referred to happened between
that year and 1562, and “Jwosp” admits 11
years of teaching before the sensational ap-
pearance at Manchester in 1873. Let him
credit another 10 years to the brilliant singer
and he will be nearer the mark. ,
The literary property of the late Mrs.
Bernard Beere, who toured Australia, has
been brought to the hammer in London.
series of letters to her from Oscar Wilde
reached good prices. One written from
Dieppe just after his release from prison
was knocked down for £lO 15s. The Times
prints it in gasps:—
My Dear Good Beautiful Friend.
....I knew you would always be sweet and good
to me....for now I need sympathy, and know its
value, a kind word to me now is as lovely to me as
a flower is, and love can heal all wounds. I cannot
write much as I am nervous —. . . .1 feel as if I had
been raised from the dead, the Sun and the Sea
seem strange to me. But, Dear Bernie, although
my life looks ruined to the outer world, to me it is
not 50.... I feel that out of it all —out of the
silence, the solitary life —;the hunger, the darkness —•
the pain....the disgrace of these things....lt was
wrong of me. Worse things might have happened
to your old friend, dear, than Two Years’ hard
labor —terrible though it were... .Suffering is a
terrible fire, it either purifies or destroys. Perhaps
I may be a better fellow after it all. Do write to
me here —Monsieur Sebastian Melmoth is my name
now to the world, with love and gratitude.—Ever
yours, Oscar.
Mrs. Beere was a god-daughter of Thack-
eray. She possessed 31 of Thackeray’s
sketches, which were sold in four batches
for £250.
“Hamer” : The melodramatic public will
take almost any line which is given with
sufficient biff, but the strangest bit of “fat”
I ever met was at a suburban dramatic show
where a scratch crowd was working off
“Peeled Onions” or something similar. The
company gallantly spiced the ordinary
stewed tripe of the “bellow” with seasoning
stripped from every known drama, and the
applause from an unusually full house was
frequent and thunderous. It was the old-
man character who got the most thunder,
assisted, probably, by the fact that the
metropolitan papers were just then trying
to define the meaning of “wowser,”
and the word was extremely topical. He
was listening, heartbroken and astounded,
to the villain’s outline of his son’s aw'ful
crimes, when suddenly he bravely drew him-
self up and thundered: “No! No! By God,
sir. My son may have robbed the bank;
he may have stolen the papers; he may
have wronged all these in-no-cent women;
he may even have murdered Gray and
throttled the baby. But, by heavens, sir!
he is not a wowser!” The lady next to me
dropped her bag of peanuts and smashed
her hands wildly together. The stout, grey-
whiskered man on the other side banged his
umbrella upon the floor a dozen times, and
chuckled “Good ! Good !” And amongst the
babel of assorted noise that nearly blew the
foof off, a shrill feminine voice yelled,
“That’s one for you, you beast!” Oh, yes.
It “went big,” that play.
“O. Blin” : H. A. Vachell, mentioned in a
recent Bulletin, must be the most prolific
of present-day dramatists. In addition to
“Quinney’s,” “Searchlights,” and “The Case
of Lady Camber,” he has. completed two
others entitled “The Chief” and “Mr.
Parker.” The last-named has been acquired
for the U.S. by Cyril Maude, and “The
Chief,” which is alleged to have been written
in three nights, to take the author’s mind
off a private bereavement, is George Alexan-
der’s next offering at London St. James’s.
Considering that “Quinney’s” is repeating
its London success in the provinces, while
“Searchlights” has found a good friend in
Mrs. Pat Campbell in America, Yachell’s
income should be swelling nicely. When the
last mail left he was receiving offers for the
Dutch and Scandinavian rights of his produc-
tions.
“D.F.”: With half a dozen picture theatres
Hobart, with a population somewhere about
40,000, ought to be fully stocked for a small
place in these hard times, but still another
one to hold 2300 people has been erected.
Jt is to be called the Strand, and the owner,
E. J. Miller, has just come back from
IT.S.A. with the latest notions in seating and
other internal arrangements.
“F.N.T.”: The Janson contralto, after a
year and a half of watching her fellow-
musicians at patriotic work, has been
attempting a shrewd smack at the Musicians’
Union. She came to Australia a dozen
years ago as a grand-opera singer. The
outlook seemed promising, so she stayed
here, presently becoming established as a
singing-teacher at the University Conserva-
torium, from which safe position she is able
to regard the Union with a defiant and un-
disciplined eye. The other day, at the Jan-
son studio, a little gathering of musicians
considered the question of taking part in
charitable and patriotic entertainments; and
decided that, instead of being patriotic for
nothing, they should ask for a reduced fee in
all cases, “such fees to be a fixed sum for all
performers.” Still, the laborer is worthy of
his bit; and the consistent attitude taken up
by the organised professional-musician has
always been that a full fee for a full day s
work is a fair thing—either that or the
performance should be a free one altogether,
given as a charity. Plenty of bricklayers
have gladly given a share of “patriotic”
labor for nothing, of course, and so have
musicians; but it is simple sweating to as t
a bricklayer to work for 6s. Bd. a day, or a
singer to warble for half-fees at any sort ot
concert where the performers are _ paid.
Sweating in the cause of charity is an
ancient, disreputable business for which
manv inexcusable excuses have been made,
and there should be none of it in “patriotic
work.
“Kaniva”: Parson George Gladstone has
preached his last sermon against dancing. A
few years back he started a crusade, against
the ancient diversion at Nathalia (Vic.), and
at once became a recreation to the ungodly.
His parents, Edward (George) and Caroline
Gladstone, came out to Melbourne m 1857 to
ioin G. V. Brooke, and played for a long
period. Gladstone, an all-round, careful
actor, was the Horatio to numberless Ham-
lets. He died 16 years ago, aged 80. Mrs
Gladstone has been quartered at the Uid
Colonists’ Hbmes for 25 years, and at 8o is
a marvel of activity. Speaking about her
clerical son’s hatred of dancing, she said:
“What was he thinking about! I was the
Columbine at the Windsor Theatre, London,
in 1853.” The Leopolds, Harry, George,
Tom and Fannie, otherwise Mrs. H. Leo-
pold, came out on the same boat with the
Gladstones.
A real war play has got on to the London
boards at last. It is a thrilling one-acter
entitled “War Mates,” by Herbert le Hamel,
a private in the London Scottish. The author
fought on the Western Front and his little
drama really wafts the mouldy smell of the
trenches over the footlights. Le Hamel is
sharing the applause with a young, hitherto-
unknown actor named Sidney Vautier, who
plays the leading part. Probably, however,
the unique piece will be artistically ruined
by some hungry manager tempting its builder
to stretch it into a three-acter.
“0.K.”: The Australian Vaudeville
Artists’ Federation dreams of getting the
Commonwealth Government to bring foreign
variety artists within the scope of the Con-
tract Immigrants Act, or in other words, to
safeguard local “talent” of the commonplace
sort from the competition of imported ditto.
Minister Mahon, when appealed to on the
subject, merely said that he didn’t think
any action could be v taken at present. He
might as well have said at once that vaude-
ville artists never can, or should be, more
deserving of protection than local books,
pictures, statuary, etc. The Australian
vaudevillain is more favorably situated than
the local novelist. His work is presented
to the public on level terms with the im-
ported stuff, whereas the writer can’t get a
local publisher to produce his stuff on level
terms with the foreign works. The A.V.A.F
doesn’t dream of closing the market against
Harry Lauder or Little Tich, or any other
“star” from Europe, but it maintains that
in general, the Australian performer, if
afforded a reasonable opportunity, could give
as much satisfaction as an imported artist.
The audacity of that “if!” What lack of
opportunity did Australian Fred Bluett have
to complain of? Or Vera Pearce, or Vaude
and Verne, or Fanny Powers, or a hundred
and one others? The local vaudeville per-
former gets all the opportunity which is
denied to the local actor, who is too often
restricted to an unimportant part, whereas
the performer at the halls creates his own
part.
“C. Ockney”: “Fax” (B. 17/2/16) is
not correct when he asserts that the silly
little ballad, “My Little Grey Home in the
West,” was made in America. It happens
to be a peculiarly English product. The
words are by Eardley Wilmot (most prob-
ably a spectacled Brixton lady who writes
Christmas cards and cracker “mottoes” for
a living), and the composer is the possessor
of a fine old British name—Herman Lohr.
This gentleman was quite, one of the most
popular makers of English drawing-room
songs before the war.
“Bluebeard”: Of war plays, Lechmere-
Worrall and Harold Terry’s “The Man Who
Stayed at Home” seems to be having the
greatest vogue. It lias been done lately in
America and Australasia, and has now been
played at The Bouffes Parisiens, Paris,
under the name of “Kit,” wdth Max Dearly
in the name part. The piece has not been
altered in the main, but the German woman
spy wears a rankly awful make-up. In the
reconciliation between the hero and his girl
pyjamas and a nightgown are worn and the
curtain falls on the line, “Chaperon be
damned.”
“Quill”: A London reviewer, apparently
trembled by the fact that if a man goes to
the theatre there he sees “Pass the Mustard”
or “The Girl in a Bathing Suit” or some-
thing of the sort, while in Berlin the Hun
has choice of “Macbeth” or “Hamlet,” con-
cludes that “the reason for Shakspeare not
having become in any large sense a national
institution in England is explained by so
very little being known about him person-
ally.” What nonsense! Who knows any-
thing about tfle writer of “Oysters for Two”
or the composer of “The Rooster’s Honey-
moon?” Who wants to know 7 ? In the course
of a misspent life I have seen at least a fair
proportion of the Yank and the French and
the Scottish and all the other plays and
farces and bellows and dress and lighting
effects produced this side of the wmrld, and
I am blessed if I can remember the titles of
half a dozen, recollect a single line of dia-
logue, or write without mistake the names
of a couple of the authors. Also, I would
like to bet that, apart from “Harrison 0.”
and one or two other specialists, everyone
else is in the same joyful condition of mind.
One doesn’t want to know something about
Shakspeare in order to make a pilgrimage
to the theatre to see someone play the devil
with “The Merchant of Venice,” or be
steeped in information about Steele Rudd
before flocking to see Dad on his selection.
HER LOWEST TERMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Much shaving had his cheeks made blue,
With fire his eye was filled;
Unto the hash-house ma’am he spoke,
In tones that many simple folk
Had heard but to be thrilled:
“For actors, ma’am,’’ he cleared his throat,
“Will you your lowest terms please quote?”
The look the hash-house keeper gave,
If looks could lacerate,
On any hide would leave a scar 1
“My lowest terms for actors are” —•
Her voice was charged with hate;
And, oh! the terms she used ’twere meet
No decent person should repeat!

G.E. FLOW METERS in the POWER PLANT
The largest steam plants in the world use G.E. Flow Meters.
The smallest plants need them even more than the big ones, because the savings are greater in
proportion to the investment.
In well=managed plants the steam Flow Meter is a necessity and not a luxury. On Boilers it shows
the output in so that the Engineer can see when the Boiler is loafing, needs cleaning
or overhauling.
Losses which waste coal will be quickly shown up.
G.E. Flow Meters are also made for measuring the flow of water, air or gas.
