NEURALGIA AND POOR BLOOD.
There are very few eases of neuralgia
that are not caused by- a thin, run-down
condition of the blood. Almost the first
thing the doctor looks for in neuralgic
patients is the anaemic appearance.
There are two things to do for any form
of neuralgia. Apply heat to the affected
part to soothe the pain and feed thj nerves
by taking a tonic containing the elements
needed by them. Heat as afforded by hot
flannel, hot-water bags, etc., lulls the in-
flamed nerves into temporary quiet. It does
not cure. Not until the nutrition of the
nerves is restored will the inflammation sub-
side and the neuralgic state be remedied.
Never under any circumstances should drugs
to deaden the pain be used.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills furnish to the
blood the needed elements, and the blood
<ionveys them to the nerves. The only way
of getting food or medicine to the nerves is
through the blood. Remember that.
Neuralgia is a general term. It means
inflammation of a nerve. It is given differ-
ent names according to the nerve affected.
Sciatica is neuralgia of the sciatic nerve.
Applications of heat will soothe the pain in
all forms of neuralgia, and Dr. 'Williams’
Pink Pills will correct the condition that
causes it.
If you have neuralgia you can send to
your dealer at once for box of Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills. A useful booklet on
“Diseases of the Nervous System” will be
sent free to any address by the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Sydney.— Aovt.
HOW TO AVOID SICK HEADACHE.
Many people suffer at regular intervals
from sick headache. Generally they can tell
hours in advance when an attack is coming
on. The trouble is caused by accumulation
of poison in the digestive organs, and when
a certain point is reached this poison pro-
duces nausea, and the attack follows.
If sufferers would take a dose of Pink-
ettes, the new laxative, at intervals these
distressing attacks may be avoided. Pink-
ettes are dainty little sugar-coated ovals
that arouse no feeling of repulsion, and they
act so gently that they are the ideal laxative
to use in correcting a tendency to sick head-
ache. They have no unpleasant effect to
make them objectionable to the extra-
sensitive, siek-headache patient. Old-
fashioned, harsh purgatives will not do at all
in such cases. If you would like to try them
send for a free sample to the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Sydney, mentioning that you
read of them in The Bulletin. Sold
everywhere, Is. per bottle. — Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A NEW WEAPON.
HE : “ You see, it was a ricochet that did it.”
SHE: “ Good gracious! What will they use
next?”
It was 25 years on March 2 since
Henry Parkes took the chair at the opening
of the Federation Convention in Sydney, at
which gathering the draft Commonwealth
Bill was adopted for submission to the State
Legislatures. Of the 45 delegates at that
Convention, only 14 are now alive.
Premier Holman’s latest excursion into
the field of what passes for “democratic legis-
lation” is represented by a Bill amending
the law of defamation to provide that pro-
ceedings may be taken in a court of sum-
mary jurisdiction against a newspaper which
calls anyone offensive names or publishes an
“insulting” drawing of the said anyone.
Apparently Holman has got tired of being
called down by the Labor press, and is going
CANUTE.
Another clause of the amendment would provide
that the publisher of a paper printing insulting
words would be liable to the same penalty as a
person who spoke insulting words in the street.
(Laughter.)
Mr. Levy: This is aimed at preventing criticism of
public men. (Hear, hear.) — Item, from debate in
N. S. Wales Assembly on Defamation Bill.
“He set his chair on the seashore and com-
manded the leaves to retire.”
Historical Note.
to put a stop to it, which, all things con-
sidered, is natural enough in him. But if
he were to give less occasion for harsh re-
marks he would be a lot happier than he
Mull be by sitting with the trigger of the
new Act cocked. This paper has doubts
whether Holman would prosecute anyone
who called him a defender of the
Labor platform, or Davy Hall in-
voke the law to silence a paper
which spoke of him as a Heaven-inspired
genius, or Dick Meagher drag an editor into
court for calling him an ornament to the
public life of the State, for by no stretch of
imagination could even a police-court beak
make himself believe that these terms were
insulting. There would be too much evi-
dence from the other side to show that when
used even at Caucus meetings they were
not looked upon as an overstatement of the
case.
“F.N.T.”: It should come as a relief to
the peaceful and law-abiding to learn that
the Australian branch of the Anarchists’
Federation is dead, or at least missing.
Some time ago, on reading in the press that
a delegate from the Federation had attended
a Sabbath meeting at the Bijou Theatre,, I
addressed a letter to the secretary of the
concern, asking him to oblige me with a copy
of the Federation’s constitution. I wrote
too late. The passing of the brotherhood
must have occurred almost at once, for my
letter was presently returned to me by the
Postal Department, after due search had
been made —no Federation, alive or dead,
could be discovered. Maybe the Federation
has gone to get its hand in by a little
grenading at the Front.
Thirty years ago, when the late W. T.
Stead was agitating for an increase in the
strength of the British Navy, General Wol-
seley wrote to him from the War Office :
What we want most is that the whole truth should
be told to the nation. We live in an age of cant and
lies and humbug, and it is difficult to make men
trust the people as they should do by telling them the
simple truth about our army and navy.
A quarter of a century later Lord Roberts
talked himself hoarse in advocating con-
scription, and the Government merely called
him an alarmist, whilst the people of "Britain
continued to think that the army didn’t mat-
ter as long as the navy was all right. The
Government never dreamed of trustfully tell-
ing the people the whole truth. The Oppo-
sition would have denounced the whole
truth as an entire lie, and maybe would have
pushed the Government out of office. No
Government is so lost to all sense of party
decency that it will take that risk.
Premier Vaughan, of S.A., and ex-
Premier Peake are keeping the grand old
party game alive during recess by 1000-word
“open letters” which are read more or less
by the public. These compositions are
quite courteously framed. “May I be
allowed to point out, with all deference, that
you have omitted to supply any evidence in
support of these statements?” Across the
floor of the House that mass of elegant
verbiage would he condensed into three
words —or possibly into one! “Flippancy”
is about the worst crime of which the
writers have, so far, accused each other.
That seems to be about all they are capable
of in the affable circumstances.
Kearsley, M.L.A., put in a word the
other day for “compensatioh for the holders
of licenses for N.S.W. railway refreshment
rooms,” which, this year, will be taken over
and riin by the Railway Department. The
refreshment rooms have hitherto been leased
to people who had the idea, apparently, that
because the tea-and-bun traders have been
allowed to run a profitable business in a
casual way, the public, after paying through
the nose individually, should shell out more
cash collectively for the privilege of dis-
turbing a good thing. It’s a brilliant idea,
but its lease of life is precarious.
“F.N.T.”: Frank Madden, Speaker of the
Vic. Legislative Assembly, has resurrected a
pet land-settlement scheme of his which he
considers suitable for soldiers. He pro-
poses to put down bores in the Heytesbury
forest, in the midst of 320-acre blocks. Each
block will provide selections for four sol-
diers, and, of each selection, 15 acres touch-
ing the bore will have a cottage built on it
and be ploughed, drained and planted with
apples. The soldier will have £1 a week
above his pension from the Government dur-
ing the first year; and for two years after-
wards, 10s. a week, “while the apple trees
are coming into bearing.” Then he is to have
30 years in which to pay his land off. The
remaining 65 acres of virgin land in each
selection, the sort that has hitherto been
considered too poor for selection, will be
converted by the soldier’s own labor into
“pasture for a horse, a few cows,” etc.
There is something wrong with the adminis-
trative equipment of a man who proposes to
put partially-disabled soldiers on land which
admittedly is of little value, expecting them
to get a living for themselves and families
from apple-trees planted three years, in the
meantime existing on 10s. a week. Of course
they might save enough money to tide them
over trouble from the £1 a week of the first
year, after paying for the horse, few cows,
etc., but it’s doubtful. Improvidence is
ingrained in some people.
HOT STUFF.
PENPUSH : “I fear that I have offended your
sister, Miss Brown. ”
MISS BROWN: “Oh?”
PENPUSH: “Yes, I sent her a copy of my
latest novel, and she sent it hack and
told me to use it as a footwarmer!”
“D.F.”: According to a resident of those
parts Private Enterprise has a good thing
going in a small way on some of the Bass
Strait islands. He alleges that the State
Government leases one island for grazing
purposes at £lO per year and that
the lessee lifts £GO per year from
the half-castes and others for the right to
collect the young of the mutton-birds that
breed, but certainly do not graze, on the
island. So the lessee gets a clear £5O per
year out of this industry and has the graz-

THE ODDS.
THE PLUNGER: “ What price Paris?"
THE BOOKMAKER: “ Write your own ticket.”
Some moderately candid official remarks on
Sweden’s dealings with Germany were made
in the House of Commons the other day.
Nevil Primrose stated as evidence of Scan-
dinavian consignees being in collusion with
the German Government that several
Swedish ships heavily laden with grain and
oil had put themselves in the way to be “cap-
tured” by German warships. It was so easy
for any vessels not to be cap-
tured by German warships that
the proceedings in these cases
hardly admitted of a doubt. So
Nevil Primrose said that if it
occurred again, “special meas-
ures might be necessary.” But
he didn’t say what special meas-
ures he meant. Perhaps he con-
templates a prohibitive duty on
Swedish lucifer matches.
Victoria and South Australia
are arising to shatter the State-
frights bogey. They are laying
down at their joint expense a
cross-border line from Mount
Gambier (S.A.) to Portland
(V.), and the latter’s splendid
harbor will serve, as nature in-
tended it should, as the port of
much of the south-east of the
adjoining State. S.A. Premier
Crawford Vaughan has turned
the limelight on the scheme by
travelling over much as is
finished of the new linking line,
in company with the mayors of
the two border towns and the
local Parliamentary representatives of Vic.
and S.A. Meanwhile Adelaide shipping cos.
are uttering hollow groans.
Last week while-the titanic struggle at
Verdun held the attention of a waiting world
and the hour at which Sydney’s beer is to
be cut off at the meter was one of the un-
solved problems of the democracy, the
N.S.W. Assembly met for the transaction of
business after several months’ spell. And
immediately the Sergeant at Arms had waved
Richard Meagher into the Speaker’s throne
•f. C. Fitzpatrick rose to the occasion and
fiercely demanded to know whether
The Minister of Labor will cause inquiry into
the facts of the case of a woman who was fined
recently at Bondi for having sold after 6 p.m. a
pound of candles!
Fitzparrot has just been appointed Liberal
Whip in place of Moxham, dead, and his
responsibilities sit heavily upon him.
CONVERTED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I much rejoice at the loyalty to death of the sons
of Brandenburg displayed in the irresistible storm-
ing attack on the enemy’s strongest fort. God bless
Brandenburg and the entire Fatherland.
—Bill the Butcher.
I refrain from vicious drinking,
I am sparing in my food;
With a bumbled heart I’m thinking
That the war has done me good.
It is well to hate the nation
Which has wrought the world’s distress;
So I hope to win salvation
Through the Kaiser’s wickedness.
Oh, this war comes as a sermon
Breathing Christianity;
Yea, the sermon preached in German
Stirs the very soul of me.
I was not a friend of Labor
(Having neither toiled nor spun),
And I did not love my neighbor—•
Till I learned to hate the Hun.
I can love the homeless workers
Of the poorest human race;
I can trust that lazy shirkers
May be brought at last to grace.
I have turned away from evil,
And its followers I shun
Since I recognise the Devil
In the gospel of the Hun.
Now young Billjim is my brother—
He at whom I used to scoff—
I could kiss him for his mother
When I see him marching off
To the troopship, never speaking
Of a profit or reward,
From the perils he is seeking
In the service of the Lord.
When the Button-Day box rattles
On behalf of brave Billjim,
Who was game to fight my battles
At the risk of life and limb,
I review the years behind me
With an old man’s vain regret,
And my prayer is, “God remind me
If I ever should forget.”
I have craved for selfish pleasure—^
But the craving is subdued —■
And to-day my choicest treasure
Is a sense of gratitude.
I have found a Faith supernal
In the firm and fixed belief
That there is a King eternal
Who will bring the Hun to grief.
Hell and furies! Donnerwetterl
’Tis the lesson of the war
That has made me so much better
Than I ever was of yore.
From the sermon of the sabre
And the all-destroying gun
We can learn to love our neighbor
As we hate the gory Hun.
VICTORIA.

KINTA VALLEY TIN-DREDGING, LIMITED, BATU GAJAII, FEDERATED MALAY STATES,

THIS COMPANY has been formed' for the purpose of acquiring and working by modern bucket
dredges tin-bearing lands at Batu Gajah,' State of Perak, Federated Malay States.
SITUATION and AREA: 609 acres, with rights over 515 acres. In all 1124 acres of tin-bearing land
secured. In addition to which, application has been made for rights over the river bed of the Kinta
River and the islands between, this application being recommended by the Kinta Lands Office to the
Resident of Perak for his approval, with every probability of same being granted. The property is
situated about half a mile from the town of Batu Gajah and between the Penang-Singapore Railway and
the Tandjong Tualong Road, with the Ivintra River running right through the property.
Exhaustive reports have been furnished by Messrs. A. E. Thomas, W. Cock, C. Hollyoak, F. W. Payne
and Wm. Kellie Smith. An area of 207 acres has been bored by the Klian Ivellas, Ltd., showing values
of tin oxide, 78 acres averaging lilb., 139 acres averaging over lib., and check boring of 100 acres
bv Mr. Thomas proved average value of 1.211 b. to the cubic yard. Since then Mr. Cock has closely
bored, proving the average of such 100 acres to be 1.221 b. Thus the ground has been carefully bored
three times, and each boring has fully confirmed the previous boring and proved the ground to be highly
payable. The area of tin-mining lands acquired by the Company is (allowing for the river bed)
approximately 1180 acres, 207 acres containing 14,000,000 cubic yards, with tin oxide even at £165 per
ton equals £495,833 gross value of tin oxide to be recovered. With a dredge turning over 100,000 cubic
yards monthly, recovering lib. tin oxide of 79 per cent, metallic, at a working cost of 34d. per cubic
yard, and with tin at £165 per ton, a profit of £2149 a month is shown, or £25,788 per year, equal
to 35 per cent, per annum on a capital of £70,000.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The “books,” as expected, viewed the New-
market as a very open race, and, having been well
fed with early investments on favorites that were no
longer in demand, the market-makers offered 10 to 1
bar one at the start. Maharajah at 7’s was the
barred one. Of the others, Amata perhaps carried
more money than anything else, small punters, as a
rule, getting only 8 to 1 for their half-sovereigns.
The least backed of the old fancies at the finish
was Traquette, who is now said to be slightly out
of gear in the wind. When the barrier went up no
prad rushed away into a commanding lead, con-
sequently the time —lmin. 15sec. —for the full jour-
ney was slower than usual. Woorak, the mad pace-
maker in so many previous sprints, behaved soberly
enough this time, but he was always one of the fore-
most. After going a furlong the leaders were
Maharajah, Eurobin, Red Signal, Burrabadeen, lown-
it and Woorak, with Tringa, La Steppe and Amata
not far behind. Three furlongs from home
Eurobin, Maharajah and Red Signal appeared to be
still a trifle ahead of the rest, but the latter (who
looked rather overdone) and Maharajah were soon
out of it. Eurobin, however, remained distinctly at
the head of affairs until the distance post was passed.
Then something seemed to happen to the handsome
gelding, for he failed as he had never failed in faster
run races. Woorak was the first to beat him. Then
came the honest Amata, running as straight as a
gun-barrel in the centre of the course, and the name
of the mare was yelled to Heaven. It seemed, too,
that lownit would catch AVoorak, but the
perky little stallion could get no nearer than third.
A length and a half, and a neck, were between the
placed horses.
It was not the Amata of a year ago, or even
the mare who won the last race of the V.R.C. Spring
meeting that did the big trick on Saturday-. Amata,
always improving, had made wonderful progress
these last two months. She is not a big one, but
she’s a true daughter of Wallace, and the children
of Wallace are slow to reach their maturity of
strength and speed. Not until she beat Woorak at
Caulfield on the Saturday previous to the Newmarket
did Amata look at all like a good thing for the
coming event. Rumors of her having “come on”
amazingly were received with suspicion. Much the
same thing had been said before she went to Rand-
wick in September to win the Epsom mile, which
proved a cakewalk for Woorak. Her owner, W. Lan-
dale, before going to England for fighting purposes,
had begged his friends to back Amata for the Epsom.
The mare’s uninitiated admirers were a little too
previous with their confidence. It was only of late
that very shrewd heads felt cocksure about the mare’s
improvement.
lownit, of course, was the wonder horse of the
Newmarket. He had run first and second in the
two previous years, and now, after doing the State
some service at the stud, the son of Grdfter ran a
good third. Sprint races are for the speediest
sprinters, no matter whether they be quite wound-up
or otherwise. lovvnit’s demeanor in the saddling-
paddock before the event suggested that in his
opinion he was at a horse-show rather than a race-
meeting. But when he got to the barrier the thoughts
of the old warhorse were on the real business.
The V.R.C. Leger race was remarkable for the
swerving of Westeourt when asked to overtake
Patrobas in the last 150 yards! Heavens, how he
swerved! Even Patrobas swerved a little, but he
was a straight goer compared with his old rival.
The time —3min. 3sec. —has only once been beaten
for the lj miles classic race at Flemington.
The Essendon Stakes (12 furlongs) was a remark-
ably fast go between Giru and Carlita, with Cyklon
putting in a great run at the finish. The time—
2min. 324 sec a see. slower than Comedy King’s
record of five years ago, the timing on which occa-
sion has been questioned. Ciru made most of the
running, Carlita taking the lead from the improved
son of Antonis when they were rounding into the
straight. Then Giru came again and fairly wore the
mare down. She had suffered from a blister in the
mouth earlier in the week, and maybe it felt some-
what sore on the bit, hut the performance
proved Giru to be a greatly improved animal. The
Essendon and Leger times being so fast the slowness
of the Newmarket is the more noticeable. Cyklon
had run in the Newmarket, by the way, before he
was stripped for the w.f.a. If they could get him as
fit as the proverbial fiddle Cyklon would be an abso-
lute topnotcher over any distance up to 12 furlongs,
or even further. But the animal that won races in
England and Germany seems to have * weak spots
about him, and has not been worked up to top
pitch.
* * * •
Horses sent across the Murray did better
than was expected at Flemington, appropriating the
stakes in three events and supplying the runner-up
in two. Wolaroi, winner of the Sires’ Produce
Stakes, is the property of a Bondi Junction meat mer-
chant, Dan Seaton. Lingle, the Brunswick Stakes win-
ner, is owned by Charlie Horan, a Kensington man. As
Horan planks down the money in solid lumps when
the provocation is sufficient, being one of Australia’s
biggest punters, the Noses must have had a Turkish
bath when the colt streaked to the front and won by
three lengths from Two Blues. Two Blues paid for
his oats on the previous Saturday when he took the
Trial Stakes at Caulfield.
* » • *
Maoriland is being exploited for horses to
race at the Associated Racing Club’s meetings round
Sydney. In the last few weeks Briseis, a mare from
the Rickety Isles, has done very well. She won, in
turn, Maiden, Encourage and Approved Handicaps
before tackling Flying events, two of which she
secured. Another Maorilander, Lady Elvsian, re-
cently won a double on the one day; and Margarita,
a 14-2 prad who distinguished herself among the
little ones by humping huge weights to the front,
beat everything in Moorefteld Flying Handicap last
Saturday. Margarita had previously tried to win
in that class of race and failed. She was greatly
fancied for the last Carrington Stakes, but she as
good as fell down paralysed.
# * * *
Another cheap nag has been doing things
in Loan Land. A few months back the owner of a
nag named Rorke’s Drift sent him down to Inverkeg-
ville with other prads to be sold by auction to
anyone with courage enough to buy. Two local men,
F. A. and V. N. Price, bought the animal for a few
pounds, and promptly won a local race with him. This
left them a lump on the credit side. The other day
Rorke’s Drift was sent up to Dunedin, entered for
the two big events, the Dunedin Cup and the Jockey
Club Handicap. He bagged both. Incidentally, the
owners got a straight-out offer of £lOOO for the
nag, but they said that it was their deaf day.
* * * *
McShortodds would have had a disastrous
time at the recent Dunedin (M.L.) meeting. On the
second day out of eight events favorites arrived home
first on six occasions. Each carried such a load of
cash that a man who had £ 1 on each of the six
achieved 4s. less from the machine than he put into
it.
* * * *
“Evens” : The Jester lias been attending a
fair number of the race meetings held lately in
Maoriland. At the Woodville meeting the Cup was
won by Tangihon, who won the M.L. Cup some three
months previously. At the Manawatu meeting at
Christmas time Tangihon was about 13th favorite
in a field of 14, seven weeks after winning the big-
gest race in the country. He ran later amongst
third raters until he got to Woodville. There
nine accepted for the big event, and Tangihon was
eighth favorite on the machine. He romped home
pa}dng a £2B dividend. During the race
Ladogeur, an alleged moral for the stake, was going
mighty well, but was outed by Roumania crossing
him. Thereafter there was nothing in the road of
Tangihon. Incidentally Roumania’s rider was fined
a fiver.
An imported trotting mare, Queen Patch,
wrecked some starting-price punters’ dreams of
profits at the Manawatu (M.L.) meeting lately. The
animal was brought up from Christchurch, and wads
of money were placed in the right hands to flop on
10 minutes before starting time. She duly won, and
the optimists were heard chuckling from Auckland to
the Bluff. But the commission had been badly
worked. When the totalisator clerks reckoned up
things they found that of 270 tickets on the race
201 were on the winner. After nipping off 12J per
cent, for war tax and 25 per cent, for the second
horse the successful financiers each got 17s. 6d. back
from their sov. Backers of the second horse got
£3 each.
* * * *
The bookmaker has been flourishing in Tas.,
in spite of the tote, for until recently he could not
be convicted unless on proof that he habitually made
a business of betting. The new Suppression of
Betting and Gaming Act has changed all this. The
first prosecutions under it came off at Launceston
the other day, when six odds merchants were told to
pay £2O and costs apiece.
* * # *
The stakes for the two Cup Weeks in
Adelaide are to remain the same as last year, the
S.A.J.C. and Adelaide R.C. together putting up about
£13,000 for the six days. Goodwood Handicap re-
mains at £7OO, City Handicap £750 and the
Adelaide and Birthday Cups will each contain £l5OO.
» * * *
F. E. Jones, a Maoriland horseman, has a
pretty good record in riding to win the Dunedin
Champagne Stakes. He had his first winning mount
on Husbandman in 1908, and since then has won the
race on Sharpshooter, Counterfeit, Brown Owl, Night-
watch, Angelina, Adjutant and Good Hope. In 10
successive years he has been on the winner seven
times, on the second horse twice and on the third
once. Bob Derritt was on top of the winner of this
race nine times between 1883 and 1906.
FRITZ HOLLAND, Middleweight.
Maoriland has said good-bye to another
old racing man—Jimmie Jeffs. Born at Kaiapoi he
lived most of his life in Dunedin, and it was in the
oatmeal city he chiefly won his reputation and
races. His best-known horses were the sprinter
Petrovna, Pipi the ’chaser and the trotter Wilkie.
In the digging days Jeffs saw some stirring times,
and ran the mail coach to Nenthorn during the big
rush there. Two years ago paralysis gripped him.
He was oul} r 63 when he boarded the chariot.
* * * *
The axe fell very lightly on the A.J.C.
(Sydney) autumn meeting prize-money. Large
reductions were foreshadowed, but only the small
events were pruned. The big events—Sydney Cup
( £5000), Sires Produce Stakes ( £3000), Don-
caster Handicap, Champagne Stakes, Autumn Stakes,
All-Aged Stakes, A.J.C. Plate, Cumberland Stakes
(each £2000) remain as in 1915. Altogether
£29,200 will be distributed in 24 events, which
compares favorably with £30,150 in 1915, and
£28,100 in 1914.
* * * *
“H. 0.”: Melbourne Argus has lately been
devoting columns to a discussion of the efficacy of
“prayers for the dead.” As an old-established pun-
ter I have never known them to do the least good.
All the prayers on earth will not make the “dead”
quick.
* * * *
Jimmy Clabby and Fritz Holland, both of
the U.S.A., collided a second time at Sydney Stadium
on Saturday and put up a vimful, scientific scrap.
When they last met James got the verdict. He got
the same thing again on Saturday, and deserved it.
Fritz was well outpointed, and two or three times in
mighty great danger of being outed. He fought a
brainy, vigorous fight, all the same. But flabby
had the greater speed and the heavier wallop, and on
Saturday was just as good as he had ever al^ e "
before. His next engagement is with Darcy —also a
return battle—and if in this Clabbv can manage to
avoid the mistake he made before of trying to fight
the youngster at close quarters he may do better
than on that painful occasion.
* * *
“Knock-out” Brown and Albert Lloyd
provided the feast of stoush at Melbourne
Stadium on Saturday night. As Browns reputa-
tion as a sleep-dispenser had started to silt up, he
went in to settle Albert early; but the latter, who
combines agility with bulk, counter-attacked with left
prods to the other fellow’s ingrowing nose, and then
either debouched or clinched. A heavy right hook in
the sixth upset Australia’s calculations and the judge
granted an injunction in the following round, with
costs against Lloyd.
* * * *
“Red” Watson settled Fred Delaney’s
optimism at Brisbane on Saturday in about one hit.
Before the first round was over Delaney’s second had
heaved the towel .in, and the referee had mentioned
to Watson that the massacre had lasted long enough.
There is afoot an agitation to bring
Griffo back to Australia. He is said to be as right
as ever in his career. How that can be surpasses
understanding. The present generation of Australian
stoush enthusiasts know him only by name, and he
would be better off left at that.
“Cleido” : That libel perpetrated by “Sar-
donyx” on present-day scrapping (B. 10/2/’l6) labels
him as one of those hoary old sinners who can see
no merit in things of to-day. “Nothing like we had
in the old days,” is their cry. What a rotten cry it
is, too. The glove-specialist of to-day is a better
man any way you take him than the old-time basher
the patriarchs of the hour prate about. Why ?
Because the science of boxing has no more deterior-
ated than the science of war, of loan-raising, of horse-
racing, or of athletics of any description. From
time to time you find horses clipping down existing
time figures and athletes establishing records in
various pastimes. The same applies to the boxer.
The evolution of in-fighting has equipped him to-day
with the means of greater offensive ability, while he
has also incomparably more scientific methods of
preparation. All this means more fighting service,
perfect physical condition, more strength and greater
powers of endurance. The rules governing the game
also provide the pug with gloves to protect his hands,
and the minute interval between rounds to recuperate
and continue the contest with a full head of steam
on. The direct result is that there is more fight
crammed into a round of three minutes to-day than
into half a dozen of those old affairs that sometimes
lasted a few seconds, and at other times were long-
drawn-out meetings which, after the first few
minutes, degenerated into a shoving match, with an
occasional trip, or stage fall to end the round. In
those long-drawn-out affrays men would go down to a
tap that wouldn’t have disturbed the billiard Cinque-
valli balanced on his chin. Present conditions are
far more exacting, and a boxer who gets through 20
rounds as they are usually fought to-day requires
great powers of endurance —that is, to the observer
who takes an ordinary size in ears.
* * ♦ *
“Sardonyx”: Numerous recent references
to Tom Sayers have reminded me that I once saw
that famous warrior strip for examination by a party
of prominent medical men. Sayers, a trifle under
five-feet-eight, weighed only 1471 b., but he was a
beautifully-built man, whose compact and powerful
upper-works were accentuated by a waist-line which
was perhaps a little too fine, and which, coupled
with an apparent lack of muscular development, gave
a misleading suggestion of delicacy to one of the
toughest and ruggedest fighters that ever entered a
ring. He showed none of the surface-ridges and
bunches usually characteristic of the athlete, but the
muscle was there, nevertheless; evenly developed in
ever\ r part, and rippling all over the man the moment
he put his hands up. Sayers was the first to demon-
strate the soundness of the newly-arisen notion that
a clever and hard-hitting middleweight was “good
enough for anything that walked on two legs.” The
knowing-ones laughed this heresy to scorn, and made
very merry at the expense of the few prominent
sports who backed Sayers to take the championship
of England from the redoubtable “Tipton Slasher,”
a 15-stone 74-incher. The Slasher himself was hugely
amused at the little man’s temerity, and said that
if he could only get one home on Sayers, there would
be “no more Tom!” In the course of the fight he
did get one home —a terrific smash that made every-
body think Sayers was killed. But Tom managed to
come up to time, and after that the big fellow never
had a chance, being hammered to helplessness and
finally knocked senseless by four successive admin-
istrations of Tom’s celebrated “auctioneer.” ’
* * * *
“Kaniva”: “Eryander” (B. 24/2/T6) is
mistaken in assigning four fights between “Bendigo”
and Caunt. He says his father was present at the
last of these encounters in ’45. This was really the
third and last meeting in the ring of these men.
The first was in 1835, when Caunt lost through hit-
ting Bendigo while the latter was on his knees. The
second affray was in ’3B. In this, during the 13th
round, Caunt squeezed the other in such a powerful
grasp against the ropes that he stood a big chance of
being killed if someone had not cut them. At the
38th term a magistrate got near the ring and lectured
the multitude in vain. In the 50th Caunt alleged
that he had been kicked while on the ground. The
scene beggared description, and the sticks of the
“Nottingham lambs” did much damage in the mix-
up about the ring. Caunt eventually won, Bendigo
dropping without a blow. The latter in old age
turned to preaching, and one day meeting an erst-
while noble patron he told him, “He was now fight-
ing Satan——and, behold, the victory shall be mine.”
“I hope so,” said his friend. “But fight him more
fairly than you did Ben Caunt, or I may change my
side.”

What looks like the biggest athletic carni-
val Australia has seen starts to-morrow night
at the instigation of Snowy Baker in Svdney Stadium
and will be continued next day at the Agricultural
Grounds, where peds and cyclists and horses will be
given a show. Darcy and McGoorty are to swap
compliments to begin with, and old time Larry
Foley is going to show Jimmy Clabby a point or
two, and there will be high diving by Aleck Wick-
ham and Fanny Durack and much other strenuous
excitement. All the proceeds to the Light Horse
Comforts Fund.

“Mulga”: I have seen Giffen, Gregory,
Graham, Noble, Trumper, Duff and others plav
magnificent innings in Test matches, but two
innings of Clem Hill s will do me. One, on the
Melbourne C. C. Ground, the Australians being five
or six wickets down for 40. Hill, then a mere
youth, on being joined by Trumble, whacked Richard-
son and co. all over the ground to the tune of 181.
The other at Adelaide a few years ago. Hill
although suffering from a severe attack of nausea
and influenza, batted grandly for 160, and pulled the
match out of the fire. Hartigan made a splendid
stand with Hill in this innings, obtaining a trip to
■Lng’.Uuia through it. Personally, although I always
considered Trumper a more spectacular batsman
than Hill, yet if great batsmen are taken by great
deeds in great matches, I consider Hill the greater
of the two.

“Ringbolt”: “Spinnaker” (B. 24/2/16)
has voiced an observation 1 have often heard made
by windjamming mariners, who have also frequently
been heard to wonder why such enthusiasts as the
Sydney yachtsmen confine themselves practically to
one rig—or, at all events, to one type, the single
master. I have never seen here that most beautiful
cf all rigs, the schooner; the yawl is very scarce
and the ketch, as a yacht,' non-existent. Let nobody
judge, from the appearance of our clumsy and lub-
berly coasting-ketches, of what a ketch-yacht looks
like, with tautly-stayed masts, well-cut and well-
stretched white canvas, and smartly-rove run-
ning gear. The ketch is by far the best rig for
cruising, because her short main-boom is much
handier than the cutter’s, or even than the yawl’s
and her mizzen, unlike the yawl’s foolish little' after-
sail, is big enough to hold her well to windward
under itself and the head-sails only. .Under these
three sails, indeed, the ketch works well upon any
helm ; and, for evidence that the rig is the best for
heavy weather we need go no further than the fact
that the British, French and Dutch fishing-craft—
the world’s best sea-boats —are all ketches. 1 have
often heard surprise expressed that the lug-rig j s
never used for yachts of any considerable size. The
dipping-lugger is certainly both fast and Weatherly,
but the sail, when of large size, is a very awkward
one to handle when beating to windward, because at
every tack it has to be lowered-away and passed to
the other side of the mast—an operation requiring
great caution and careful steering in anything like
a stiff breeze.
* * * *
Frank de Beaurepaire, ex-champion
swimmer of Victoria, who had worked up to a lieu-
tenancy in the A.I.F. and turned his thoughts to
Gallipoli, acquired appendicitis last August and his
plans were upset. In September the Y.M.C.A. asked
the public for funds with which to do some sort of
work on the transports. The result was satisfac-
tory, and several young men have since been sent to
Egypt as Y.M.C.A. secretaries, starting with the
troops from various ports. Four have gone from
Melbourne and Beaurepaire was one of them.

Motor-cycle road records come and go.
Last week Jack Booth (Vic.) startled official time-
keepers by flying over half a mile of highway in
18 4-ssec. —95 miles 1310 J yards per hour and a
world’s record.
* * * ♦
Erwin G. Baker, the Yankee motor-cyclist
who recently fractured some records in Vic-
toria, a couple of years ago whizzed from
west to east of United States (3378 miles) in
276 hours —easily the best spin over the long
stretch. Baker is a non-smoker and non-drinker
and turns the beam at 15st., which shows the
quality of the Indian to carry weight at record
speed.
* # » »
A Victorian motor-cyclist will shortly be
making an attempt to reduce J. Bolger’s 1913 Mel-
bourne-Sydney record of 23hrs. 41min. With the
up-to-date spring-framed twins now available to
speedmen, the wonder is that this record has stood on
the b4fcs so long. With decent luck and weather
conditions the 565 miles should be covered in from
18 to 19 hours.

F. E. Wells, the well-known golf player
and winner of the silver cup at Manly this year pre-
sented by Allen Taylor, M.L.C., has decided to ex-
change the putter for the rifle and the bayonet. His
is a well-known figure on the Manly and Leura links.
In his spare time he is accountant for the Newcastle
Wallsend Coal Company.
“The Master Gravedigger” forwards a few
remarks written on a second-hand coffin lid:
Apropos of the sale of two graves at Rookwood
(Sydney) The Bulletin (2/3/’l6) remarks that few
men buy themselves a burrow. Well, I have the
personal supervision of two large cemeteries and
general oversight for the trustees of many others,
and can assure you that it is a fairly common prac-
tice. Under the model code of regulations recom-
mended by the Department of Lands and adopted by
most religious bodies in N. S. Wales certificates
(transferable) are issued called “exclusive rights,
whereby any person may become the owner of a plot
9ft. x 4 ft. in any particular corner of a boneyard he
fancies. The cost of an interment is as a rule 30s. 10s.
to the clergyman, 10s. to the sexton, and 10s. to the
cemetery. Of course this fee does not carry with it
the ownership of the grave, which at the discretion
of the Trustees after a certain lapse of years may be
reopened. Before decorating it with a monument the
freehold of a grave must be secured, and this may
cosr anything from £1 to £3. It might almost e
said to be the usual practice rather than the ex-
ception for the relatives of the dear departed to take
up two or three graves alongside each other so as to
make a family party of it later on. In the rare
cases where they find they do not require the lan
the collection finds a ready sale. One does not o ten
sec desirable grave sites advertised because, as a rue,
the local undertaker is a ready buyer. y
an interment will take place in a grave yawning or
someone else, and the trustees will have to reim
burse the disappointed owner, who might otherwise
hurl the bones of the claim-jumper into the street.

Kempe and Andrews, lately principal and
vice-principal respectively of the Bush
Brotherhood, are now army chaplains, though
they were too “High” Church for the Sydney
nominators to consider. Kempe was ap-
pointed almost on his arrival in England, and
went to Gallipoli with an English division.
Andrews left Australia as a private in the
A.A.M.C., and was through the campaign
at Anzac. Invalided to England, he got his
coveted chaplaincy with a North of England
lot. It was bad luck they couldn’t have char>-
lained with Australian troops, as both knew
Billjim better than most Australian parsons,
having met him often back o’ sunset. Their
unimportant “highness” offset their valuable
experience.

THE growing activities of our second year’s hostilities
find Avon Tyres in increased demand for H.M. Trans-
port. Tried and proven in the fierce flame of War, as they
have been tried in every sort of civilian service, Avons emerge
as Seasoned Campaigners worthy of the highest place in the
esteem of the motoring public.
cWe are told that this is a war of attrition or exhaustion. If tyres
are tried out to the last limit of endurance we may count upon
British Avons being found among the surviving fittest. They are
running in all theatres of the War, on all sorts of motors —from
H.R.H. The Prince of Wales’ Staff limousine to the humblest
regimental runabout.

It was stated that last week’s attack by
the Germans on Verdun was directed by the
Crown Prince. There are 27 Crown Princes,
the multitudinous Kaiser having been a
polygamist in his youth and kept the matter
secret until the papers let the cat out of the
bag. This is the record of what has hap-
pened to the various Crown Princes with the
watery, tired smile: On August 4, 1914, one
was at the front in command of the Third
Army; on August 5. 1914, another was
nearly murdered at Berlin. A third was
carried to hospital at Aix-la-Chapelle on the
18th. There was something about a Crown
Prince being nearly murdered on the 24th, of
one committing suicide on September 4,
another dying in Brussels on the 13th. Then
in the first big attack launched west of
Metz two days later a Crown Prince was in
command, but was unfortunately blown into
pieces by shrapnel in Poland next morning.
Either a miracle of surgery was performed
or the theory of 27 holds good, because on
October 18 a Crown Prince was wounded in
the Champagne country and the Crown Prin-
cess visited the corpse on the 20th. Then a
Crown Prince’s body was picked up on a
field of battle somewhere on the 25th and
kept until November 4, when it was definitely
put into a hole in the ground. The armj’
corps of Crown Princes seemed to have been
about de.cimated by now, but a bullet passed
one out on November 4, and on the Bth a
further person of the same name went raving
mad. There was a Crown Prince in supreme
command on the Verdun-Esparges front dur-
ing the 18th; there was one wounded to
death on December 17, and after one had been
detected nursing a female on a pub balcony
and another had violently quarrelled with
his wife, he was wounded some more on
January 10. Whereupon he kept quietly
in bed until March 3, and was in
commant of the Turkish army on the 14th,
but was fired next day. and during the next
week was promoted. Since then something
has been happening to him or them every
other afternoon, and if 472 more Crown
Princes are not passed out before Christmas
the reliable daily press will be failing to do
its duty as an impartial chronicler of fact.
“Masarcus”: Cold Tea lost one of its
champions when the Eev. George Gladstone,
of Nathalia (Vic.), died. Twenty years
ago, while he was an Anglican parson at
Nathalia, his temperance sermons were so
fiery that his congregation regarded him as
OA'er the odds. Bishop Goe, on pressure,
introduced Gladstone to another parish; but
the fire-eater refused to calm his appetite for
rockets and blue flares. Dissociating him-
self from the Church of England he opened
another religious shop or arcade, which he
christened splendidly, “The Reformed Church
of England.” To the dismay of his former
employers a large section of his congregation
followed him into the new fold, and, of
course, took the collection-plate with them.
His ex-bosses, reckoning that the cancella-
tion of his license to celebrate marriages
would induce some of the lost sheep to re-
turn, then moved Heaven and earth (other-
wise Chief Sec. Alec Peacock) to extract
the license from him. But H.-an-E. Peacock
put on his reverse gear and went astern.
So Gladstone continued to rivet Bill and
Emily in the holy bonds at the usual rates.
For all his narrowness in one or two direc-
tions Gladstone was unusually openhearted,
and he gave freely of his income, which was
never large.
A remarkable coincidence is related by
Granville Fortescue, who was London Daily
Telegraph’s war correspondent in Warsaw
in the early days of the German attack. The
first air-raid by Wilhelm’s fliers was made
on a Sunday, and as the first shell crashed
into the city the clock on the District Court
chimed out “One!”
Henry Lucy, or his representative, in his
last letter to S. M. Herald, has made
another blunder about a simple matter of
fact: —•
Forty-five years ago France went to war with Ger-
many with a light heart, confident in the supremacy
of its newly-invented weapon, the needle-gun.
This is so inaccurate that the Herald really
ought to have detected and corrected it. The
needle-gun was not France’s weapon at all.
but Prussia’s, and it was not newly invented
45 years ago, because Prussia had then won
two wars with it —against Denmark and
Austria. The French army rifle of ’7l was
so much better than the Prussian weapon
that the Germans never missed a chance of
arming their infantry with it when they
could get a chance of taking any of the
chassepots, as they were called, from the
enemy. France’s trouble was that her
armies were badly trained and indifferently
handled and had only makeshift artillery.

GOD SAVE THE KING.
Say it once and mean it.
By a judicial decision given in Sydney last
week hotelkeepers are to be allowed deduc-
tion from taxable income of the amount ex-
pended by them in providing counter-lunches.
The citizen who surreptitiously wolfs 15
sausage-rolls and a loaf of bread while wait-
ing at the bar for a pint can now proceed to
further execution with a heart lightened by
knowledge that he is doing Bung a good turn.
But there is something lopsided about this
business. A man who provides three meals a
day for his family is exempt to the extent of
4s. 2d. per annum of taxation'on each child,
which is perhaps sufficient to provide one
day’s parsimonious feeding for a small house-
hold. On all the rest of bis expenditure on
grub for the home he is allowed no deduction
—so it looks as if buying a cartload of pigs’-
trotters to decorate a bar and fill Percy
Deadbeat is in the eyes of the law a more
socially useful form of expenditure than
acouiring bread and butter for Young Aus-
tralia.

The Magnetism of Beautiful
Hair.
“Applied Arts.”
Beautiful hair adds immensely to the per-
sonal magnetism of both men and women.
Actresses and smart women are ever on the
lookout for any harmless thing that will
increase the natural beauty of their hair.
The latest method is to use pure stallax as a
shampoo, on account of the peculiarly glossy,
fluffy and wavy effect which it leaves. As
stallax has never been used much for th 5 s
purpose it comes to the chemist only in
sealed original packages, enough for twenty-
five or thirty shampoos. A teaspoonful of the
fragrant stallax granules, dissolved in a cup
of hot water, is more than sufficient for each
shampoo. It is very beneficial and stimulat-
ing to the hair, apart from its beautifying
effect.

Superfluous Hair.
“Toilet Gossip.”

to know. It is a pity that it is not more
generally known that pure powdered pheminol.
obtainable from the chemists, may be used
for this purpose. It is applied directly to
the objectionable hair. The recommended
treatment not only instantly removes the
hair, leaving no trace, but is designed also to
kill the roots completely.

The first business of the Ma State Par-
liament was the Dentists Amendment Bill,
which proposes to wipe out the frequently
unskilful fraud who dodges the present regu-
lations enforcing proper qualifications. The
law, which could fall on the unregistered
man who called himself a dentist, or the
registered dentist who lent his name to the
unlicensed smith, was powerless to deal with
the increasing fraud who hustles the public
into showy parlors under a cloud of adver-
tisement. The new legislation is expected
to be a fatal obstacle in his career as a
jobbing tooth carpenter.
Bellerive: “A Moonlight Night at Gallipoli” fills the
bill this time :—•
The red cross lights
From the ships devine
O’er the rippling waters
Doth glitter arid shine,
While the crystal moon
From the starlit sky,
Streams down upon the
Weird mountains high,
As the sentinel on guard
The scene survey,
His thoughts are charmed
From the morrow’s fray.

Put all your eggs in one basket
and then watch that basket.
(Carnegie.)
Many people can do several things fairly well, but nobody can do many
things superlatively well.
This is what Michelin recognises, and it is why WC concentrate
the whole of our attention upon the manufacture of pneumatic
tyres only.
Michelin Tyres
are superlatively good tyres,
because we have put all our eggs in one basket, and do not spread our
efforts over the manufacture of various articles like If balls, rubber
shoes, rubber hose pipes, hot-water bags, etc. etc.
All the brains of our chemists and engineers are concentrated upon
one task—the task of making the Michelin Tyre the best tyre in the world.
If you are not a user of Michelin Tyres, try them!
Wc don’t fear comparisons ; we solicit them.
Michelin Tyres and Accessories can be
obtained from all the Leading Garages.

EORGE HOTEL,
St. Hilda,, Melbourno.
Largest Hotel Accommodation in Victoria-
The Australia Hotel
BUSINESS AS USUAL.
The Management of the “Australia ’
Hotel are gratified to state that, not-
withstanding the upset caused by the
recent fire they are able to announce
“business as usual.” The service in
the Grand Dining Room, on the
Balcony, and in the Winter Garden
is being conducted in the same style
that has made the “Australia ’ Hotel
so popular with its patrons, while the
arrangements for Private Dinners and
Suppers are working with their accus-
tomed satisfaction.
Throughout the holiday season
arrangements for Afternoon Teas will,
as usual, be carried out in the. Winter
Garden, which has been running con-
tinuously throughout.
J. URE SMITH, Manager.

IN BANANA LAND
The question of State-school teachers’
salaries came along with the usual annual
remarks at the recent Teachers’ Conference.
The pedagogues were so insistent in demand-
ing a rise that Director of Education Roe
found it necessary to hasten round and ex-
plain that these were parlous times finan-
cially and that everybody couldn’t get pro-
moted. Meanwhile the young male teacher
is either drifting away to war or into com-
merce, and the schoolmarms are taking their
places all over the State.
“Musketoon”: A truly naturalised Aus-
trian at Clermont has done his little bit for
his adopted country. The old chap, who
has been naturalised for about 40 years,
recently wrote to the Federal Treasury, ex-
plaining that, as he had disposed of a small
property, the proceeds of which would just
about keep him, his old-age pension money
migllt be of some assistance to the Com-
monwealth Government in prosecuting the
war. He did not even ask for the usual
44 per cent, per annum for his voluntary con-
tribution.
“Yannatherra”: These anaemic Banana-
landers? Was recently browsing in the
mulga belts on the Nebine, 70 miles from a
house-dog and 110 miles from a rail-track.
The mother-in-law of all storms was threat-
ening, and when the boss’s wife uttered her
first groan the full terror of the elements
screamed the dingo dirges down. Bill
hopped across his prad, and made 28 miles
of mulga tangle look silly. Meantime a
woman’s agony developed inside the tent.
The boss pottered about, helplessly cursing
the black horizon. The hurricane matured
just as Bill and the helpful female hove
alongside. Bill and I acted as anchors on
the weather side of the flimsy, bulging man-
sion. The rain soaked us in a refreshing
coolness; the wind subsided; and the calm-
ness of the night echoed the yelp of a juven-
ile Bananalander. Both were doing tiptop
as I lit out for a nerve tonic.
Judging by a deputation of the recently-
formed Police Union, which waited on the
Home Secretary the other day, it was about
time J. Hop organised. It brought along
a dray-load of grievances for the Minister’s
inspection, and prominent amongst them was
the complaint that most of the men are on
duty for 12 to 15 hours daily, and that tele-
phone attendants are practically on the
wire-end continuously for 48 hours, except
when they might be relieved from midnight
till five next morning. “Why don’t you com-
plain?” asked the astonished Minister.
“Because if we did we would be brought up
before the administrative head,” was the
reply. How encouraging is the sight of a
body of civil servants begging an eight-hours’
day from a Labor Government!
“E.J.8.” reflects on the invader of the
past:—
During six months I spent in that collection of
galvanised-iron humpies, Camooweal (where the ther-
mometer goes to 120 deg. in the shade and drinks
are a shilling), about 40 Chinese came in from the
wilds beyond. They came in twos and threes and
sometimes alone overland from Port Darwin, and as
a feat of human endurance the performance was re-
markable. They all had padded the hoof—none of
them had horses —through 500 miles of uninhabited
and, in parts, waterless country. Some were shod
with light sandals, but many were barefooted. How
they negotiated the long, waterless stages is a
marvel.
When they reached Camooweal these stragglers would
be arrested by the local John. He did not need to
overheat himself chasing after them, for they took
care he didn’t overlook them —after they had
delivered their loose cash into the safe-keeping
of their local brethren, who cultivated the elusive
cabbage on the muddy waterhole dignified by the
name of Lake Francis. They were kept in the
lock-up yard during the day and shoved into
a shed at night. The sergeant didn’t lie awake
through anxiety that they might escape. They
were not anxious to escape. They were in clover,
plenty of tucker, nothing to do but enjoy themselves,
having a well-earned rest after their long and
arduous journey.
Then the sergeant would write to Brisbane for
instructions, and after a few weeks —or months—he
would be directed to escort them to the border, four
miles away. When he came to the border-line he
would order them in a stern voice to go back to
Port Darwin —which they didn’t. They had had a
feed and a rest, and under the friendly shade of
night they sped eastward again.
“Itan” ; The ill-advised Brisbane tramway
strike is recalled by the death of Dave
Bowman. It was he who saved a serious
riot on the day Police-Commissioner Cahill
paraded his forces. The unionists were
already marching to the fray when Bowman
hastened along and implored them to refrain
from their head-smashing expedition. Bow-
man’s personality and the strikers’ respect
for the man alone saved bloodshed on that
occasion. Curiously Cahill was M.C. at the
State funeral accorded the dead democrat.
At Ipswich District Court the other day
the plaintiff in a civil claim alleged that his
ear had been severely chewed by the defend-
ant. His counsel after a lengthy oratorical
outbreak concerning the damaged face wing
concluded: “Gentlemen, you will observe
the nature and extent of these injuries on
the plaintiff, whose ear, gentlemen, speaks
for itself!” It isn’t so long since a Brisbane
barrister told the Full Court that.Dugong
Flat was “10 miles from Caloundra as the
fly crows.” Still another of the learned pro-
fesh told the High Court, which was sol-
emnly considering a section in a Queensland
statute drafted from an Imperial enactment,
that it should disregard the latter, “as the
tendency of Queensland legislation is to
travel far ahead of Imperial Acts, but
always in a different direction.” Where-
upon Griffith blinked, Isaacs looked pained
and Higgins became profoundly grave. It
was a solemn occasion.
IMPORTANT.
OWNER : “The mare got a nasty bump. She seems very distressed .”
JOCKEY: “How about ME! I backed her.”
John Plumper Hoolan, who contributed
much to the gaiety of the Assembly at one
time, has been travelling the inky way for
many years. The ex-politician is the owner
and editor of the Mundic Miner, a Far-
Northern print which is seen occasionally
around Georgetown, Little River and Fossil-
brook. It has periods of enforced slumber,
and has just been awakened by Hoolan after
two months’ repose. The editor thus alludes
to the awakening :
Sleep is “Nature’s sweet restorer,” and having
slept for two months I have again started to twinkle
amidst our lights in the newspaper firmament. The
start is made with a light heart, a clean sheet and
an empty cash-box. Wealth is a curse to mankind ;
even so, a small bit of the curse alighting at the
door would not be hooshed away with the office
broom.
Hoolan was a politician of real grit, and no
party pence could hold him. He would be
an acquisition in politics now.
Police-Commissioner Cahill is apparently to
enjoy a longer tenure ,of his job than was
expected. At a caucus meeting the other
day Cahill’s retention was hotly discussed,
but Premier Ryan informs an indifferent
public that as the Denham Government gave
Cahill a statutory contract of employment
to the end of 1917, “provided he is of good
behavior,” any alteration in the contract
must be approved by Parliament.
“The Nomad’s” send-off to David Bow-
man :
It was hard work that killed him ; not the hard
work of weeks or months, but the consuming of his
energies for years. He was one of the hardest
workers the Labor movement ever had, and the
beacon (hat "drew - him on was always honesty of
purpose. When a young man he pushed a bike all
over the Western Districts organising for the A.W.U.,
and was the most successful organiser the big union
ever had in the North.
His entry into politics was discouraging. In 1893
he suffered defeat as a candidate for South Brisbane.
Again in 1898 he went under in the contest for
Warrego by only 14 votes; but next poll he won the
seat. He represented the great Western constituency
until 1902, when he was again defeated. In 1904 he
was returned for Fortitude Valley, the seat he held
to the end. Bowman led the Labor party from 1907
to 1912, when ill-health forced him to reliquish the
leadership. Like most men of his characteristics he
died a comparatively poor man.
A Courier reprint brings to memory a
famous fraud of 50 years ago—Count von
Atoms. The Count, who arrived in Sydney
with splendid credentials, was lionised and
feted. Later, in Brisbane, he was enter-
tained by Geo. Harris at Newstead, at that
time the centre of fashion and squatterdom.
Then one day the Count casually borrowed
Harris’s yacht and sailed clean off the map.
r Ihe missuses of Queensland are annoyed.
They 7 have been informed by the Insurance
Commissioner that from date all employees,
casual or regular, must be insured under the
Workers’ Compensation Act, even the occa-
sional washerwoman. Not long ago a depu-
tation of housewives, who over-ran the
Treasury buildings, said that if such a law
were passed the marriageable girls would
decline to become wives. So another Labor
Government has set out to burst the
marriage tie.
Witch of the Pan!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Talk of the hags of the night
Dancing, a mystical crew,
Moulding Macbeth in their might,
Stirring a magical stew,
Over with them and their brew,
Harry them on with your ban.
Here is the sorceress true,
Here is the Witch of the Pan.
What is the name of the wight
Who would deny her her due?
Lo —she is Angel of Light
Blending all savors anew ;
Mark how the pancake she threw,
Damosels flirting the fan,
Gallants who wisely would woo —-
Here is the Witch of the Pan.
Swains who would marry aright
Rave not of optics of blue,
Yain are the pleasures of sight
’Ware how you wander and sue;
List while I give you a cue
If ye put bliss in the van.
Seek in the kitchen a clue —
There is the Witch of the Pan !
Envoi.
If ye look life through and through
Counting the joys of its plan.
Lo —she hath garnered a few,
She who is Witch of the Pan!

Justice Real recently announced his
opinion from the Bench that drink was best
left alone and that he never touched it.
Rechabites rejoiced immensely. Thereupon
the Judge deemed it necessary to administer
a corrective. “Although a strict teetotaller
myself,” he said a few days later, “the aver-
age teetotaller I don’t like, because he doesn't
leave people in peace. Some of them think
because they are teetotal they can do what-
ever else they please.” It is now Bung who
is quoting the Judge enthusiastically, but
nobody cares tuppence, the wrangle being
interminable and without profit.
“Ossian” adds his tribute: —
No man in Australian politics rivalled Bowman in
sincerity, candor and honesty; and his private life
was without blemish and without reproach. He was
one of the few Labor leaders that the party did not
throw over—his was not the fate of Glassey, Airey
or Kidston. Ilis success was not due to special gifts
of leadership nor to marked ability. His great
qualities were his kindness of heart, his unswerving
loyalty to his party and his unsparing efforts in
public and in private to advance the cause of Labor.
With all his colleagues Bowman enjoyed unbounded
popularity, and, with many, deep affection; whilst
with the rank and file of the masses no man, not
even Janies Blair, has been so popular an idol in
Queensland for more than a decade.

Business, Robbery, etc.
There is a whole economic sermon in the
euphemistic tag now attached to a high
grade of imported cotton stuffs. Once they
were known as “guaranteed cloths, ” mean-
ing they were guaranteed to be fast-dyed.
Now they bear the legend in danger-red
“War Dye,” which means Dye”—per-
haps. The haughty draper who handles
them often has blue on his hands as well
as in his noble veins by the time he has
measured off a dozen at eleven and three.
Once these guaranteed-cloths were fast-fixed
with good German aniline (discovered by
an Englishman ) ; now they are war-dyed
with British Satan-knows-what. It looks as
if storekeepers will soon be offering a blood-
poisoning insurance policy with every yard
of the stuff in order to induce sales.
“Sinapsis”: There seems to be a confu-
sion of evidence in the mustard issue.
“Copabella” alleges (B. 25/11/’! 5) that
Australia cannot make mustard. “F.N.T.”
(B. 6/l/’l6) is unable to find any differ-
ence in flavor or bite between the local and
imported article, but the local grocer told
his wife the foreign stuff was best. Then
“D.S.F.” (B. 13/1/T6) tells someone to
“get a magnifying glass to work on Aus-
tralian-made mustard tin....and make sure
it doesn’t say ‘Made in England for So-and-
So,’ ” and alleged that “one good Australian
firm in Melbourne,” which mills everything
they possibly can on the spot, used to mill
mustard, but gave it up because the seed
went bad, and now imports in bulk. Pre-
sumably “the good Australian firm” is “So-
and-So,” who get their mustard manufac-
tured in England, and only tin, label and sell
it here. But “D.S.F.” takes no notice of
the two good Australian firms who have
made a of mustard milling in Mel-
bourne. The yarn about the seed going bad
on the voyage is nonsense. It is far less
likely to lose condition than manufactured
mustard in bulk. Giving full credit to
the two leading brands of English mustard,
now amalgamated in one proprietary, and
allowing that they set the standard of mus-
tard for the world, the facts are that no
other mustard, English or American, has
come so close to the standard as the success-
ful Australian brands, so close indeed that
few people can see any difference between
the Australian and British (so-called stan-
dard) article.
* * * *
“Hamer”: The vegetable John is an over-
estimated toiler; he certainly works long
hours, but he is the father of all Conserva-
tives and does not improve his methods ; con-
sequently the white market-gardener, with
up to-date appliances, can beat him easily
if he likes. The trouble is that there are
too few white gardeners. A county Cook
(N.S.W.) farmer, working 40 acres on
the eight-hour system, with the aid of
his two sons and an oil engine, is
alleged to have made a net profit
of £968 last year from vegetables; and on
the Hawkesbury, where there are about 50
irrigation plants, the Chows who used to
work the farms on shares have been beaten
out by white men. Still, the cabbage some-
how contrives to be infrequent and dear, and
the Chinaman often buys in the markets
the white-grown vegetable and sells it at
a profit. The N.S.W. G overnment is
sending to S. Australia to inquire into the
vegetable industry; but what seems to be
most required is the advertisement of the
information that market gardening does not
of necessity, mean long hours and a big
struggle against the much-boomed industry
of Cathay. If I had the time to spare I
would become a rich cabbage missionary or
agricultural bishop spreading the glad tid-
ings.

“X.Y.” : About “The Grease Trap at the
Wrong End” (B. 24/2/’10). For many
years the writer (a chemist) has been howl-
ing at the people who allow this waste, and
the most sympathetic reply he ever received
was from a man who called him a dreamer.
Lanolin, or wool fat, forms about 84 per
cent, of the weight of greasy wool, but, as
this product is usually combined with any-
thing up to 100 per cent, of water, the
yield is considerable. The process used in
separating the lanolin from the scouring
water is partly mechanical and partly chemi-
cal, but presents no scientific difficulties.
One factor in the Australian system of
scouring would tend to make the extraction
slow and costly, and that is the vast amount
of water used. As all the scouring water
must pass through a centrifugal appliance
something like a magnified cream separator
bowl, in order to separate the grease from
the dirt, the magnitude of the job of saving
the lanolin from the scouring water,
in which it forms the very small
fraction of 1 per cent., may be appre-
ciated. In one woolscour in Western
Queensland 2,000,000 gallons of hot arte-
sian water are used daily. Of course it would
not pay to recover fat from such an ocean
of water as this; but there is a much
cheaper and more direct way of extracting
the lanolin, and that is to send the jum-
buck’s overcoat to be dry cleaned. The
greasy fleece is tumbled into a drum with
benzine for a few minutes, and the benzine
does the trick. There is in this process the
disadvantage that it is almost impossible to
get rid of the last trace of the smell of ben-
zine. The lanolin does not contain all the
grease which the wool carries, as a certain
proportion of it is combined with earthy
salts, mostly lime, and forms an insoluble
soap which cannot profitably be recovered.
Apart altogether from loss of grease in the
wool, there is a further loss of sudorate of
potash, which constitutes 15 per cent, of
the weight of greasy wool. The potash is
recovered by evaporating the scouring water.
The residue is calcined, the product ground
fine, and the potash washed out with hot
water, and upon this water being evapor-
ated the potash, worth at the present time
in London 2s. 4d. per lb., crystallises out.
One little group of woollen manufacturers in
the north of France used to recover 250,0001 b.
of this potash annually. The Australian
practice of using too much water tells against
the saving of the potash even more than it
does against the saving of the lanolin.

“The Oldest Inhabitant’s” item :
Bernard Otto Holtermann, a pioneer of North
Sydney, was verbally dug up and examined at a
meeting .of the local council last week, and it was
decided to put his bones back and leave them : n
peace. Holtermann’s memory is partly preserved by
a street named after him, and a proposal to re-
christen it was rejected. A German, he
first came into history when mining at Hill End
and Hawkins Hill in the ’so’s and running a
barber’s shop as a side-line with Louis Beyers. The
partners married sisters, daughters of a Bathurst
bootmaker named Emmett. After various vicissi-
tudes Iloltermann and Beyers in ’7l opened out a
vein yielding 50oz. to the ton. Beyers sold his
share in the ground for £70,000. Holtermann hung
on, and in June of that year tore up
gold worth £12,000. Later on, he invested
his cash ’in the Lithgow estate (North Syd-
ney), which he cut up and sold and built a man-
sio , known as Holtermann’s Tower, now the Church
of England Grammar School. He was the first man
to spend coin in Australia on the development of
photography and pioneered outdoor work with the
camera. Louis',Beyers lived quietly on at Hill End,
and represented the Western goldfields in the
Assembly of 1877-80 and Mudgee in 1880-82. He
called himself the Representative of the Interior.
Holtermann was elected for St. Leonards in Decem-
ber, 1882. He died in April, 1885.

The Inky Way.
S. M. Herald the other day reported a
ease in which a writ of prohibition was asked
for, thus:—■ *
The respondents declared that they intended to
take no further action in the matter, and the case
was adjourned generally to allow this to be done.
Evidently the old lady finds 8 o’clock closing
hard to get accustomed to.
* * * *
“Copy Boy”: Jack Blackham, who was
the Beau Brummel of Melbourne journalists
until a couple of years ago, when he gave up
his job as chief-of-staff of the Herald to
become advertising manager of the paper,
has had to retire owing to bad health. He
had not been well for some time, and a month
or two ago he came into collision with a
motor-car with unsatisfactory results.
* * * *
“Piou-Piou”: French military slang has
as many holes for the unwary as has the
dinkum language of the Australian legion-
aries for the Maltese papers. Recently an
article, bearing the pen-name “Pousse-
Cailloux,” appeared in Blackwood’s on the
subject of the French “seventy-five.” The
Age of 19/2/T6, in reproducing the article,
respectfully attributes it to “M. Pousse
Cailloux,” being apparently under the im-
pression that monsieur is a French military
expert. As a matter of fact, “Pousse-Cail-
leux” (pebble-pusher) is the French cav-
alry’s slang term for the infantry soldier —
the foot-slogger, as we would call him. But
he doesn’t mind. He even survives another
scornful epithet of the prideful cavalry—
“rampart-crab,” or, as Tommy Anzac might
render it, “funk-hole yabbie.”
* • » •
“Moana”: The most remarkable number of
London Punch that was ever hoisted out
appeared on the bookstalls of the U.S. re-
cently. In size, shape and outward appear-
ance the production was identical with any
ordinary number; but it contained 15 full-
page cartoons lifted from the far-back
Punch of 1842-70. On the cover there was
the legend: “As England sees U.S. Some
Famous and Forgotten Cartoons that the
Present Generation of Americans Should
See. Reproduced by the American Truth
Society.” The cartoons selected were
mainly those of the Yankee Civil War period,
when England was not at all in sympathy
with Uncle Sam. To each cartoon some
comment was appended in bold type and red
ink, such as “John Bull felt like thrashing
us during the Civil War.” The cartoons had
all been carefully selected to misrepresent
the Punch of to-day. The cheeky piracy
had a short life, for the London proprietors
started after the so-called “American Truth
Society,” and found it was run by disinter-
ested truth-lovers bearing names like Schlatz
and Schmoltz.
* * • •
Despite the success of “The Sentimental
Bloke,” C. J. Dennis is not as happy as he
might be at present, having been dealt one
by “crool forchune’s dirty left.” In step-
ping from a tram he fell and gave his inside
a wrench, so that he had to be operated on.
The operation turned out all right, but “Den”
has to lay up for some weeks. Incidentally,
he recently threw up his job in the Navy
Office to become private secretary to Labor
Senator Ted Russell.
* * * •
“C. 8.”: A Melbourne weekly sent its
lady writer to have a talk with Ashmead
Bartlett. When the war-correspondent saw
the result the chances are that for the
moment he almost forgot to keep his cigar-
ette alight. F’rinstance :—■
He looks very young upon this early-in-the-morn-
ing visit, when he is caught in unconventional
attire; attire that shows some inherent love of the
warmth of Oriental coloring, too, for it .is a silk
dressing-gown of rich, deep orange coloring which
reveals glimpses of pyjamas of the same shade
beneath.
Also, “He lounges in a comfortable room
before a breakfast-tray on which oranges
almost repeat the color note of his attire
and intensify the Oriental suggestion.” Ap-
parently Bartlett is always careful that his
pyjamas shall match his breakfast. And he
smokes cigarettes, paces the room nervously
and lounges (some more) for all the world as
though he had just stepped from “The Pic-
ture of Dorian Gray.” Woman scribes seem
to be on the look-out for a beauty war-
correspondent to take the place of the beauty-
actor whose immemorial futility, is being
made manifest in these exciting times.
* * * *
London Standard, which was the other
day offered for sale, but was passed in at
£lO,OOO (subsequently an attempt was made
to dispose of the goodwill, but nobody would
go more than £100), has not flourished of
late. The Standard was started as an
evening paper in 1827. Thirty years later it
changed its publishing time to the morning,
and for many years was one of London’s
leading Tory dailies. The late Marquis of
Salisbury, when Lord Robert Cecil,. pushed
a pen on its behalf, and Alfred Austin, who
was exactly fitted as Poet Laureate, was one
of its leader-writers —and a first-class one at
that. John Gorst was for a time editor, and
the paper had several capital war-correspon-
dents—G. A. Henty, the writer of boys’
yarns; John Cameron (killed at Abi Klea)
and William Maxwell, who was sent here to
do the opening of the first Commonwealth
Parliament. The Standard cherished Free-
trade as a fetich until 1904, when it was
bought by C. A. Pearson and became a bar-
racker for Tariff Reform. A few years ago
the paper was taken over Dy a proprietary
company, but of late things have not gone
well. Its three big Conservative rivals have
been the pushful Daily Mail, the old-estab-
lished Morning Post and The Times, especi-
ally since the price of the last-named was
reduced.

“The Printer’s Devil”: Once in a Rock-
hampton office the social column collided
with the shipping. Next morning the town
sat back and sized up the damage; “A con-
versazione was held in the School of Arts
last night in honor of Archbishop Donald-
son. Amongst those present were the Hon.
and Mrs. So-and-So, etc. In addition there
were 35 in the steerage.”

in the future. I have a beautiful boy fed on your foods since infancy,
we tried other foods (not knowing of yours) and they almost killed
him, he became so thin and old looking that I felt compelled to have
the doctor's advice. Directly he saw the child he said he was being
starved, he was nothing but bone ; he told me to try Allen & Hanbury’s
foods and see how they would act. I did so with a marvellous result,
in a week there was a big change, he is now eleven months old and
such a big boy— not flabby fat but proportionate. He is just starting
to walk and has eight teeth so you see his illness has not made him
backward. I feel I cannot say enough for your foods and advertise
them all I can. Trusting you will send the pamphlet I asked for,”

Mrs. H. Pearson.
