Letter to an Injured Lady.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DEAR Little Lady, I admit
Your ease is hard. To think of it!
You married, being still a kid,
With life's deep meanings subtly hid
(These educators have no sense)
From your unfledged intelligence;
Then in a whirl of strange alarms
Lay sobbing in your husband's arms-
Sobbing to think that Love had been
Degraded by the Lampsacene.
But somehow, in a week or two,
Sweet resignation came to you,
With glows of joy, transcendent gleams
That promised fruitage of your dreams,
And Something (who shall guess at
Whence?)
Restored to you your innocence.
Which rose resplendent in a flame
Of joy the day that Baby came.
Then years of light and dark together;
The summer glows, the wintry weather ;
The tedious days of cark and fret
That made you almost hate him....yet
The blinding moments of such pain
As made your love seem young again.
So far—l bluntly state the case—
Your story’s blessed commonplace.
Marriage is torment more or less
To everyone, as wise folks guess.
Marriage is still (the wise ones know)
A little bit of Heaven below.
Thus man and wife are perfect friends —
If they are wisest: much depends
On that. The game is give and take
In sympathy, for Love's sweet sake.
Ah well, you know all that, and I'm
No moralist at any time.
I’m busy, anyhow ; so we
Go to your special tragedy.
After these years of varied hue,
These years in which you have been true
(I take your word for that, you see,
Without suspicion, cordially)
After these years of joy and pain,
Of little ease and constant strain,
You find (and earth’s foundations shake!)
That you are married to a rake!
They rock, the mountains huge and steep !
The fountains of the vasty deep
Are broken up! The gods are dead !
And all because of Mister Ted.
My dear, as sure as Satan lives,
You’re given to superlatives.
It’s all because (’twixt you and me)
Just now r you're mad as you can be.
Your wriggling on one venomed point
Puts all creation out of joint.
And makes you see your husband —he,
Poor chap—as your worst enemy.
Yet, at the worst, what has he done
To “wreck your life,” this faithless one?
Item: Two years ago, or three,
He flirted much with Mrs. 8.,
And now r you find that late last June
They filched two weeks of honeymoon;
That more than once in years gone by
He’s paid her bills for her —oh, fie !
Item: You learn that, once or twice,
With chorus-girls he’s Plunged in Vice,
And furnished petticoats and gloves
To quite a flock of crumpled doves.
And there the dire indictment ends. . ..
Heaven save you, lady, from your friends!
Let us admit (since you’re so moved)
Your worst suspicions fully proved.
Let us convict your roving dunce
Of tickling Rahab more than once.
Do you, for your part, elevate
These w r enches to your proper state?
Do you pretend that Mrs. B.
Could ever be your rival ? She !
You don’t. At any rate, I should
Just hate myself to think you could !
Admitted that your naughty man
Has sinned against the Moral Plan —•
Has bought a trull, with toy or gaud,
By sale direct, or through a baw’d.
There are, I straightway tell you flat,
Worse infidelities than that.
Ted’s rather mean infrequent sin
Is an affection of the skin:
He’s idle, and you are not by;
Some dainty strumpet winks an eye:
So (Lord ha’ mercy!) Mister Ted
Gets something buzzing in his head,
And (at preposterous expense)
Makes Phryne his convenience.
Immoral? Let’s admit it is.
If that will sooth your vanities.
Immoral? Let’s not be in haste—
I call it horribly bad taste.
I hear you cry: Suppose that you
Had buzzings, what would Edward do?
But you’re a sweet, wee w r oman, white
Of soul and body, exquisite.
Fastidiously clean and chaste
Because God made you so. . . .Why waste
Tour breath in vile pretence that you
Could stoop to things that men may do?
M e’ll leave—l’m still your friend, I trust —
That damned suggestion undiscussed.
Pve sinned and roystered, gulping down
The dregs and ordure of the town ;
I am no better than the rest
Of men to-day. I stand confessed.
Ive tongue (’twas better so),
But here’s a thing you have to know.
Though much bemired, the path I’ve trod,
There was one woman sent from God
To tend for me the Inward Light
That somehow keeps a fellow right
Among it all. She shines for me,
My constant star, whatever be ;
And I’ll not have you pull her down
lo mate with harlots of the town,
rou told your story yesterday,
was much I yearned to say,
And didn’t. Girl, do you know why?....
because I love you, honestly.
S° on, you, and live your life,
Tnspotted, mother, comrade, wife,
111 talk like to Mister Ted,
.' j om s tiH you love, when all is said ;
And never, sweetheart, shall there be
me word of love ’twixt you and me.
Vrl t '/• y . ou dare to drag her down,
t If her gown
is dabbled in the noisome stews
With the gross filth that lechers use—
Then, by the God that made me, Kit,
I’U hill you, though I swing for it!
On Thursday night (I’ll fix with Ted)
Dinner and show, you Poppy-Head.
N.S.W.
SELWYN RIDER.
The Good Samaritan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ 1 RATHER JOLLY called ’im ‘ther good
■ 1 Sir Mariton’; an’ I reckon that if
Sir Mariton was anything like ’im
’e wasn’t er bad sort of a bloke, although a
bit of a trick.”
All the same, in spite of the eloquence of
Keene, the solicitor, on his behalf, the Law,
taking no notice of his virtues, had sent him
up for six months, with some hard remarks
about his many appearances. Incidentally,
on the observations made by the Court, the
shadow of prosecution for perjury lay over
“Scrooger” Simpson, who gave him a testi-
monial.
“You done yer best, o’ course,” said
Scrooger to Keene. “I suppose when a
chap does things ’e’s bound to go up some-
times. Still I was ready to tell any blanky
lie ter get ’im off, knowin’ what a fine bloke
’e ’is. Will yer friend ’ave a whisky, Mr.
Keene?”
We would, an’ we did.
“Smiler was always a rum sort a cove —•
we always called Jack Davis Smiler. He
was too good-natured fer anything. That’s
what got ’im inter trouble mostly. ’E
useter lose ’is work through it. Coming
inter toil one day ’e sees Bill Jenkins putting
up a shed in ’is place an’ stops ter word ’im.
‘What’s the lurk. Bill?’ says ’e—‘fowls or
lodgers?’ ‘The old ’un’s knocked off work
an’s gotter pension, an’s coming ter stop,’
says Bill, ‘an’ as ’e don’t like sleepin’ in
the kitching I’ve gotter put up a place of
some sort.’ ‘Going ter do it by y’rself?’
asks Smiler. ‘Gotter,’ grunts Bill. ‘Ain’t
got no dough for anybody else.’ “Want an
’and?’ says Smiler; and ’e offs with ’is coat,
an’ does most of the work, and, of course,
does in ’is job. And then, after all, the
’ealth inspector comes erlong in a week or
so, an’ won’t let the old ’un sleep there.
“You know Soldier Haynes?” asked
Scrooger, after a pause, during which the
glasses were refilled.
“Soldier Haynes?” said Keene, reflec-
tively. “Oh, yes! Stealing from the per-
son ; eight convictions; six months hard! —
I remember him.”
“ That's him,” returned Scrooger, checking
off the description with nods at each item.
“Well, when Soldier comes out ’e’s pretty
well stumped after spendin’ ’is _ gaol money
makin’ ’is thirst good ; an’ meetin’ Smiler ’e
says: ‘No more quod fer me, if I can ’elp
it,’ says ’e. ‘lf I only ’ad an ’orse an’ cart,’
’e says, ‘l’d be able ter get right down ter
work.’ Now, Smiler ain’t the sort of bloke
ter let a pal o’ ’is go short fer an ’orse-gn’-
cart, so ’e tells Soldier that ’e 11 get ’im one.
An’ ’e does: only it turns out that Smiler
—’oo owing to ’is ’abit of giving things away
never ’as anything of ’is own ter give—’as
borrered the turnout from a yard where there
didn’t seem to be anyone in charge.
“Still Soldier didn’t know that until after-
wards. But though it gave ’im a start, ’e
was quite sore with Smiler when ’e ’ad ter
part about three times what it’d ’ave cost
’im ter ’ire it so as ter square ther thing.
A reg’lar screw was old Gilligan, ’oo owned
it. After ’e’d got at Soldier like that ’e
gets at Smiler with a tale that ’e’s a pore
man an’ ’is ’arness is rooined. It wasn’t
much ter rooin, being a good bit string-’eld
tergether with pieces of leather, but Smiler
’e says, ‘I ain’t ther bloke ter let anybody
suffer fer what I done/ _’e says; ‘l’ll get you
some new ’arness.’ Which he does.
“That’s ther time Smilef went up fer
stealin’ ’arness,” he added, to Keene, who
nodded. „ , ~
“Was that why Father Jolly called him
a good Samaritan?” I asked, motioning the
barmaid to put the bottle down again, be-
cause the story of Smiler interested me.
“No; Sir Mariton don’t come in there.”
said Scrooger. “That was another time —
over a tart named Eva Kelly ’e was shook
on. .
“There was a sevenp’ny meal joint where
a lot of the blokes useter go; an’ nearly orl
of ’em was shook on Eva. She comes from
the country, or else she’d ’ave ’ad the savvy
'ter get inter a better joint, ’cos she was an
A 1 looking tart.
“She could ’ave married orl right; but
she turned every bloke down. She’d been
useter living in a dull sort er loose-box way
in the country, an’ she didn’t like ’er place
in the city. She wanted somethin’ better,
bad. But she wasn’t fer settling down an’
marrying, an’ ’aving the same thing as she’d
’ad all ’er life.
“She puts up at the Young Women s
Christian, an’ no one ’as a word to say
about the way she behaves. I reckon it was
the bloomin’ skite she ’ears erbout the gilded
ways of sin what done it. It’s ter blame
fer a lot. The gilded ways of sin!” snorted
Scrooger disgustedly. “There ain’t no gilded
ways. .
“Well, we could all see Eva gettin’ more
discontenteder and more discontenteder
every day, an’ ’ating ’er work, an’ not able
ter get away from it fer the reason that it
ain’t no recommendation in tonier places to
’ave ’ad experience in shoving round food
in a sevenpenny joint, where the people
mostly do their own stretchin’ fer things;
an’ yer c’d see the impression of the girls
as ain’t careful of their morals seemin’ ter
get on very well, an’ ’ave a good time, and
the ideas of the gilded w r ays of sin soakin’
in. An’ one day she comes out with paint
on ’er lips, where it wasn’t wanted.
“Well, Smiler, ’oo’s shook on ’er, sees it
all, and gets wild erbout it; until one day
’e waits for ’er an’ threatens to ’ammer
sense inter ’er —though ’e- ain’t that sort of
bloke really, being the sorter feller who
wouldn’t ’it anyone fer fear ’e’d ’urt ’em;
and she tells ’im : ‘What’s the use of being
virtuous, anyway,’ she says—‘it only gets a
’usband like you !’ she says.
“It was a low-down cur of a motor-car
driver done it, doing the grand on the
strength of ’is boss’s car. ’E took ’er up the

J. A. BURKE, 5 Primrose Buildings, 255aGeorge St., Sydney
mountains a week-end, an’ she loses ’er job
at the sevenpenny through not being there
when she’s wanted.
“That decided ’er, I s’pose. Anyway, she
takes to the gilded path, at the worst time
of the year, an’ finds it ain’t gilded; and
seein’ the ’ole thing’s a fraud makes _ ’er
careless erbout drinkin’ when there’s drinks
erbout. So she goes to the bad so much that
Smiler meets ’er one wet night, ’ardly able
ter stand, an’ with a cough as would ’ack a
tree.
“The gilded ways of sin!” Scrooger
snorted again. “They’re not so bad fer them
as takes to them natural and knows the way
erbout; but they’re ’ell to them as goes be
the wrong directions.
“Anyway, Smiler, seeing she’s ill, gets ’er
decent lodgings and puts ’er in ’em; an’ in
the morning the doctor says she’s too ill
to be moved. It’s pleurisy, starvation, care-
less-living an’ all sorts of things.
“So Smiler parts up, an’ ’e done it cheer-
ful. Don’t arsk me ’ow ’e done it —I don’t
know! But ’e done it. An’ ’e gets ’er
jellies an’ things, an’ flowers; an’, when
she might be shifted, ’e keeps ’er where she
is, findin’ all the needful an’ cheerin’ ’er up
by kidding that when she’s better they’ll be
married, spite of everything. An’ all the
time ’e knows on the doctor’s word that
there’s only a chance of ’er gettin’ better
if she keeps cheerful; so ’e’s with ’er most
of the day an’ of course loses er lot of work.
God knows ’ow ’e managed to keep things
goin’—without bein’ caught.
“ ‘You’re a good man, Smiler,’ she says to
him; “you’re the best man I’ve ever known
—overlooking everything, like you have.
You’re a saint!’
“Smiler tells me this one day as I meets
him just as ’e’s leaving, and he adds, ‘Me!
Blime!’ And I hears him say, twice, before
lie’s too far away, ‘Blime!’ as if what she
said was sticking in ’is nut.
“Smiler don’t ’ave much religion, but he
reckoned a parson of some sort was wanted,
an’ he brings along Father Jolly—you might
know him; useter swagger erlong the pave-
ment, noddin’ an’ smilin’ at everybody, an’
one of the whitest coves ever wore a black
coat. ‘Give ’er a chance, Father,’ ’e says.
‘Fix ’er up as well as you can’; and Father
•Tolly comes erlong, sees what Smiler’s doin’
an' says he’s the good Sir Mari ton.
“Father Jolly has a ivory stick, and one
day after he misses it he corners Smiler.
‘ ’Ad ter do it, Father,’ says Smiler. ‘She
wants things, an’ I ain’t got no money.
’Ere’s the ticket.’
“Last was Fatty Thomson —the plain-
clothes —as arsks me one day where me mate
Smiler is, an’ if ’e doesn’t go to a ’ouse in
Goodwell-street —the ’ouse wTiere Eva is—
every day. Then I knew Smiler’s game was
up, an’ I waits to tell ’im and warn ’im off.
“ ‘ ’Twas bound ter come,’ says Smiler,
quite cheerful. ‘Anyway, I ’ave ter go.
Eva’s dying.’ And ’e goes.”
Scrooger went to the window.
“They arrested him?” I said.
“I went to make the d.’s wise to the case
when they came —which they did soon
after he went in. I’ll say this fer Fatty
Thomson, he waited there outside the room
till it was all over.
“An’ afterwards when they’ve taken him
away and Eva is gone, Father Jolly
comes out, an’ says: ‘Whatever ’e done.’ ’e
says. * ’e’s the good Sir Mariton,’ ’e says.”
We had a last drink.
“ ’Go d’yer think is findin’ the cash I’m
payin’ for the defence?” he asked, and
blinked his eyes in a way that suggested he
felt something besides cheerfulness.
HAMER.
Funeral Exercise.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE Awkward Squad has fallen in two-
deep and is “standing-easy,” awaiting
the arrival of the instructor. The
recruits have been going through a course of
exercises—“motions of the rifle on the
march,” “piling arms,” “sword-bayonet-
fighting” and such like Greek-to-the-l^yman.
The instructor, having received certain
routine injunctions from the apple-cheeked
officer-of-the-day on the quarter deck, salutes,
turns on his heel and marches for’ard.
Mr. William Corking (such is his tally),
C.P.O. in His Majesty’s Royal Navy, is a
gentleman of proportions whose massive
shoulders severely tax the seams of his
sleeves. His chest, which has slipped in a
southerly direction with “new navy” and
increasing years, causes undue strain to the
lower buttons of his coat. He wears a
“torpedo” beard, and with his cap cocked at
a rakish angle over his starboard eye, he
stands as a worthy representative of the
Bulldog Breed.
Several times he paces to and fro in front
of the squad with his hands clasped behind
his back, as though in deep thought. Then
he expectorates skilfully clean over the rail,
clears his throat, and, still walking, mumbles
something sounding like “Proplatease.” The
squad square themselves up painfully. Sud-
denly he turns on his heel toward the ranks
and halts abruptly. Then he slowly inflates
his lung£ and bursts out: “SQUAD!” —(in
an undertone: “Let’s-see-yer-move-as-one-
raan-n0w.”)... .“SHUN” !! A foghorn is
a poor, sickly murmur compared to that
syllable.
The party springs smartly to attention—
“ ’eads and eyes erect, ’eels together”—picks
up its dressing and numbers. This being
performed to the instructor’s satisfaction, it
pleases him to make a few criticisms.
“ ’ltch up that there belt there, No. 6,”
he says; and adds sarcastically, “ ’tain’t
meant fer a ’ip redoocer fakemajig, yer
know!” He glances along the ranks. “And
you there, No. 13,” he remarks, “stow that
there ‘Patric’ Campbell fixin’ away under
yer ’at. None of yer fancy kiss-me-Charlie
curls ’ere, yer know !”
Having passed sundry playful, perhaps
crushing, remarks, Mr. Corking continues
his even tread in front of the squad, sunk
again, apparently, in deep thought. A piece
of oily waste lies on the deck; he kicks it
deftly into the waterway, turns smartly
about —bingy distended, buttons straining
heavily—and shouts out impressively:
“FUNERAL—HEXERCISE !” Then in
the same breath: “As-yer-were.” (Pause)
“FUNEREAL HESCOURT cor-
rect mistake—carry—on.”
Mr. Corking then proceeds upon the detail
of the drill which is not devoid of a peculiar
eloquence.
“Now then,” he commences, “the requi-
sight number forming the hescourt is fell in
in two ranks at ‘the order.’ The orficer
in command of the party is selected upon
accordin’ to the rating of the diseased!!
.... Should the diseased be a hadmiral,” he
explains, “then a capting takes charge; if
a capting, then a lootenant, and so on and
so forth. If, ’owever,” adds the instructor
with a fine sense of justice and proportion,
“the diseased ’appens ter be just a wily
muckko, a midshipman or a chief petty
orficer is suffishunt I”
Mr. Corking resumes his measured beat
with hands behind his back. He glances
interestedly over the rail at a tug endeavor-
ing to manoeuvre a sailing vessel through the
dock gate. The exchange of lurid repartee
between the tug skipper and the windbag
mate produces a bland smile. He wheels
about smartly.
“HESCOURT! Now wot the ’ell are
yer movin’ off fer then, you, No. 3?....N0.
10?.... Wotsup, Fat’eads?” remarks the
instructor with some annoyance.... “That
there’s only the ‘caution’... .Asyewere.”
He starts afresh: “HESCOURT
Front rank four paces—forward....
MARCH!.— Now—then —come —along—
front—rank... .smack —it —about Let’s
see something,” he continues encouragingly.
“Front Rank... .About turn.”
Mr. Corking now “carries on” between
the ranks and proceeds as follows: “The
ranks ’avin’ been formed up facing heach-
other, the next order will be to present
arms.”
“Now then,” he says in rousing tones,
“HESCOURT PRE-SENT ARMS....
Ccme-along-there-No. 30—No. 35”—there is
some exasperation in his voice—“ Galoots!
....I’ll be callin’ of yer by name yet, an’
don’t yer forget it.”
To be called by name is a fate which
should never befall a “muckko” at drill.
“Can’t yer ‘pre-sent’ no better than that,
eh?” he asks. ...“Some of yous coves would
be more handier with a ’ammer breakin’ up
rocks, I reckons,” he adds with fine sarcasm.
After a pause more eloquent than abuse
the Instructor continues.' “Now, then,” he
says, “at the word.... ‘The-corpse-is-about-
to-come-up-the-’atchway,’ -the Hescourt will
Rest-on-yer-arms-reversed, which is like this
’ere.” Mr. Corking describes the manipula-
tion with an outer files rifle “In this
manner,” he says with emphasis. “Muzzle of
the rifle restin’ on the toe of the left futt,
right ’and placed open over the butt —like
this ’ere,” he demonstrates—“and covered
with the left ’and. That’s all about it, then
—-see?” He concludes finely with, “The ’hole
will then ’ang their ’eads upon their chests,
and, if possible, will assoom a mournful ex-
pression !”
“Now, then,” he says in even tones. “The-
corpse-is-about-to-come-up-the-’atchway
HESCOURT,” he orders in stentorian voice,
“REST- ON-YER-ARMS-REYERSED !”
and in a stage whisper—“muzzle-on-left-futt
there, No. 8....1eft ’and over right, No. 13.
WELL.”
The Instructor then marches gravely
towards one end of the squad, turns about
suddenly and placidly remarks: “Fer drill
purposes, I shall be the corpse!”
Mr. Corking passes majestically between
the ranks —a peculiarly observant corpse.
“ ’Ang yer ’ead on yer chest there, No.
12,” he says in passing... .“And No. 15
assoom a mournful expresshun, there!”
Finally, he imparts the grievous information
that “if possible the ’hole may shed a silent
tear.”
The imaginary cortege having passed
along, the Instructor lifts up his voice again.
“The corpse, mourning-party and hescourt
’avin’ landed on the jetty, causeway or pier-
’ead, the hescourt will ‘form fours,’ with the
arms at the ‘Reverse’ in this manner.”
He again illustrates how the rifle must be
carried so as to “ ’ave the muzzle pointing
down under the left arm, sling hnppermost,
guard as ’igh as the chin, right ’and carried
away to the rear so as to seize the rifle with
it be’ind the body.” He winds up gloomily
by remarking that “the ’hole will then march
off at the slow to the toon of the ‘Dead
Marchin’ Souls!’ ”
For no apparent reason, at this interesting
juncture the escort is peremptorily “stood at
ease.” Mr. Corking ceases his eloquent flow,
struts aft, and has a chinwag with the Q.M.
at the gangway. Incidentally, the “stand
easy” affords him an opportunity to duck
behind the engine-ro®m casing to inspect the
quarter-deck clock. As there is still a few
minutes to go before “pack up,” he assumes
a nonchalant air and returns to duty.
“HESCOURT!” he shouts (sotto voce. ...
smartly-together”) “ ’SHUN!”
The class springs to attention as one man.
and the Instructor continues: “The funeral
cortajay, ’avin’ arrived at the ‘graveyard, cem-
etery, or burying-ground, the party will take
up its position in two ranks on one side of
the ’ole; while the chapling carries on with
the burial routine. A few ’andfuls of gravel,
dust, or wotnot ’avin’ been chucked upon the
coffing,” concludes the Instructor hurriedly,
as time is limited, “the orficer in charge will
order—‘VOLLEYS.’ The rifles will be raised
into the air at the angle of 45 degrees,” he
adds with mathematical precision, “and at
the word of command, as a larst respec’, to
the diseased, three volleys will be fired simul-
taneous-like across the graveside Raise
ver rifles ’igh enough,” he warns the escort,
so- as - not-to-’it-the-orficer-in-the-back-of-the-
neck - with-a-plug-of-guncotton-’cos-its-unplea-
sant!”
The final instruction from Mr. Corking is
that “the ceremony ’avin’ been successfully
carried out, the ’hole will re-form fours and
march off at the quick to the joyful toon of
the band, which,” he adds airily, “is most
always ‘The Gurl I Left Be’ind Me,’ or some
such T-Tiddly-I-Ti’ refrain! The reason for
tins ere lively bust up,” he explains breezily,
being for ter ’elp ter cheer up the spirits of
the diseased pals.”
Five bells strikes on the quarter-deck.
Smoke-o!”
